Denial with Hili Support in Conditional Syllogism
Example of denial with essential support in a conditional syllogism about zakat on trade goods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the hili support in a conditional syllogism and reconstruct the implicit condition it exposes.

Core Idea
The hili support reveals the hidden condition that makes a premise true, turning a seemingly valid syllogism into an overgeneralization.

In the previous lesson, you learned about denial with support in unconditional syllogisms. Now we turn to conditional syllogisms, where the proponent uses a conditional argument to prove a claim about zakat on trade goods. The questioner responds with an essential (hili) support that uncovers a missing condition. This lesson shows how the hili support operates in a conditional context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claim and proof
The proponent asserts that trade goods (al-'urud) have no zakat obligation. He then constructs a conditional syllogism to support this: 'If they were trade goods, then zakat would not be obligatory; but they are trade goods; therefore zakat is not obligatory.'
2
Extract the conditional form
The syllogism is a conditional (istithna'i muttasil) of modus ponens form: If P (trade goods), then Q (no zakat); P; therefore Q. Note the major premise is a conditional statement.
3
Locate the questioner's response
The questioner (sa'il) responds with 'محل ذلك لو كانت لغير التجارة إلى آخره' meaning 'the place for that is if they were not for trade, etc.' This is the hili support: it identifies a condition that the proponent's premise implicitly assumes.
4
Recognize the implicit condition
The hili support reveals that the proponent's conditional premise is only true when the trade goods are not for trade (i.e., personal use). For actual trade goods ('urud al-tijara), zakat is obligatory. Thus the proponent's premise is not universally true, and his argument fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال ذلك في السند الحلي أن يقول صاحب التصديق : العروض لا تجب فيها الزكاة، ثم يقيم على ذلك دليلاً استثنائيًا فيقول : لو كانت عروضًا ما وجبت فيها الزكاة لكنها عروض، ينتج : لم تجب فيها الزكاة .
فيجيب السائل : محل ذلك لو كانت لغير التجارة إلى آخره .
And an example of that in the essential (hili) support: The proponent of the affirmation says: Trade goods (al-'urud) do not have zakat obligatory on them. Then he establishes a conditional proof for that: If they were trade goods, zakat would not be obligatory on them; but they are trade goods; therefore: zakat is not obligatory on them.
Then the questioner answers: The place for that is if they were not for trade, etc.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage gives a third example of denial with support in a conditional syllogism, this time using the 'hili' (essential) support. The proponent claims that 'trade goods (al-'urud) do not have zakat obligatory on them.' He reasons conditionally: 'If they were trade goods, then zakat would not be obligatory; but they are trade goods; so zakat is not obligatory.' The questioner responds with a hili support, pointing out that the ruling applies only when the trade goods are not for trade? Actually, the text says 'the place for that is if they were not for trade, etc.' This means that the proponent's premise is only valid for goods not intended for trade (i.e., personal use), but the conditional does not specify that condition. The essential support identifies the missing condition that, once included, undermines the opponent's argument. This demonstrates the hili support's function: it gets at the essence of the issue by revealing an implicit qualification that, when made explicit, shows the opponent's premise is not universally true. The example is drawn from fiqh: zakat is not generally obligatory on personal effects, but on trade goods ('urud al-tijara) zakat is obligatory. The proponent's error is ignoring the distinction between trade goods and personal goods.

Key Terms
السند الحلي
العروض
محل ذلك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of denial with hili support regarding trade goods, what is the hidden condition the questioner reveals?
A) That the goods are perishable
B) That the goods are not for trade (i.e., personal use)
C) That the goods are owned by a Muslim
D) That the goods are of a certain value

Key Takeaway
The hili support in a conditional syllogism works by exposing an unstated condition that limits the premise, forcing the proponent to qualify his claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyse this argument: 'If a drink is wine, it is impure. This drink is wine. Therefore it is impure.' Use the hili support to identify a possible hidden condition (e.g., wine vs. vinegar after transformation).
?
In a debate about zakat on livestock, a proponent argues: 'If the herd is pastured, then zakat is not obligatory; the herd is pastured; therefore no zakat.' Find the hili support that reveals the condition under which the premise fails (e.g., if the herd is pastured and owned for less than a full year).

Three Types of Sanad for Man' in Conditional Syllogisms
Author gives three examples of man' in istithna'i syllogisms with different sanad types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the type of sanad (lami, qat'i, or hali) used in a conditional syllogism objection and explain how each targets a specific premise.

Core Idea
The sanad type determines how an objection is supported: causally, decisively, or circumstantially.

In dialectical debate (jadal), objecting to a premise is not enough; you must support your objection with a sanad. This passage gives three examples of man' in conditional syllogisms, each with a different sanad type. Understanding these types is essential for evaluating the strength of objections in theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism and the objection
For each example, locate the conditional syllogism (istithna'i) and identify which premise the objector prevents (man'). Note whether the objector says 'I prevent the major' (sanad lami) or 'I prevent the minor' (sanad qat'i) or gives a circumstantial qualification (sanad hali).
The major is the conditional statement; the minor is the exception (istithna').
2
Determine the sanad type
Check the support given: if the objector provides alternative possibilities (e.g., 'why not a horse or mule?') it is lami (causal); if they assert an undeniable fact (e.g., 'it is speaking') it is qat'i (decisive); if they add conditions (e.g., 'if for trade') it is hali (circumstantial).
The sanad type determines the mode of objection.
3
Explain why the sanad is appropriate
Consider the logical gap in the targeted premise. For lami, the conditional's necessity is challenged; for qat'i, the exception's truth is denied; for hali, the scope of the premise is limited. Each sanad addresses a specific weak point.
4
Compare the three types
Contrast how each sanad attacks the argument: lami offers alternatives, qat'i presents a contradictory fact, hali adds a condition. This helps in choosing the right objection in debate.
All are valid forms of man' in jadal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال المنع بالسند اللمي في الاستثنائي أن يقول المعلل صاحب التصديق : ذلك الشبح إنسان، ثم يقيم على دعواه دليلاً استثنائيا فيقول : لو كان ذلك الشبح حيوانًا لكان إنسانًا ، لكنه حيوان، ينتج فهو إنسان . فيقول السائل : أمنعُ كبرى دليلك هذا، وهي الشرطية، أعني قولك : لو كان هذا حيوانًا لكان إنسانًا ويقرن منعه إياها بالسند اللمي فيقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون حيوانًا فرسًا، ولم لا يجوز أن يكون حيوانا بغلا، وهكذا. ومثال المنع بالسند القطعي في الاستثنائي أن يقول المعلل صاحب التصديق : ذلك الشبح ليس بإنسان، ثم يقيم على دعواه دليلاً استثنائيًا فيقول لو كان ذلك الشبح إنسانًا لكان حيوانا، لكنه غيرُ حيوان ينتج فهو غير إنسان . فيقول السائل : أمنعُ صُغرى دليلك هذا، وهي الاستثنائية، أعني قولك : لكنه غيرُ حيوان، كيف يكون غير حيوان والحال أنه ناطق . ومثال ذلك في السند الحلي أن يقول صاحب التصديق : العروض لا تجب فيها الزكاة، ثم يقيم على ذلك دليلاً استثنائياً فيقول : لو كانت عروضًا ما وجبت فيها الزكاة لكنها عروض، ينتج : لم تجب فيها الزكاة . فيجيب السائل : محل ذلك لو كانت لغير التجارة إلى آخره .
Example of prevention with the causal support in the conditional [syllogism]: The rationalizer, the possessor of assent, says: 'That phantom is a human,' then he establishes a conditional proof for his claim, saying: 'If that phantom were an animal, it would be a human, but it is an animal, therefore it is a human.' The questioner says: 'I prevent the major of your proof, which is the conditional, namely your statement: If this were an animal, it would be a human, and he pairs his prevention with the causal support, saying: Why is it not possible that it be an animal that is a horse, or an animal that is a mule, and so on.' Example of prevention with the decisive support in the conditional: The rationalizer, the possessor of assent, says: 'That phantom is not a human,' then he establishes a conditional proof for his claim, saying: 'If that phantom were a human, it would be an animal, but it is not an animal, therefore it is not a human.' The questioner says: 'I prevent the minor of your proof, which is the exception, namely your statement: But it is not an animal. How can it be not an animal while it is speaking?' Example of that with the circumstantial support: The possessor of assent says: 'Commodities do not require zakat,' then he establishes a conditional proof for that, saying: 'If they were commodities, zakat would not be obligatory on them, but they are commodities, therefore zakat is not obligatory on them.' The questioner responds: 'Its place is if they were for other than trade, etc.'
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage provides three examples of man' (objection) in istithna'i (conditional) syllogisms, each with a different type of sanad (support). The first example uses sanad lami (causal support): the objector prevents the major premise (the conditional) by asking 'Why is it not possible that it be an animal that is a horse, or a mule?' This challenges the necessity of the conditional by offering alternatives, thus giving a reason (lam) for the prevention. The second example uses sanad qat'i (decisive support): the objector prevents the minor premise (the exception 'but it is not an animal') by asserting a decisive fact: the figure is speaking (natiq), which implies it is human, contradicting the premise. The third example uses sanad hali (circumstantial support): the objector qualifies the premise by specifying the circumstances—'if they were for other than trade'—showing that the ruling of zakat changes based on intention. These examples illustrate the practical application of sanad types in jadal (dialectical debate), where each type targets a specific premise and uses a different mode of objection.

Key Terms
المنع
السند اللمي
السند القطعي
السند الحلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example of man' with sanad lami, which premise is being objected to?
A) The minor premise (صغرى)
B) The major premise (كبرى)
C) The conclusion (نتيجة)
D) The conditional as a whole

Key Takeaway
In conditional syllogisms, man' with a sanad targets a specific premise; the sanad type (lami, qat'i, hali) reflects the nature of the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the sanad type in the following objection: A mutakallim argues 'If the world were eternal, it would be uncreated; but it is created, so it is not eternal.' The objector says 'I prevent the major: why is it not possible that the world be eternal and yet created by God's will?'
?
For each of the three examples in the passage, rewrite the objector's sanad in your own words and state which premise it targets.

The First Division of Sanad: When the Evidence is the Contradictory of the Claim
Author classifies six divisions of sanad and details the first division where sanad equals the contradictory of the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first division of sanad in a disputation and explain how it functions using the contradictory relationship.

Core Idea
The most powerful objection is one where the evidence is exactly the contradictory of the claim.

In disputation, not all evidence (sanad) is equally useful. The author classifies six types, but only three are truly relevant. The first and most direct is when the questioner's evidence is itself the exact contradictory of the claim being denied. This lesson unpacks that classification and its logical underpinning.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the six divisions
The passage divides sanad into six categories. Identify which three are useful for the questioner and which are not. This sets the stage for the first division.
Three divisions are beneficial to the questioner; one only helps the responder; two are useless.
2
Locate the first division
Find where the author says: 'الأول من الأقسام الستة المذكورة هو مايكون فيه السند نفس نقيض الدعوى الممنوعة'. Note that the sanad (evidence) is exactly the contradictory (naqid) of the contested claim.
The Arabic term 'عين' emphasizes identity, not mere equivalence.
3
Analyze the example
The responder argues 'This is human' using 'it is rational, and every rational is human.' The questioner denies the minor premise 'it is rational' and offers sanad: 'Why may it not be non-rational?' Here 'non-rational' is the contradictory of 'rational'.
The sanad is a possibility (jawaz), not an assertion, but it still constitutes the contradictory.
4
Understand the logical basis
The author references the disjunctive syllogism (qiyas sharti munfasil) of the exclusive type. Establishing the contradictory (non-rational) would negate the original (rational), which in turn undermines the responder's proof.
This is based on the principle that establishing a proposition's contradictory negates that proposition.
5
Apply the pattern
Recognize this division in other dialectical exchanges: whenever the questioner's evidence is the exact contradictory of the claim being denied, it is the first division. Practice identifying the contradictory in a given exchange.
The pattern generalizes beyond syllogisms with explicit minor premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أولاً أن ثلاثة من هذه الأقسام الستة هي محل المناظرة المقصود؛ لأن السائل ينفعه الإتيان بها في إبطال دعوى المعلل والمعلل ينفعه الاشتغال بالرد عليها؛ لأن إبطالها سبب لاستقامة دليله، وواحد منها لا ينفع السائل إتيانه، ولكن ينفع المعلل إبطاله، واثنان منها لا فائدة فيهما للمعلل ولا للسائل فلا حاجة للإتيان بهما أصلاً، ولكنه قد يأتي بهما يظن فيهما فائدة والأمر بخلاف ما يظن. وإذا علمت ذلك فالأول من الأقسام الستة المذكورة هو مايكون فيه السند نفس نقيض الدعوى الممنوعة. ومثاله أن يقول المعلل : هذا إنسان، ثم يستدل على هذا التصديق فيقول : لأنه ناطق، وكل ناطق إنسان، ينتج : هذا إنسان. فيقول السائل : أمنع صغرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك : لأنه ناطق ويجعل هذا المنع مقرونا بالسند اللمي فيقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون غير ناطق، فالصغرى الممنوعة : هو ناطق والسند جواز كونه غير ناطق، وغير الناطقِ هو عين نقيض الناطق. وهذا القسم من الأقسام الثلاثة التي ينفع السائل الإتيان بها، وينفع المعلل إبطالها؛ لما قدمنا من أن إثبات النقيض يلزمه نفي نقيضه، كعكسه، كما أوضحنا في القياس الشرطي المنفصل، المركب من حقيقية مانعة جمع وخلو.
And know first that three of these six divisions are the locus of the intended disputation; because the questioner benefits from bringing them in refuting the claim of the responder, and the responder benefits from engaging in refuting them; because their refutation is a cause for the soundness of his proof. One of them does not benefit the questioner to bring, but benefits the responder to refute it. Two of them have no benefit for the responder nor for the questioner, so there is no need to bring them at all, but one may bring them thinking there is benefit in them, but the matter is contrary to what is thought. And if you know that, the first of the six mentioned divisions is that in which the evidence is exactly the contradictory of the contested claim. Its example: the responder says 'This is a human,' then proves this assent by saying 'Because it is rational, and every rational is human,' concluding 'This is a human.' The questioner says: 'I deny the minor premise of your proof, which is your statement 'because it is rational,' and he makes this denial accompanied by the causal evidence by saying: 'Why may it not be non-rational?' So the denied minor premise is 'it is rational,' and the evidence is the possibility of it being non-rational, and 'non-rational' is exactly the contradictory of 'rational.' This division is among the three divisions that benefit the questioner to bring, and benefit the responder to refute; because, as we have stated, establishing the contradictory necessitates the negation of its contradictory, and vice versa, as we clarified in the disjunctive conditional syllogism composed of the true exclusive type.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage begins by categorizing the six divisions of evidence (sanad) used in disputation. It states that three of these six are the primary focus of the intended discussion. These three are beneficial to the questioner (al-sa'il) in that he can use them to refute the claim of the responder (al-mu'allil), and likewise beneficial to the responder to refute them. One division benefits only the responder to refute, not the questioner. Two divisions have no benefit to either party, though they may be mistakenly used. The author then details the first division: the evidence is exactly the contradictory (naqid) of the contested claim. He gives an example: The responder says 'This is a human' and proves it by 'because it is rational, and every rational is human.' The questioner denies the minor premise 'it is rational' and supports this denial with causal evidence: 'Why may it not be non-rational?' The evidence (the possibility of non-rationality) is the very contradictory of the denied premise. This division benefits the questioner because establishing the contradictory (non-rational) would negate the claim. The responder benefits from refuting it, as explained via the disjunctive conditional syllogism (qiyas sharti munfasil) of the exclusive true type.

Key Terms
السند
النقيض
القياس الشرطي المنفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first division of sanad, what is the relationship between the sanad and the contradictory (naqid) of the contested claim?
A) The sanad is exactly the contradictory of the claim.
B) The sanad is equivalent to the contradictory, not identical.
C) The sanad is the opposite of the contradictory.
D) The sanad is a part of the contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The first division of sanad occurs when the evidence is the exact contradictory of the contested claim; it is a powerful tool for the questioner because establishing the contradictory negates the claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a disputation about the temporality of the world, the claimant says 'The world is temporally originated because it has change.' The opponent denies the minor premise 'it has change' and gives sanad: 'Why cannot it be without change?' Is this an instance of the first division? Explain why or why not.
?
Construct your own dialectical exchange on a fiqh ruling (e.g., wine is haram) and show an example of the first division where the sanad is the contradictory of the contested claim.

Second Division: Evidence Equivalent to the Contradictory
Author explains the second division where the sanad is equivalent to the contradictory of the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a sanad is musawi al-naqid and explain why establishing it refutes the claim.

Core Idea
Evidence equivalent to a claim's contradictory has the same refuting power as the contradictory itself.

In munazara, the questioner can object by bringing evidence that is not exactly the contradictory of the claim but is logically equivalent to it. This move, known as musawi al-naqid, relies on the principle that equivalent propositions share truth values. The author illustrates with a mathematical example and links it to the disjunctive conditional syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested claim and denied premise
Read the example: The responder claims 'These dinars are even' and proves it using the minor premise 'they are divisible into two equal parts'. The questioner denies this minor premise. Note what exactly is denied.
The denied premise is 'the dinars are divisible into equal parts'.
2
Find the evidence brought by the questioner
The questioner asserts 'they are odd' as evidence. Check whether 'odd' is exactly the contradictory of the denied premise or merely equivalent.
The contradictory of 'divisible into equal parts' is 'not divisible into equal parts'. The text says 'odd' is equivalent to that contradictory.
3
Verify the equivalence
Confirm that 'odd' implies 'not divisible into equal parts' and vice versa. This is a conceptual truth about numbers, which the author uses as an example of musawi al-naqid.
In Islamic sciences, analogical equivalences are often used, e.g., 'unjust' is equivalent to 'not just' in rulings.
4
Understand the logical force
The author explains that establishing the equivalent (odd) proves the negation of the original denied premise, just as establishing the exact contradictory would. This is because the equivalent shares the same truth value as the contradictory.
This principle is derived from the disjunctive conditional syllogism, where the equivalent of a contradictory has the same effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني منها : هو ما يكون فيه السند مساويًا لنقيض الدعوى الممنوعة. ومثاله أن يقول المعلل : هذه الدنانير زوج، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنها منقسمة إلى قسمين متساويين، وكل منقسم إلى متساويين فهو زوج ينتج من الشكل الأول : هي زوج. فيقول السائل : أمنع صغرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك: إنها منقسمة إلى متساويين، والحال أنها فرد فالصغرى الممنوعة هي كونها منقسمة إلى متساويين، والسند الذي هو (هي فرد) مساو لنقيض كونها منقسمة إلى متساويين؛ لأن نقيضه (هي ليست منقسمة إلى متساويين والفرد مساءٍ لهذا النقيض؛ لأن كل مالم ينقسم إلى مستاويين فهو فرد کعکسه. وهذا القسم أيضًا ينفع السائل الإتيان به، وينفع المعلل إبطاله؛ لما أوضحناه في القياس الشرطي المنفصل المركب من الحقيقية المانعة للجمع والخلو معًا والاستثنائية؛ لأنا قدمنا أنها لا تتركب إلا من النقيضين، أو من قضية ومساوي نقيضها ومساوي النقيض حكمه كحكم النقيض، فثبوت مساوي النقيض يدل على نفي نقيضه، کعکسه، كما تقدم إيضاحه في القياس الشرطي المنفصل. فإذا أثبت المانع مساوي نقيض الدعوى الممنوعة فقد أثبت بطلانها قطعا؛ لأن إثبات مساوي النقيض يدل على انتفاء نقيضه.
The second division: it is that in which the evidence is equivalent to the contradictory of the contested claim. Its example: The responder says 'These dinars are even,' then establishes proof for that by saying 'Because they are divisible into two equal parts, and everything divisible into equal parts is even,' concluding from the first figure: 'They are even.' The questioner says: 'I deny the minor premise of your proof, which is your statement that they are divisible into equal parts, whereas they are odd.' So the denied minor premise is their being divisible into equal parts, and the evidence which is 'they are odd' is equivalent to the contradictory of their being divisible into equal parts; because its contradictory is 'they are not divisible into equal parts' and the odd is equivalent to this contradictory; because everything that is not divisible into equal parts is odd and vice versa. This division also benefits the questioner to bring, and benefits the responder to refute it; because we have clarified in the disjunctive conditional syllogism composed of the true type that prevents both conjunction and disjunction together with the exclusionary, because we have stated that it is only composed of two contradictories, or of a proposition and the equivalent of its contradictory, and the equivalent of the contradictory has the same ruling as the contradictory. So establishing the equivalent of the contradictory indicates the negation of its contradictory, and vice versa, as we have already clarified in the disjunctive conditional syllogism. So if the objector establishes the equivalent of the contradictory of the contested claim, he has established its invalidity decisively, because establishing the equivalent of the contradictory indicates the negation of its contradictory.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage explains the second division of the beneficial sanad types. Here, the evidence is not exactly the contradictory of the claim but is equivalent to it (musawi li-naqid). The example: The responder claims 'These dinars are even' and proves it by 'They are divisible into two equal parts, and everything divisible into equal parts is even.' The questioner denies the minor premise 'divisible into equal parts' by asserting 'they are odd.' The evidence 'odd' is equivalent to the contradictory of the denied premise, because the contradictory is 'not divisible into equal parts' and oddness is coextensive with that (every odd number is not so divisible, and vice versa). The author argues that this division also benefits both parties, referencing the disjunctive conditional syllogism where the equivalent of a contradictory has the same logical force as the contradictory itself. Establishing the equivalent necessarily negates the original claim, just as establishing the contradictory would. Thus, if the objector proves that the evidence (which is equivalent to the contradictory) holds, the claim is refuted with certainty.

Key Terms
مساوي النقيض (musawi al-naqid): A proposition equivalent to the contradictory of the claim, meaning they are logically coextensive and interchangeable in terms of truth value. [Note: This term is specific to the discussion of sanad in munazara; in general logic it refers to logical equivalence but here it has a particular role in objection.]
المدعى الممنوعة (al-da'wa al-mamnu'a): The claim that is contested or denied by the questioner; the object of the objection. [Note: No significant shift; standard in munazara.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second division, why does establishing the evidence 'odd' refute the claim that the dinars are even?
A) Because 'odd' is the exact contradictory of 'even'.
B) Because 'odd' is equivalent to the contradictory of the minor premise 'divisible into equal parts'.
C) Because the claim 'even' is false by definition.
D) Because the questioner has the burden of proof.

Key Takeaway
In munazara, evidence equivalent to the contradictory of a disputed premise has the same refuting force as the exact contradictory.

The questioner denies the minor premise 'it is commanded in the Quran' and brings evidence that the act is explicitly exempted (e.g., a hadith). Third Section: Sanad More Specific than Negation
Author explains the third section of objection where sanad is more specific than negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a sanad is more specific than the negation of the objected claim and explain why proving the sanad necessitates proving the negation.

Core Idea
If the sanad is a subset of the negation, proving the sanad proves the negation.

In dialectical debate, the questioner can object to a premise by providing a sanad (evidence) that is more specific than the negation of the claim being objected to. This third section of manع is particularly powerful because establishing the more specific automatically establishes the more general negation, thereby invalidating the original claim. In this lesson, we will dissect the logical structure behind this move and see it applied in a concrete debate example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objected claim and its negation
Find the premise that the questioner is denying (e.g., 'That apparition is a human'). Its logical negation is 'It is not a human'.
2
Find the sanad provided by the questioner
The sanad is the evidence supporting the objection. In the example, the questioner says 'It might be a horse', so the sanad is 'being a horse'.
3
Compare the extension of the sanad to the extension of the negation
Determine if every instance of the sanad is also an instance of the negation. Here, every horse is a non-human, but not every non-human is a horse. So the sanad is a proper subset of the negation.
4
Determine if the sanad is absolutely more specific
If the sanad's extension is wholly contained within the negation's extension and the negation has additional members, then the sanad is akhas mutlaqan (absolutely more specific).
5
Verify that proving the sanad proves the negation
If the sanad is established (e.g., it is proven that the apparition is a horse), then the negation (it is not a human) follows necessarily because the specific entails the general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث منها : هو ما يكون السند فيه أخص مطلقا من نقيض الدعوى الممنوعة . ومثاله أن يقول : ذلك الشيخ ضاحك ، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه إنسان وكل إنسان ضاحك ينتج من الشكل الأول : هو ـ أي الشبح المذكور - ضاحك . فيقول السائل : أمنعُ صُغرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك : إن ذلك الشبح إنسان ثم يجعل هذا المنع مقرونا بالسند اللمي فيقول: لم [؟] لا يجوز أن يكون فرسًا، فالمقدمة الممنوعة كون ذلك الشبح إنسانًا، ونقيضها ليس هو بإنسان، والسند هنا هو جواز كونه فرسا، وكونه فرسًا أخص من نقيض كونه إنسانًا ؛ لأن نقيضه غير إنسان، وغير الإنسان صادق بالفرس وغيره، فنقيض الدعوى الممنوعة أعم مطلقا والسند أخص منه مطلقا كما ترى . وهذا القسم الثالث أيضًا ينفع السائل الإتيان به؛ لأن إثبات ماهو أخص من نقيض الدعوى يستلزم إثبات نقيض الدعوى ضرورة؛ لأن ثبوت الأخص يستلزم ثبوت الأعم، فإذا أثبت السائل ماهو أخص من نقيض الدعوى لزم ثبوت نقيض الدعوى، وإذا لزم ثبوت نقيضها تحقق انتفاؤها ؛ لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين. وقد أوضحنا أن ثبوت ماهو أخص من النقيض يستلزم ثبوت النقيض، وثبوت النقيض يستلزم انتفاء نقيضه الآخر، في الكلام على القياس المركب من مانعة الجمع المجوّزة للخلو ؛ لأنها لا تتركب إلا من قضية وأخص من نقيضها، وثبوت كل واحد من طرفيها يقتضي نفي الآخر؛ لما أوضحنا من أن ثبوت الأخص من النقيض يستلزم ثبوت النقيض، إلى آخره .
The third section of them: it is that in which the sanad is absolutely more specific than the negation of the objected claim. Its example is that he (the mu'allil) says: 'That old man is laughing,' then he establishes the proof for that by saying: 'Because he is a human, and every human is laughing,' producing from the first figure: 'He – i.e., that aforementioned apparition – is laughing.' Then the questioner says: 'I deny the minor premise of this proof of yours, which is your statement: "That apparition is a human."' Then he couples this denial with the 'illī sanad and says: 'Why is it not permissible that it be a horse?' So the denied premise is that that apparition is a human, and its negation is 'it is not a human', and the sanad here is the possibility of it being a horse, and its being a horse is more specific than the negation of its being a human, because its negation is 'non-human', and 'non-human' is true of a horse and other than it, so the negation of the objected claim is absolutely more general and the sanad is absolutely more specific than it, as you see. And this third section also benefits the questioner in bringing it, because establishing what is more specific than the negation of the claim necessitates establishing the negation of the claim necessarily, because the establishment of the more specific necessitates the establishment of the more general. So if the questioner establishes what is more specific than the negation of the claim, the establishment of the negation of the claim follows, and if the establishment of its negation follows, its invalidity is realized, due to the impossibility of the two contradictories coexisting. And we have clarified that the establishment of what is more specific than the negation necessitates the establishment of the negation, and the establishment of the negation necessitates the invalidity of its other contradictory, in the discussion on the compound syllogism composed of the disjunction with 'man'at al-jam' that permits emptiness, because it is composed only of a proposition and what is more specific than its negation, and the establishment of each of its two sides necessitates the negation of the other, due to what we clarified: that the establishment of the more specific than the negation necessitates the establishment of the negation, etc.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the third type of objection (manع) in dialectical reasoning. The author defines it as the case where the evidence (sanad) provided by the questioner is absolutely more specific (akhas mutlaqan) than the negation of the claim being objected to. He illustrates with an example: the responder claims a certain apparition is laughing, proving it with a categorical syllogism in the first figure: all humans laugh, that apparition is human, therefore it laughs. The questioner objects to the minor premise, denying that the apparition is human, and supports his denial with a sanad: 'Why can it not be a horse?' The author explains that the denied premise is 'it is human', its negation is 'it is not human', and the sanad 'it is a horse' is more specific than 'not human' because 'not human' applies to horses and many other things. Hence the negation is more general and the sanad is more specific. The benefit for the questioner is that by proving the more specific (e.g., that it is a horse), he necessarily proves the more general negation (that it is not human), which in turn invalidates the original claim because the two contradictories cannot coexist. The author then connects this to a previous discussion on compound syllogisms with exclusive disjunction, reinforcing the logical principle.

In dialectical objection, if the sanad (evidence) is absolutely more specific than the negation of the claim, establishing the sanad necessarily establishes the negation, thereby refuting the original claim.

Fourth Section: Sanad More General than Negation
Author explains the fourth section of objection where sanad is more general than negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a sanad is absolutely more general than the negation of the claim and determine whether the objection benefits the questioner or the responder.

Core Idea
Proving the general does not prove the specific, but denying the general does.

In dialectical debate, the questioner often supports a denial (manع) with a sanad, a piece of evidence. This section examines a specific case where the sanad is absolutely more general than the negation of the objected claim. By analyzing the logical relationship between the sanad, the denied premise, and its negation, we can determine whether such an objection advances the questioner's position or, instead, opens the door for the responder's counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its negation
Locate the original claim (the mu'allil's thesis) and the premise that is denied. In the example, the claim is 'This apparition is a stone,' and the denied premise is 'it is non-speaking.' The negation of that premise is 'it is speaking.'
2
Locate the sanad and compare generality
The questioner provides a sanad: 'it is an animal.' Compare the extension of the sanad (animal) with the extension of the negation (speaking). Determine if the sanad is absolutely more general (a'am mutlaqan) than the negation. Here, every speaking being is an animal, but not every animal is speaking, so animal is indeed more general.
The relation must be proper inclusion; if the terms are coextensive or unrelated, the rules differ.
3
Analyze the logical effect of proving the sanad
If the questioner proves the sanad (that it is an animal), does that prove the negation (that it is speaking)? No, because establishing a more general category does not entail establishing any specific subset. Many animals are not speaking. Therefore, this objection does not benefit the questioner.
This is a common pitfall: confusing 'implies' with 'is implied by'.
4
Consider the reverse: denying the sanad
If the mu'allil denies the sanad (proves it is not an animal), then the negation (speaking) also falls, because denying the more general (not animal) necessarily denies the more specific (not speaking). However, this counter-move is made by the responder, not the questioner.
The passage emphasizes that this section is not useful for the questioner but could be exploited by the responder.

The fourth section of them: it is that in which the sanad is more general than the negation of the objected claim. Its example is that the mu'allil says: 'This apparition is a stone,' then he establishes the proof for that by saying: 'Because it is non-speaking, and every non-speaking is a stone,' producing from the first figure: 'It – i.e., that apparition – is a stone.' Then the questioner says: 'I deny the minor premise of this proof of yours, which is your statement: "Because it is non-speaking."' Then he couples this denial with the categorical sanad and says: 'How can it be non-speaking when it is an animal?' So the denied premise is 'non-speaking', its negation is 'speaking', and the sanad is 'animal', and 'animal' is absolutely more general than 'speaking', which is the negation of the objected claim. And this fourth section does not benefit the questioner, because establishing the more general does not necessitate establishing the more specific. But if the mu'allil denies that sanad which is more general than the negation of the claim, then the invalidity of the negation of the claim follows, because the negation of the more general necessitates the negation of the more specific.

Commentary
This passage describes the fourth type of objection, where the sanad is more general (a'am) than the negation of the claim. The author gives an example: the responder claims a certain apparition is a stone, proving it with a syllogism: all non-speaking things are stones, that apparition is non-speaking, therefore it is a stone. The questioner denies the minor premise (that it is non-speaking) and provides a sanad: 'How can it be non-speaking when it is an animal?' Here, the denied premise is 'non-speaking', its negation is 'speaking', and the sanad 'animal' is more general than 'speaking' because every speaking being is an animal but not every animal is speaking. The author explains that in this case, the questioner cannot benefit from simply proving the sanad (animal), because establishing a more general term does not entail establishing a more specific one (speaking). However, if the responder (mu'allil) were to deny the sanad (i.e., prove that it is not an animal), then the negation of the claim (speaking) would also be invalidated because denying the more general (not animal) entails denying the more specific (not speaking) — but this is a different usage. The passage highlights that this section is not useful for the questioner's objection, but rather for the responder's counter-objection.

The Fourth Division: Sanad More General Than Negation
Author explains fourth division where sanad is more general than the negation of the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth division of dialectical prevention where the sanad is more general than the negation of the prevented claim, and evaluate whether the questioner benefits from offering or negating the sanad.

Core Idea
Proving the more general does not prove the more specific; only negating the more general helps.

In dialectical reasoning, the questioner sometimes provides a supporting premise (sanad) to block the reasoner's argument. This lesson examines the case where the sanad is more general than the negation of the claim being prevented. Understanding this division is crucial for knowing when the questioner's intervention is effective or ineffective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the prevented premise
Find the reasoner's original claim (e.g., 'this phantom is a stone') and the premise the questioner prevents (e.g., 'it is non-speaking'). The prevented premise is what the questioner objects to.
2
Determine the negation of the prevented premise
The negation is the logical contradictory of the prevented premise. In the example, 'non-speaking' is prevented; its negation is 'speaking'. This is what the questioner would need to prove to contradict the reasoner's premise.
3
Identify the sanad and compare its generality
The sanad is the premise the questioner offers to support the prevention. Here, the sanad is 'it is an animal'. Compare it to the negation: 'animal' is more general than 'speaking' (since all speaking things are animals, but not vice versa).
The author says 'absolutely more general' (aamm mutlaqan), meaning every instance of the negation is an instance of the sanad, but not the reverse.
4
Evaluate the benefit of the sanad
Proving the sanad (that it is an animal) does not prove the negation (that it speaks) because the more general does not entail the more specific. So the questioner gains nothing from proving the sanad. However, if the reasoner negates the sanad (proves it is not an animal), then the negation (speaking) is also negated, which forces the claim (it is a stone) to be true.
This is because negating the more general necessarily negates the more specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الرابع منها هو ما يكون السند فيه أعم من نقيض الدعوى الممنوعة.
ومثاله أن يقول المعلل : هذا الشبح حجر، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه غير ناطق وكل غير ناطق فهو حجر، ينتج من الشكل الأول : هو - أي ذلك الشبح - حجر .
فيقول السائل : أمنع صغرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك : لأنه غير ناطق، ثم يجعل هذا المنع مقرونا بالسند القطعي فيقول : كيف يكون غير ناطق والحال أنه حيوان؟ فالمقدمة الممنوعة هي (غير ناطق) ونقيضها (هو ناطق) والسند هو حيوان والحيوان الذي هو أعم مطلقا من الناطق الذي هو نقيض الدعوى الممنوعة .
وهذا القسم الرابع لا ينتفع به السائل ؛ لأن إثبات الأعم لا يستلزم إثبات الأخص، ولكن المعلل إذا نفى ذلك السند الذي هو أعم من نقيض الدعوى لزم انتفاء نقيض الدعوى؛ لأن نفي الأعم يستلزم نفي الأخص، وانتفاء نقيض الدعوى يلزمه صحتها؛ لأن النقيضين لا يرتفعان ولا يجتمعان وقد أوضحنا هذا غاية الإيضاح في مبحث الضربين المنتجين من القياس الشرطي المتصل (۱) .
The fourth division is that in which the sanad is more general than the negation of the prevented claim. An example: The reasoner says: 'This phantom is a stone,' then he establishes a proof for that by saying: 'Because it is non-speaking, and everything non-speaking is a stone,' which yields from the first figure: 'It – i.e. that phantom – is a stone.' The questioner says: 'I prevent the minor premise of this proof of yours, which is your statement: "because it is non-speaking,"' then he makes this prevention accompanied by the categorical sanad by saying: 'How can it be non-speaking when it is an animal?' So the prevented premise is 'non-speaking' and its negation is 'speaking', and the sanad is 'animal', and the animal is absolutely more general than 'speaking' which is the negation of the prevented claim. This fourth division is of no benefit to the questioner because proving the more general does not necessitate proving the more specific. But if the reasoner negates that sanad which is more general than the negation of the claim, then the negation of the claim follows necessarily, because negating the more general necessitates negating the more specific, and the negation of the claim necessitates its truth, because two contradictories cannot both be false nor both be true. We have clarified this with utmost clarity in the discussion of the two productive types of conditional syllogism.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage classifies the fourth division of a particular type of dialectical argument. The author begins by defining it: the sanad (the supporting premise) is more general than the negation of the claim that the questioner has prevented (mumna). He then gives an example. The reasoner claims 'this phantom is a stone' and argues: it is non-speaking, and every non-speaking is a stone (first figure syllogism). The questioner prevents the minor premise 'it is non-speaking' and attaches a categorical sanad: 'how can it be non-speaking when it is an animal?' The prevented premise is 'non-speaking'; its negation is 'speaking'; the sanad is 'animal', which is absolutely more general than 'speaking'. The author then states that this division is useless for the questioner because proving the more general does not prove the more specific. However, if the reasoner negates the sanad (i.e., proves that the object is not an animal), then the negation of the claim follows (since negating the more general negates the more specific), and the claim itself becomes true (since contradictories can't both be false). The author references his earlier discussion on conditional syllogisms for further clarification.

The Fifth Division: Sanad More General and More Specific in One Respect
Author explains fifth division with example and declares its uselessness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a sanad is more general than the negation of the claim in one respect and more specific in another, and explain why such a sanad is useless in jadal.

Core Idea
When sanad and claim negation intersect, neither proves nor disproves the other.

In jadal (dialectical debate), the questioner often attacks a premise by providing a sanad (reason) that seems to contradict the claim. However, sometimes the sanad and the negation of the claim are related in a way that makes the attack ineffective. This lesson examines the fifth division, where the sanad is more general in one respect and more specific in another, and shows why it offers no benefit to either party.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reasoner's claim and proof
The reasoner claims 'this phantom is breathing' and proves it by saying it is human and every human breathes. This is a categorical syllogism in the first figure. Note the minor premise: 'this phantom is a human'.
2
Locate the questioner's objection and sanad
The questioner prevents the minor premise ('it is a human') and attaches a causal sanad: 'Why is it not possible that it be white?' This means the questioner suggests the phantom could be white, implying it might not be human.
The sanad is the possibility of being white, not a certainty.
3
Determine the relationship between sanad and negation
The prevented premise is 'human', its negation is 'not human'. The sanad is 'white'. Examine how 'white' and 'non-human' relate: they intersect (in snow and ivory), but each has exclusive instances. This is called 'generality and specificity in one respect' (عموم وخصوص من وجه).
They are neither fully general nor fully specific relative to each other.
4
Assess the usefulness of this sanad
The author declares this division useless because when two terms are related in this way, the existence of one does not necessitate the negation of the other, nor its non-existence. Therefore, the sanad cannot prove or disprove the claim.
The reasoner can still defend the original proof; the objection fails to undermine it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الخامس منها : هو مايكون فيه السند أعم من نقيض الدعوى من وجه وأخص منه من وجه .
ومثاله أن يقول المعلل : هذا الشبح متنفس، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه إنسان وكل إنسان متنفس، ينتج : هو ـ أي ذلك الشبح - متنفس .
فيقول السائل : أمنعُ صغرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك: لأنه إنسان، ثم يجعل هذا المنع مقرونا بالسند اللمي فيقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكن أبيض؟ فالمقدمة الممنوعة هي كونه إنسانًا، ونقيضها (ليس بإنسان والسند جواز كونه أبيض ، و (الأبيض) و (غير الإنسان بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه يجتمعان في الثلج والعاج، فكلاهما أبيض وكلاهما غير إنسان، وينفرد غير (الإنسان عن الأبيض في الغراب والفحم، فكلاهما غير إنسان وليس واحد منهما بأبيض، وينفرد الأبيض عن (غير الإنسان في الإنسان الأبيض، فهو إنسان أبيض، وليس (غير إنسان).
وهذا القسم لا فائدة في إثباته ولا نفيه للمعلل ولا للمستدل؛ لأن الأعمّين من وجه لا يقتضي وجود أحدهما نفي الآخر ولا عدمه ولا 
(۱) راجع ص ۱۳۰ .
The fifth division is that in which the sanad is more general than the negation of the claim in one respect and more specific in another respect. An example: The reasoner says: 'This phantom is breathing,' then establishes a proof for that by saying: 'Because it is a human, and every human is breathing,' yielding: 'It – i.e. that phantom – is breathing.' The questioner says: 'I prevent the minor premise of this proof of yours, which is your statement: "because it is a human,"' then he makes this prevention accompanied by the causal sanad by saying: 'Why is it not possible that it be white?' So the prevented premise is its being a human, and its negation is 'not a human', and the sanad is the possibility of its being white. And 'white' and 'non-human' are related as general and specific in one respect: they meet in snow and ivory, for both are white and both are non-human; non-human is exclusive of white in ravens and charcoal, for both are non-human and neither is white; white is exclusive of non-human in the white human, for he is a white human, not a non-human. This division is of no benefit in either proving or negating it for the reasoner or the prover, because two things that are general and specific in one respect do not necessitate that the existence of one negates the other nor its non-existence nor...
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth division of the same type. The author defines it: the sanad is more general than the negation of the claim in one respect and more specific in another. He gives an example: the reasoner claims 'this phantom is breathing' and argues: it is human, every human breathes (first figure, conclusion: it breathes). The questioner prevents the minor premise 'it is human' and attaches a causal sanad: 'Why is it not possible that it be white?' The prevented premise is 'human', its negation is 'non-human', and the sanad is 'white'. The author then explains the relationship between 'white' and 'non-human': they are related as general and specific in one respect (عموم وخصوص من وجه). They intersect in objects like snow and ivory (both white and non-human). 'Non-human' is exclusive of 'white' in ravens and charcoal (non-human but not white). 'White' is exclusive of 'non-human' in a white human (white but human). The author then states that this division is useless for both parties because if two things are related in this way, neither proving nor negating one implies anything about the other. The argument ends with an incomplete sentence, referencing an earlier discussion.

Key Terms
السند اللمي
عموم وخصوص من وجه

When sanad and claim negation intersect, the sanad cannot prove or disprove the claim, making the division useless in jadal.

The Sixth Division: Ground Completely Different from Negation
Defines the sixth type of ground-objection relationship with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a ground (sind) is completely different (mutabayin) from the negation of the claim and explain why such an objection is useless in jadal.

Core Idea
Complete difference between ground and negation gives no logical connection—the objection is pointless.

In the science of disputation (adab al-bahth), objections must be logically relevant. The sixth and final division of grounds involves complete difference (tabayun). This lesson explores why this type of objection fails to advance the debate and how to recognize it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the denied premise and its negation
In the objection, locate the premise that the objector denies (e.g., 'this phantom is not a human'). Its negation is that premise's contradictory (e.g., 'this phantom is a human').
2
Extract the ground provided by the objector
Find the ground (sind) the objector gives to support his denial (e.g., 'if it is a stone'). This is the condition under which the denial holds.
3
Compare the ground with the negation of the denied premise
Determine the relationship between the ground and the negation. Are they the same? Different? Contradictory? Here, 'stone' and 'human' are completely different (mutabayin) — they have no instances in common.
Tabayun is mutual exclusivity: no overlap in instances.
4
Assess the logical relevance of the objection
If the ground and negation are completely different, the ground provides no support for the denial because it does not bear on the truth of the negation. The objection is therefore useless.
Useful objections must have a logical link (e.g., identity, partial overlap).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم السادس منها : هو مايكون فيه السند مباينا لنقيض الدعوى الممنوعة . ومثاله أن يقول المعلل : هذا الشبح غير مفكر، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه غير إنسان، وكل غير إنسان فهو غير مفكر، ينتج من الشكل الأول : هو - أي ذلك الشبح ـ غير مفكر . فيقول السائل : أمنعُ صغرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك : لأنه غير إنسان، ثم يجعل هذا المنع مقرونا بالسند فيقول: محل كونه غير إنسان إذا كان حجرًا، فالمقدمة الممنوعة هي (غير إنسان)، ونقيضها (هو إنسان)، والسند هو إذا كان حجرًا، وكونه إنسانًا وكونه حجرًا متباينان، لأن النسبة بين الإنسان والحجر التباين، كما تقدم إيضاحه . وهذا القسم السادس لا فائدة البتةَ في الإتيان به للمعلل ولا للمستدل كما لا يخفى
The sixth division of them: it is that in which the ground is completely different from the negation of the denied claim. Its example is that the arguer says: 'This phantom is not thinking,' then he establishes evidence for that by saying: 'Because it is not a human, and every non-human is not thinking,' producing from the first figure: 'It—that is, that phantom—is not thinking.' Then the questioner says: 'I deny the minor premise of this evidence of yours, which is your saying: because it is not a human.' Then he couples this denial with the ground and says: 'The locus of its being not a human is if it is a stone.' So the denied premise is 'not a human,' its negation is 'it is a human,' and the ground is 'if it is a stone.' And 'it is a human' and 'it is a stone' are completely different because the relationship between human and stone is complete difference, as previously clarified. And this sixth division has absolutely no benefit in bringing it up for the arguer or the reasoner, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the sixth and final type of objection in the classification. Here, the ground (sind) given by the objector is completely different (mutabayin) from the negation of the claim. In the example, the arguer claims something is not thinking because it is not a human. The objector denies the minor premise ('it is not a human') and provides a ground: 'it is a stone.' The negation of the denied premise is 'it is a human,' and the ground 'it is a stone' is completely different from 'human' (they are mutually exclusive with no overlap). The author notes that this type of objection is utterly useless because the ground has no bearing on the negation of the claim; their complete difference means the ground neither supports nor refutes the claim. The passage reiterates that the relationship of tabayun (mutual exclusivity) provides no logical connection, making the objection irrelevant in disputation.

Key Terms
السند
نقيض الدعوى
المباين
التباين
الشكل الأول

C) Because complete difference provides no logical connection between the ground and the negation.
D) Because the objector always loses such objections.

Key Takeaway
When the ground (sind) is completely different (mutabayin) from the negation of the claim, the objection provides no logical link and is therefore useless in jadal.

Combining the Six Divisions with the Three Reasoning Modes
States that each division may combine with the three modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how each of the six divisions of objections in munazara can be combined with the causal, certain, and descriptive modes of reasoning.

Core Idea
The six divisions are not rigid; each can be applied with causal, certain, or descriptive reasoning.

In previous lessons, you learned about six divisions of objections in munazara, defined by the relationship between the ground and the negation of the claim. You also learned three modes of reasoning: causal (lami), certain (qat'i), and descriptive (hali). This brief note by the author clarifies that these are not separate systems—every division can be expressed using any of the three modes. This flexibility is crucial for effective dialectical engagement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modes
Recall the three modes: causal (lami) gives the cause, certain (qat'i) presents the ground as decisive, and descriptive (hali) describes a state or condition. These are ways to present the ground in an objection.
The modes are not tied to any specific division.
2
Understand the combinatorial claim
The note states that each of the six divisions can be used with any of the three modes. This means, for example, a division where the ground is identical to the negation can be presented in causal, certain, or descriptive fashion.
No division is inherently restricted to one mode.
3
Appreciate the practical implication
In a real debate, the objector can choose the mode that best supports the ground, regardless of which division the objection belongs to. This increases argumentative flexibility.
The choice of mode depends on the nature of the evidence and the opponent's stance.
4
Recognize the author's closure
The author asserts that sufficient examples have been provided throughout the discussion. This indicates that the classification is now complete for the reader to apply in practice.
Review earlier examples to see the combinations in action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : اعلم أن كل واحد من هذه الأقسام الستة التي ذكرنا يجوز أن يؤتى به على الأوجه الثلاثة المتقدمة، التي هي اللمي والقطعي والحَلي، وقد أوردنا من الأمثلة مافيه كفاية .
A note: Know that each of these six divisions that we have mentioned may be used with the three previously-mentioned modes, which are the causal (al-lami), the certain (al-qat'i), and the descriptive (al-hali). And we have provided sufficient examples.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This brief concluding note clarifies that the six types of objections (defined by the relationship between the ground and the negation of the claim) are not limited to a single form of reasoning. Instead, each can be employed with any of the three earlier modes: lami (causal reasoning that gives the cause), qat'i (certain or decisive reasoning), and hali (descriptive reasoning that provides a state or condition). The author asserts that enough examples have been given throughout the discussion to illustrate these combinations. This note emphasizes the flexibility and comprehensive nature of the classification.

Key Takeaway

The Sixth Type of Evidence: Evidence Opposite to Negation
Author defines the sixth type of evidence where the evidence is opposite to the negation of the denied claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sixth type of evidence (sanad) in dialectical debate and explain why it is useless for both parties.

Core Idea
The sixth type of evidence opposes the negation of the claim but brings no benefit.

The passage presents the sixth type of evidence in dialectical debate, where the evidence (sanad) is opposite to the negation of the denied claim. Through a concrete example of a categorical syllogism, it illustrates how the objector denies a premise by attaching a conditional context. The author concludes that this type is utterly useless. Understanding this helps you recognise when a dialectical move is self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Identify the denied premise and its negation
In the example, the mu'allil claims 'this specter is not thinking' and uses the minor premise 'it is not a human'. The questioner denies this minor premise, so the denied premise is 'it is not a human'. Its negation is 'it is human'. Find these in the text.
2
Locate the evidence (sanad) attached to the denial
The questioner attaches a condition: 'if it is a stone'. This condition is the evidence. It is not a simple denial but a conditional one. Identify the phrase 'إذا كان حجرًا' as the sanad.
3
Check the relation between evidence and the negation of the denied premise
The negation of the denied premise is 'it is human'. The evidence is 'if it is a stone'. Human and stone are mutually exclusive (mubāyana). So the evidence is opposite to the negation. Confirm this relation as described.
4
Evaluate the usefulness of this type of evidence
The author states this sixth section has absolutely no benefit for either the mu'allil or the mustadill. Reflect on why: the conditional evidence does not actually refute or support the original claim in a way that advances the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم السادس منها: هو مايكون فيه السند مباينا لنقيض الدعوى الممنوعة. ومثاله أن يقول المعلل: هذا الشبح غير مفكر، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول: لأنه غير إنسان، وكل غير إنسان فهو غير مفكر، ينتج من الشكل الأول: هو - أي ذلك الشبح - غير مفكر. فيقول السائل: أمنع صغرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك: لأنه غير إنسان، ثم يجعل هذا المنع مقرونا بالسند فيقول: محل كونه غير إنسان إذا كان حجرًا، فالمقدمة الممنوعة هي (غير إنسان)، ونقيضها (هو إنسان)، والسند هو (إذا كان حجرًا)، وكونه إنسانًا وكونه حجرًا متباينان؛ لأن النسبة بين الإنسان والحجر التباين، كما تقدم إيضاحه. وهذا القسم السادس لا فائدة البتة في الإتيان به للمعلل ولا للمستدل كما لا يخفى. تنبيه: اعلم أن كل واحد من هذه الأقسام الستة التي ذكرنا يجوز أن يؤتى به على الأوجه الثلاثة المتقدمة، التي هي اللمي والقطعي والحَلي، وقد أوردنا من الأمثلة ما فيه كفاية.
The sixth section of them: it is that in which the evidence is opposite to the negation of the denied claim. An example is that the mu'allil says: 'This specter is not thinking', then he establishes the proof for that saying: 'Because it is not a human, and every not-human is not thinking', producing from the first figure: 'It – i.e., that specter – is not thinking.' Then the questioner says: 'I deny the minor premise of your proof, which is your statement: 'because it is not a human', then he makes this denial accompanied by the evidence, saying: 'The locus of its being not a human is if it is a stone.' So the denied premise is 'not human', and its negation is 'it is human', and the evidence is 'if it is a stone', and its being human and its being stone are mutually exclusive because the relation between human and stone is opposition, as previously explained. And this sixth section has absolutely no benefit in bringing it forth for the mu'allil nor for the arguer, as is not hidden. Note: Know that each of these six sections we mentioned is permissible to be presented in the three aforementioned modes: the limi, the qat'i, and the hali. And we have presented from examples what suffices.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth type of evidence in dialectical debate (āda al-baḥth). The evidence (sanad) is described as being opposite (mubāyin) to the negation of the denied claim. A concrete example is given: The arguer claims a specter is not thinking, reasoning that it is not human, and everything not human is not thinking – a categorical syllogism in the first figure. The objector denies the minor premise ('it is not human') but attaches a condition: 'if it is a stone'. Thus the denial is accompanied by a specific context. The passage clarifies that this type of evidence is entirely useless for both parties. The note concludes by stating that each of the six types can be presented in three modes: al-lamī (from cause), al-qaṭʿī (certain), and al-ḥalī (descriptive), with sufficient examples already given.

Key Terms
السند (al-sanad): The evidence or supporting reason used by the arguer in dialectical debate to justify a premise or claim. [Note: In later usul al-fiqh, sanad also refers to the chain of transmission in hadith, but here it means the logical support for a claim.]
المباينة (al-mubāyana): The relation of mutual exclusivity or opposition between two terms, e.g., human and stone. [Note: No significant shift; standard logical meaning.]
المنع (al-manʿ): The objection or denial by the questioner of a premise or claim in debate. [Note: Stable term in dialectic and usul al-fiqh.]
اللمي والقطعي والحلي (al-lamī, al-qaṭʿī, al-ḥalī): Three modes of proof: lamī (from cause or why), qaṭʿī (categorical or certain), ḥalī (circumstantial or descriptive). [Note: These terms are specific to post-classical dialectic; earlier logic uses different classifications.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The sixth type of evidence, where the evidence opposes the negation of the denied claim, is recognized as useless in dialectical debate.

First Response to Denial: Proving the Same Claim
Author explains the first function of the arguer in responding to denial by producing a proof for the same claim, its equivalent, or a more specific claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a response to a dialectical denial by proving the same claim, an equivalent claim, or a more specific claim, using a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
When your opponent denies your claim, prove it again — or something equivalent or more specific.

In dialectical debate, denial is not the end. The arguer (mu'allil) has several tools to respond. The first and most direct is to produce a fresh proof that re-establishes the denied claim — or a logically equivalent or more specific claim. This works for both bare denials and those backed by evidence. The classic kalam example in this lesson shows how a categorical syllogism in the first figure does the job.

Guided Path
1
Identify the denied claim
Find the exact da'wa that the questioner denied. In the example, it is 'The world is originated' (العالم حادث).
The denial may be directed at a premise or a bare claim.
2
Choose the target of your proof
Decide whether you will prove the same claim, an equivalent claim, or a more specific claim. Proving an equivalent or more specific is logically sufficient.
Proving the more specific implies the general, and proving the equivalent proves the original.
3
Construct a categorical syllogism
Build a valid first-figure syllogism whose conclusion is your target. In the example: Everything changing is originated; the world is changing; therefore the world is originated.
Ensure the syllogism terms are clear and the mood is valid (e.g., Barbara).
4
Verify the proof's relevance
Check that the conclusion matches your chosen target (same, equivalent, or more specific). If it's more specific, confirm that it entails the original general claim.
The proof stands as a valid response to the denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأجوبة عن المنع. اعلم أن للمعلل وظائف في جوابه عن المنع الذي منع به السائل إحدى مقدمات دليله أو دعواه المجردة عن الدليل: الأولى أن يقيم المعلل دليلا ينتج نفس الدعوى التي منعها السائل، أو ينتج دعوى أخرى تساويها، أو ينتج دعوى أخرى أخص منها مطلقًا؛ لأن إثبات مساوي الشيء إثبات له، وإثبات الأخص يستلزم إثبات الأعم، كما تقدم إيضاحه. وهذا الجواب يصلح للرد على المنع المجرد والمصحوب بالسند جميعا. ومثال إقامته الدليل على الدعوى المجردة التي منعها السائل أن يقول: العالم حادث فيقول خصمه القائل بقدم العالم من الفلاسفة: أمنع دعواك هذه التي هي العالم حادث. فيقيم المعلل الدليل عليها جوابًا لمنع السائل فيقول: العالم متغير بالانعدام ونحوه وكل متغير كذلك فهو حادث، ينتج من الشكل الأول: العالم حادث، وهو عين الدعوى الممنوعة.
Section on responses to denial. Know that the mu'allil has functions in his response to the denial with which the questioner denied one of the premises of his proof or his bare claim without proof: The first: that the mu'allil produces a proof that yields the same claim that the questioner denied, or yields another claim equal to it, or yields another claim more specific than it absolutely; because establishing the equivalent of a thing is establishing it, and establishing the more specific necessitates establishing the more general, as previously explained. And this response is suitable for replying to both the bare denial and the denial accompanied by evidence. An example of his producing proof for the bare claim that the questioner denied: that he says: 'The world is originated', so his opponent who holds the eternity of the world from the philosophers says: 'I deny this claim of yours which is: the world is originated.' So the mu'allil produces proof for it as a response to the denial of the questioner, saying: 'The world is changing by non-existence and the like, and everything changing like that is originated', producing from the first figure: 'The world is originated', and it is exactly the denied claim.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on how the arguer (mu'allil) should respond when the questioner (sā'il) denies a premise or an unproven claim. The first function is to produce a proof that establishes either exactly the same denied claim, or a claim equal to it, or a more specific claim. The logical justification is given: proving the equivalent proves the original, and proving a more specific claim necessitates the more general. This response works for both bare denial (without accompanying evidence) and denial accompanied by evidence. The example illustrates a classic kalām argument: the opponent (a philosopher believing in the eternity of the world) denies the claim 'the world is originated'. The arguer responds with a proof: 'The world is changing (by passing out of existence, etc.) and everything changing is originated; therefore, the world is originated.' This is a categorical syllogism in the first figure, directly proving the originally denied claim.

Key Terms
المنع
المعلل
الدعوى
الأخص والأعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what three types of claims can the arguer prove in response to a denial?
A) The same claim, the opposite claim, or a different claim.
B) The same claim, an equivalent claim, or a more specific claim.
C) The same claim, a more general claim, or an equivalent claim.
D) Only the exact same claim.

Key Takeaway
When your claim is denied, you can respond by proving the same claim, an equivalent claim, or a more specific claim, using a valid syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The First Function of Answering an Objection: Proving the Denied Claim
Author defines the first function of answering an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which type of claim (identical, co-extensive, or more specific) a prover can prove in response to an objection, and construct a valid syllogism from a kalam example.

Core Idea
When your claim is denied, you can prove it, an equal, or a more specific claim.

In a dialectical debate (jadal), when the objector denies a claim, the prover must respond. The author of this passage explains the first of three functions: the prover can produce a proof that yields the same claim, an equivalent claim, or a more specific claim. Using a classic kalam argument, he demonstrates how proving the more specific entails the more general. This lesson focuses on that first function and its logical basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prover's original claim and the objection
Find the claim that the objector (sā'il) denies. In the example, the prover (mu'allil) claims 'the world is originated (ḥādith).' The objector responds with 'I deny this claim.'
2
Understand the three options for a response
The prover can prove: (1) the exact same claim, (2) a claim logically equal (co-extensive) to it, or (3) a more specific (akhass) claim. The author notes that proving an equal or more specific claim entails the original.
The logical principle: if A = B then proving B proves A; if A ⊃ B then proving B proves A.
3
Analyze the example syllogism
The prover constructs: 'The world is changing through annihilation. Everything changing in that way is originated. Therefore, the world is originated.' This is a first-figure categorical syllogism (Barbara).
Check the terms: major term = originated, minor = world, middle = changing.
4
Verify the syllogism's validity
The first figure requires the middle term to be the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise. Here: Major: everything changing is originated; Minor: the world is changing; Conclusion: the world is originated. Valid.
This directly proves the denied claim.
5
Generalize the strategy
If the objector denies the claim, the prover can always prove it by finding a middle term that connects the subject to the predicate. The example uses 'changing' as the middle term.
This is one of the standard jadal tactics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأجوبة عن المنع اعلم أن للمعلل وظائف في جوابه عن المنع الذي منع به السائل إحدى مقدمات دليله أو دعواه المجردة عن الدليل : الأول ذلك أن من دليلا يقيم ينتج نفس الدعوى التي منعها السائل، أو ينتج دعوى أخرى تساويها، أو ينتج دعوى أخرى أخص منها مطلقًا؛ لأن إثبات مساوي الشيء إثبات له، وإثبات الأخص يستلزم إثبات الأعم، كما تقدم إيضاحه . وهذا الجواب يصلح للرد على المنع المجرد والمصحوب بالسند جميعا . ومثال إقامته الدليل على الدعوى المجردة التي منعها السائل أن يقول : العالم ،حادث فيقول خصمه القائل بقدم العالم من الفلاسفة : أمنع دعواك هذه التي [هي] العالم حادث . فيقيم المعلل الدليل عليها جوابًا لمنع السائل فيقول : العالم متغير بالانعدام ونحوه وكل متغير كذلك فهو حادث، ينتج من الشكل الأول : العالم حادث، وهو عين الدعوى الممنوعة
Section on answers to the objection. Know that the prover has functions in his answer to the objection with which the objector denied one of the premises of his proof or his bare claim. The first: that he produces a proof that yields the same claim that the objector denied, or yields another claim equal to it, or yields another claim more specific than it absolutely; because proving the equal of a thing is proving it, and proving the more specific entails proving the more general, as previously clarified. And this answer is suitable for replying to the bare objection and the one accompanied by evidence alike. An example of his producing proof for the bare claim that the objector denied: he says: 'The world is originated.' So his opponent, the philosopher who believes in the pre-eternity of the world, says: 'I deny this claim of yours, [which is] that the world is originated.' So the prover produces proof for it in answer to the objector's denial, saying: 'The world is changing through annihilation and the like, and everything changing in that way is originated.' This yields from the first figure: 'The world is originated,' which is the very claim that was denied.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a definition and exemplification of the first function of answering an objection in dialectical debate (jadal). The author begins by stating that when the objector (السائل) denies a premise of the proof or a bare claim, the prover (المعلل) has three options. The first option is to produce a proof that directly yields the same claim that was denied. The author explains that proving an equal claim is also proving the original, and proving a more specific claim entails proving the more general. These logical principles justify the three functions. He then gives a concrete example from kalam: the claim that the world is originated (حادث) is denied by a philosopher who holds that the world is pre-eternal (قديم). The prover counters by constructing a categorical syllogism in the first figure: the world is changing (متغير), and everything changing is originated, therefore the world is originated. This directly proves the denied claim.

Proving a More Specific Claim: The Third Function
Author introduces the third function with an incomplete modal example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third function of answering an objection and explain how proving a more specific claim logically entails the original denied claim.

Core Idea
Proving a stronger claim proves the weaker one automatically.

In dialectical debate, when an objector denies a claim, the prover has several options. The third function is to produce a proof that yields a claim more specific (أخص) than the one denied. This is a powerful logical move because if the stronger claim is true, the weaker one must also be true. The passage begins a modal example illustrating this, though the text cuts off; nonetheless, the principle is clear and widely applicable.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three functions
The first function proves the same claim; the second proves an equivalent claim; the third proves a more specific claim. This passage focuses on the third.
A more specific claim is logically stronger: it restricts the original claim in some way.
2
Understand the entailment
If B is more specific than A, then B entails A. Therefore, proving B automatically proves A. For example, necessity entails possibility.
3
Analyze the modal example
The denied claim is a modal proposition with possibility (الإمكان). The prover attempts to establish it with absolute necessity (الإطلاق). Since necessity implies possibility, proving necessity suffices to prove the original possibility claim.
The passage breaks off at 'because', but the logical structure is clear.
4
Identify the incompleteness
The text ends abruptly; the detailed proof is missing. Nevertheless, the conceptual framework is complete for understanding the function.
Incomplete passages require careful interpretation; focus on what is given.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

An example of producing a proof that is more specific than the denied claim: that the discussion of the two disputants is about a modal proposition with possibility; the objector denies its modality which is possibility; so the prover provides proof for the validity of its modality with absolute (necessity) because...
