This passage introduces the third function of answering an objection: proving a claim that is more specific (أخص) than the denied claim. The author begins an example from modal logic (موجّهة) concerning possibility (الإمكان). The objector denies that the modality is possibility; the prover then attempts to prove that the modality is actually absolute (الإطلاق, likely meaning necessity or unrestricted). By proving the stronger modality (more specific), the original claim (possibility) is implied. However, the passage ends abruptly at 'because', leaving the example incomplete. The student should understand that while the example is not fully given, the logical principle is clear: proving a more specific claim entails proving the more general.

Invalidating the Opponent's Basis in Debate
Author defines the second duty of the proponent in debate: to invalidate the evidence relied upon by the opponent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a muʿallil uses the strategy of invalidating the sanad and reconstruct the debate exchange accordingly.

Core Idea
Attack the basis, not the objection itself.

In dialectical exchanges (jadal), the proponent (muʿallil) must respond when the objector (sāʾil) denies a premise. This lesson introduces one such response: instead of defending the premise directly, the muʿallil attacks the evidence (sanad) that the sāʾil relied on for his denial. This tactical move shifts the burden back to the objector.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sāʾil's objection (manʿ)
Find which premise or claim the objector has denied. This is the starting point of the exchange.
The objection could target a premise of a proof or a bare claim.
2
Reconstruct the sanad (basis) of the objection
Determine the evidence or reasoning the sāʾil uses to support his denial. This is what the muʿallil will target.
The sanad may be a scriptural text, a rational argument, or a scholarly consensus.
3
Analyze the muʿallil's invalidation of the sanad
Observe how the muʿallil undermines that basis—by questioning its authenticity, its interpretation, or its applicability.
This is not a defense of the original premise but an attack on the objector's support.
4
Evaluate the effectiveness of the invalidation
Consider whether the muʿallil's response successfully refutes the objection or leaves the debate open.
A valid invalidation renders the sāʾil's denial unsupported.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني من وظائف المعلل في جوابه عن منع السائل إحدى مقدمات دليله أو دعواه المجردة هو أن يبطل السند الذي استند إليه
The second aspect of the functions of the proponent (al-muʿallil) in his response to the objector's denial of one of the premises of his proof or his bare claim is that he invalidates the basis (sanad) upon which [the objector] relied.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage introduces the second function of the muʿallil (the proponent) in a debate. After the sāʾil (objector) either denies one of the premises of the muʿallil's proof or denies the bare claim (without proof), the muʿallil must respond by invalidating the basis (al-sanad) that the objector relied upon for his objection. In other words, instead of directly defending the premise, the muʿallil attacks the source or evidence that the objector uses to support his denial. This is a common tactic in jadal.

Proving Possibility from Actual Occurrence
Author explains the dialectical inference from actual occurrence to possibility using the ru'ya example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism that proves possibility from actual occurrence in a dialectical argument and explain why the specific entails the general.

Core Idea
Proving actual occurrence is a stronger proof for possibility because the specific entails the general.

In dialectical debates, an opponent may concede that something is possible but deny that it actually occurs. This lesson shows how the proponent (mutallil) can use a stronger proof: by establishing actual occurrence from a revealed text, he automatically establishes possibility, because the existence of the specific (actual occurrence) necessitates the existence of the general (possibility). The passage uses the example of seeing Allah (ru'ya) on the Day of Resurrection, citing Quran 75:22-23.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dialectical positions
The opponent claims that ru'ya (seeing Allah) is merely possible (imkan). The proponent aims to prove that it actually occurs (wuqu' fi'li). Understanding the stakes: the opponent denies actuality but may accept possibility.
2
Extract the categorical syllogism
The proponent constructs a syllogism: Major premise: 'Everything Allah establishes in His Book is true.' Minor premise: 'Allah established in His Book the actual occurrence of ru'ya (in 75:22-23).' Conclusion: 'Therefore, ru'ya is true and actual.' This is a first-figure syllogism (Barbara).
The major premise is implied: 'Whatever Allah says is true.'
3
Apply the specific-generic principle
Actual occurrence is more specific (akhas) than possibility (a'am). Since the specific exists, the general must exist. Thus, proving actual occurrence automatically proves possibility. Recognize this as a logical implication: if A (specific) then B (general).
The Arabic phrase: 'وجود الأخص يستلزم وجود الأعم' (the existence of the specific entails the existence of the general).
4
Understand why this defeats the opponent
The opponent's claim of mere possibility is weaker. By proving actual occurrence, the proponent forces the opponent to accept possibility as a necessary consequence. If the opponent rejects the conclusion, he must deny the premises (e.g., the Quranic verse or its truth).
The author notes that Mu'tazila objections using other verses have been refuted elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا علمت أن المناظرة في الإمكان علمت أن المعلل إذا أقام دليلاً ينتج الإطلاق، الذي هو الوقوع الفعلي، الذي هو أخص قطعا من الإمكان، فكأنه أقام الدليل على الإمكان؛ لأن وجود الأخص يستلزم وجود الأعم. وإذا علمت ذلك فاعلم أن صورة استدلاله على ذلك أن يقول : الله أثبت في كتابه وقوع الرؤية بالفعل في قوله : ﴿ وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ ﴾ إلَى رَتِهَا نَاظِرَةٌ [القيامة/ ۲۲ - ۲۳] فصح بنظرها إلى ربها بالفعل يوم القيامة، وكل ما أثبته الله في كتابه فهو حق صحيح، ينتج من الشكل الأول : الرؤية حق صحيح، وهذه النتيجة أخـص مـن الـدعـوى الممنوعة، التي هي الإمكان، فافهمه؛ لأنه لا يقع بالفعل إلا ماهو ممكن الوقوع، ومن المعلوم بالضرورة أن المستحيل وقوعه عقلاً لا يقع بالفعل، كما لا يخفى . ومناقشة استدلال المعتزلة ببعض الآيات كقوله تعالى: ﴿لا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ ﴾ [الأنعام / ۱۰۳] وقوله : لَن تَركني ﴾ [الأعراف/ ١٤٣]، ونحو ذلك قد بينا بطلانها في غير هذا الموضع، ومقصودنا هنا مطلق المثال .
And if you know that the debate is about possibility, then you know that when the proponent produces a proof that yields actual occurrence, which is definitely more specific than possibility, it is as if he produced a proof for possibility, because the existence of the specific entails the existence of the general. And if you know that, then know that the form of his inference for that is that he says: Allah established in His Book the actual occurrence of vision in His saying: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' [75:22-23], so it is established that they look at their Lord actually on the Day of Resurrection, and everything that Allah established in His Book is true and correct, so from the first figure it yields: vision is true and correct. And this result is more specific than the prohibited claim, which is possibility, so understand it; because only that whose occurrence is possible occurs actually, and it is known necessarily that what is impossible to occur rationally does not occur actually, as is not hidden. And the discussion of the Mu'tazila's inference by some verses, like His saying: 'Sight does not perceive Him' [6:103] and His saying: 'You will not see Me' [7:143], and similar, we have explained their invalidity elsewhere, and our purpose here is merely the example.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage explains a key dialectical maneuver: the proponent (mutallil) can argue for possibility by proving actual occurrence. Since actual occurrence (al-wuqu' al-fi'li) is more specific than possibility (al-imkan), establishing it automatically establishes possibility. The author illustrates with the example of ru'ya (seeing Allah) on the Day of Resurrection. The proponent cites the Quranic verse that faces will look at their Lord, inferring that actual vision occurs. Using a first-figure categorical syllogism (Allah established it; whatever Allah establishes is true; therefore, vision is true), he gets a result that is more specific than the opponent's claim of mere possibility. The passage then notes that objections from the Mu'tazila using other verses have been refuted elsewhere, and this is just a general example.

Invalidating the Questioner's Evidence in Dialectic
Author defines and exemplifies the second function of the proponent: nullifying the evidence behind the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second function of the proponent in dialectic and apply it by attacking the questioner's evidence rather than defending the premise.

Core Idea
Attack the evidence, not the premise: invalidating the sanad collapses the objection.

In a dialectical debate, when the questioner objects to a premise, the proponent does not always have to defend it. The second function taught here empowers the proponent to invalidate the evidence behind the objection, thereby nullifying the objection itself. This is a powerful strategy that shifts the burden back to the questioner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and its evidence
Find the questioner's objection (man') and the evidence (sanad) he uses to support it. In the example, the objection is to the minor premise, and the evidence is causal possibilities.
The sanad is often introduced with 'why is it not possible?' or similar.
2
Recognize the proponent's target
Note that the proponent does not defend the original premise directly. Instead, he attacks the sanad. This is the second function.
3
Analyze the proponent's counter-argument
The proponent introduces a new fact (the apparition is a writer) and constructs a syllogism to show that the questioner's possibilities are impossible. Trace the syllogism: 'This apparition is a writer; every writer excludes those possibilities; therefore, this apparition excludes them.'
This is a first-figure categorical syllogism.
4
Understand the logical consequence
Invalidating the sanad invalidates the objection because the objection depends on the sanad. If the sanad is false, the objection falls, and the original claim stands.
The author notes that invalidating one of two coextensive things invalidates the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني من وظائف المعلل في جوابه عن منع السائل إحدى مقدمات دليله أو دعواه المجردة هو أن يبطل السند الذي استند إليه السائل في المنع المذكور، وهذا الجواب خاص بالمنع المقترن بالسند ؛ لأن إبطاله السند يستلزم بطلان المنع ؛ لأن المنع ماسو السند في نظر المانع دائما، وإن كان في نفس الأمر قد يكون بخلاف ذلك، كما بيناه في الأقسام الستة المذكورة آنفا، وإبطال أحد المتساويين إبطال لمساويه الآخر كما هو معلوم، وإذا ثبت بإبطال السند إبطال المنع فقد تحقق ثبوت نقيضه وهو الدعوى الممنوعة كما لا يخفى ؛ لأن النقيضين لا يرتفعان . و من أمثلة ذلك أن يقول المعلل صاحب التصديق : هذا الشبح إنسان، ثم يقيم دليله على ذلك فيقول : لأنه ناطق، وكل ناطق إنسان . فيمنع السائل صغرى دليله بسند لمّي فيقول : لا أسلّم أنّ هذا الشبحَ ناطق، لم لا يجوز أن يكون حجرا؟، أو لا يجوز أن يكون غير ضاحك؟ ، أو : لم لا يجوز أن يكون غير ناطق؟ ونحو ذلك فيقول المعلل : السائل هذه التجويزات العقلية التي استندت إليها في منع صُغرى دليلي كلها باطلة، ولا يجوز شيء منها عقلاً؛ لأن ذلك الشبح كاتب قطعًا، وكل كاتب لا يجوز فيه شيء مما ذكرت عقلاً، ينتج من الشكل الأول : ذلك الشبحُ لا يجوز فيه شيء مما ذكرت عقلاً، وإذا بطل هذا السند الجوازي بطل المنع كما تقدم إيضاحه قريباً .
The second aspect of the proponent's functions in his response to the questioner's objection to one of the premises of his proof or his bare claim is that he invalidates the evidence upon which the questioner relied in that objection, and this response is specific to the objection accompanied by evidence; because invalidating its evidence necessitates the invalidity of the objection, since the objection is always attached to the evidence in the view of the objector, even if in reality it may be otherwise, as we explained in the six divisions mentioned earlier. And invalidating one of two coextensive things is invalidating its coextensive other, as is known. And when the invalidity of the objection is established by invalidating the evidence, then the establishment of its opposite, which is the prohibited claim, is realized, as is not hidden, because two opposites do not both disappear. And of its examples is that the proponent, the possessor of assent, says: 'This apparition is a human.' Then he sets up his proof for that by saying: 'Because it is rational, and every rational is a human.' Then the questioner objects to the minor premise of his proof with a causal evidence, saying: 'I do not accept that this apparition is rational; why is it not possible that it is a stone? Or why is it not possible that it is not laughing? Or why is it not possible that it is not rational?' and similar. Then the proponent says: 'O questioner, these rational possibilities upon which you relied in objecting to the minor premise of my proof are all invalid, and none of them is rationally permissible; because that apparition is certainly a writer, and every writer does not permit anything you mentioned rationally.' This yields from the first figure: 'That apparition does not permit anything you mentioned rationally.' And when this evidential possibility is invalidated, the objection is invalidated, as its clarification has recently preceded.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage describes the second function (al-wajh al-thani) of the proponent (mutallil) in answering a questioner's objection (man'). The function is to invalidate the evidence (sanad) that the questioner used to support his objection. This only applies when the objection is accompanied by evidence. The reasoning is that if the questioner's evidence is false, the objection built on it collapses. The author notes that invalidating one of two coextensive things invalidates the other. He then provides an example: the proponent claims 'This apparition is a human' and proves it by the minor premise 'It is rational' and the major 'Every rational is human'. The questioner objects to the minor premise with a causal evidence (sanad lammi), suggesting possibilities: 'Why is it not possible that it is a stone?' etc. The proponent then attacks the questioner's evidence by showing that the apparition is certainly a writer, and every writer rationally excludes those possibilities. Using a first-figure syllogism (It is a writer; every writer excludes those possibilities; therefore, this apparition excludes those possibilities), he invalidates the questioner's evidence and thus the objection.

المنع
السائل

Invalidating the questioner's evidence is a strategic dialectical move that directly causes the objection to collapse.

Third Function: Tahrir al-Murada
Author introduces the third function: the proponent clarifies the intended meaning of his claim when it is objected to.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a proponent should use tahrir al-murada and formulate a clarifying statement for a contested claim in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
When a claim is met with a bare objection, clarify its intended meaning before proving it.

In dialectical debates (munazara), a proponent makes a claim, and the questioner may object. One common objection is a simple refusal: 'I do not accept your claim.' The proponent need not immediately provide proof; instead, he can first clarify what he means. This is the third function: tahrir al-murada. This lesson will teach you when and how to use this tool to keep the debate focused and productive.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the bare objection
The questioner says 'لا أسلم الدعوى' (I do not accept your claim). This is not a substantive argument but a request for clarification or a refusal to engage until the claim is clear.
2
Identify the contested claim
Pinpoint the exact statement being objected to. In the example: 'Zakat is not obligatory on trade goods.' Ambiguity may lie in terms like 'trade goods' (al-'urud) or 'zakat'.
3
Clarify the intended meaning
The proponent should explain what he means by key terms. For instance, he might specify that 'trade goods' means goods intended for sale, not personal property, or define 'zakat' according to a particular school.
4
Apply the clarification to the debate
After clarification, the questioner may accept the clarified claim or raise a substantive objection. This prevents misunderstandings and keeps the debate on track.

Source Text

والوجه الثالث : من وظائف المعلل في جوابه عن المنع المذكور هو تحرير المراد من المدعى - باسم المفعول - الممنوع ، كأن يقول المعلل : لا تجب الزكاة في العروض، فيقول السائل : لا أسلم دعواك
And the third aspect: of the proponent's functions in his response to the mentioned objection is clarifying the intended meaning of the claimed proposition (as a passive participle) that is objected to. For example, the proponent says: 'Zakat is not obligatory on trade goods.' Then the questioner says: 'I do not accept your claim.'

Commentary
This passage introduces the third function (al-wajh al-thalith) of the proponent in answering an objection: clarifying the intended meaning (tahrir al-murada) of the claim that is being objected to. The author uses a simple example: the proponent says 'Zakat is not obligatory on trade goods' (a legal claim in fiqh). The questioner responds with a bare objection: 'I do not accept your claim.' The passage ends here on this page, but the idea is that the proponent should then explain what exactly he means by 'trade goods' or 'zakat' to resolve the dispute. This function is a standard technique in dialectic to avoid ambiguity.

Tahrir al-murada is a dialectical tool to resolve ambiguity before engaging in proof, ensuring the debate proceeds on clear terms.

Tahrir al-Murad: Clarifying Scope to Answer an Objection
Author describes how the mu'allil answers a man' by clarifying the scope of the objected premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply tahrir al-murad to respond to a man' by restricting a universal premise to typical cases, preserving the argument's validity.

Core Idea
Clarifying intent, not changing the argument, is the proper defense against rare counterexamples.

In Islamic dialectic, when an opponent denies a universal premise by citing a rare counterexample, the proponent does not need to abandon the premise. Instead, he can clarify that the premise was intended to cover only well-known cases. This maneuver, called tahrir al-murad, preserves the argument's force while acknowledging the exception. The passage illustrates this with a syllogism about animals moving their lower jaws.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism and the objected premise
Find the categorical syllogism the mu'allil constructs and the premise the opponent denies. The mu'allil says: 'This is an animal, and every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing, therefore this moves its lower jaw when chewing.' The opponent denies the major premise: 'every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing.'
2
Understand the opponent's objection
The opponent cites a counterexample: the crocodile, which moves its upper jaw. This challenges the universality of the premise. Note that the opponent does not attack the logic but the factual claim.
The crocodile is a real counterexample in nature.
3
Examine the mu'allil's response (tahrir al-murad)
The mu'allil clarifies that by 'every animal' he means only the well-known, customary animals familiar to the public, not rare exceptions like the crocodile. This is a restriction of the scope of the premise.
The premise is reinterpreted as a generalisation about typical animals.
4
Evaluate the legitimacy of the response
In Islamic dialectic, tahrir al-murad is considered valid when the objector exploits a rare case that the original claim did not intend to cover. It is not a dodge but a proper clarification of the speaker's intended meaning.
The burden is on the objector to show that the exception falls within the intended scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة وكذلك الجواب عن المنع المذكور بتحرير المراد من الممنوعة، كأنه يقول المعلل: هذا حيوان، وكل حيوان يحرك فكه الأسفل عند المضغ، ينتج من الشكل الأول: هذا يحرك فكه الأسفل عند المضغ. فيقول السائل: أمنع كبرى دليلك هذا، وهي قولك (وكل حيوان يحرك فكه الأسفل عند المضغ؛ لأن التمساح حيوان لا يحرك فكه الأسفل عند المضغ، وإنما يحرك فكه الأعلى. فيجيب المعلل عن منع هذه المقدمة بتحرير المراد منها فيقول: مرادي بقولي (وكل حيوان يحرك فكه الأسفل عند المضغ) أفراد الحيوان الظاهرة المشهورة المتعارفة عند العامة، ولا أقصد الفرد النادر الذي لا يكاد يخطر بالبال لقلة ملابسة أغلبية الناس له ملابسة تؤدي إلى معرفة شيء عنه. فقد أجاب عن منع كبرى دليله بتحرير مراده منها.
The introduction: Likewise, the answer to the mentioned objection by clarifying the intended meaning of the objected premise, as if the mu'allil says: This is an animal, and every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing, which yields from the first figure: This moves its lower jaw when chewing. The questioner says: I deny the major premise of your proof, which is your statement 'every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing' because the crocodile is an animal that does not move its lower jaw when chewing, but rather moves its upper jaw. So the mu'allil answers by clarifying the intended meaning of this premise, saying: By my statement 'every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing' I mean the apparent, well-known, customary individuals of animals among the common people, and I do not intend the rare individual that hardly occurs to the mind due to the lack of association of most people with it in a way that leads to knowing something about it. Thus he answered the denial of the major premise of his proof by clarifying his intended meaning from it.

Commentary
This passage describes a dialectical maneuver in which the proponent (mu'allil) responds to an objection (man') by clarifying the intended scope of a universal premise. The setting is a syllogism: 'This is an animal, and every animal moves its lower jaw, therefore this moves its lower jaw.' The opponent denies the major premise by citing a counterexample (crocodile, which moves its upper jaw). The proponent's answer is tahrir al-murad (clarifying the intended meaning): he restricts the premise to well-known animals familiar to the public, excluding rare cases. This is a legitimate response in Islamic dialectic when the opponent exploits an atypical instance. The example uses zoological knowledge familiar to the audience. Note that the proponent does not abandon the premise but refines its scope, preserving the argument's validity for typical cases.

الممنوعة

Clarifying the Madhhab: A Dialectical Move
Author explains the fourth function: clarifying the madhhab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how a mu'allil clarifies his madhhab to defend a premise and identify the dialectical structure in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
A mu'allil can neutralize an objection by specifying the madhhab his premise relies on, not by proving it universally.

In this passage, the author explains the fourth dialectical function: clarifying the scholarly madhhab (madhhab 'ilmi) underlying an objected premise. This is a key move in jadal (dialectics) because it allows the mu'allil to avoid a universal refutation by confining his claim to a specific school. We will analyze the example of flogging and exiling to see how this works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mu'allil's original syllogism
The mu'allil constructs a first-figure categorical syllogism: all additions to the text are abrogation; exiling is an addition; therefore exiling is abrogation. Locate the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion in the passage.
2
Identify the mu'tarid's objection
The mu'tarid (questioner) denies the major premise by narrowing the definition of naskh: only additions that negate or affirm something against the text are naskh; removing original absolution is not. Thus the premise is not universally true.
The objection targets the universality of 'every addition is abrogation'.
3
Analyze the jawab via madhhab clarification
The mu'allil does not defend the premise universally; instead, he clarifies that he built it on the Hanafi madhhab. This restricts the debate to the Hanafi definition of naskh, which includes every addition. So the objection is answered on the mu'allil's own terms.
This is a legitimate jadal move: specifying the doctrinal basis.
4
Assess the dialectical outcome
The mu'allil's response is valid under jadal rules because he has specified the doctrinal basis. The mu'tarid can no longer object that the premise is false in general—only that it is false outside the Hanafi school. Thus the argument is preserved within the dialectical context.
The debate shifts from absolute truth to school-specific truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع من وظائف المعلل في جوابه عن المنع المذكور هو تحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي الذي يبني عليه الممنوع. ومثاله أن يقول المعلل: تغريب الزاني البكر سنة زيادة على قوله تعالى: {فَاجْلِدُوا كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا مِائَةَ جَلْدَةٍ} [النور/2]، وكل زيادة على النص فهي نسخ، له ينتج من الشكل الأول: تغريب الزاني البكر سنة نسخ لقوله فَاجْلِدُوا كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا الآية، ومراده أن الآية متواترة، وأحاديث التغريب أخبار آحاد والمتواتر عنده لا ينسخ بالأحاد، فيُمنع عنده التغريب لذلك. فيقول السائل: أمنع كبرى دليلك هذا وهي قولك: (وكل زيادة على النص نسخ؛ لأن محل ذلك فيما إذا نفت الزيادة شيئًا أثبته النص، أو أثبتت شيئًا نفاه فإن كانت لم تنف ما أثبته النص ولم تثبت ما نفاه فإنها ليست بنسخ؛ لأنها إنما رفعت البراءة الأصلية فقط، ورفعها ليس بنسخ. فيجيب المعلل عن منع كبرى دليله هذا بتحرير مراده من المذهب العلمي الذي بنى عليه الممنوع فيقول: إني بنيت قولي (وكل زيادة على النص فهي نسخ) على مذهب الإمام أبي حنيفة رحمه الله، ولم أبنه على رأي من خالفه. فقد أجاب عن منع كبرى دليله بتحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي الذي بناها عليه.
The fourth function of the mu'allil in his answer to the mentioned objection is clarifying the intended meaning from the scholarly madhhab on which the objected premise is built. An example is that the mu'allil says: Exiling the unmarried fornicator for a year is an addition to His statement - exalted -: 'Flog each one of them a hundred lashes' [An-Nur 24:2], and every addition to the text is abrogation. Therefore it yields from the first figure: Exiling the unmarried fornicator for a year is an abrogation of His statement 'Flog each one of them' the verse. He means that the verse is mutawatir, and the hadiths of exiling are khabar ahad, and mutawatir in his view is not abrogated by ahad, so exiling is prevented in his view due to that. The questioner says: I deny the major premise of this proof, which is your statement 'every addition to the text is abrogation'; because the scope of that is when the addition negates something established by the text, or affirms something denied by it. If it does not negate what the text established nor affirm what it denied, then it is not abrogation; because it only removes the original absolution (bara'a asliyya), and removing it is not abrogation. So the mu'allil answers this denial of his major premise by clarifying his intended meaning from the scholarly madhhab on which he built the objected premise, saying: I built my statement 'every addition to the text is abrogation' on the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa - may Allah have mercy on him - and I did not build it on the opinion of those who disagree with him. Thus he answered the denial of the major premise of his proof by clarifying the intended meaning from the scholarly madhhab on which he built it.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth dialectical function: clarifying the scholarly madhhab behind a premise to answer an objection. The example involves a syllogism on flogging and exiling a fornicator: The mu'allil states that exiling an unmarried fornicator is an addition to the Quranic text of flogging, and according to his premise 'every addition to the text is abrogation', it follows that exiling is an abrogation. But this leads to a conflict because the Quranic verse is mutawatir while the exiling hadith is ahad, and mutawatir cannot be abrogated by ahad. The opponent denies the major premise (all additions are abrogation) by arguing that only additions that negate or affirm something contrary to the text are abrogation; merely lifting a default ruling (bara'a asliyya) is not. The mu'allil's response is to clarify that his premise is not universal but is based on the madhhab of Abu Hanifa, who holds a broader definition of naskh. By doing so, he avoids the absolute rejection of his premise and confines the debate to the Hanafi school. This illustrates how dialectical clarification of doctrinal allegiance can neutralize an objection.

Key Terms
المذهب العلمي
النسخ

Key Takeaway
Clarifying the intended madhhab allows a disputant to defend a premise without proving it universally, by grounding it in a specific scholarly school.

Responding to a Man' by Specifying the Madhhab
Author illustrates how a mu'allil answers an objection by clarifying his madhhab and warns against irrelevant objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a proper response to a denial of a premise (man') by specifying the doctrinal school (madhhab) on which the premise is based, and distinguish proper responses from irrelevant objections.

Core Idea
When your premise is denied, clarify its authority – do not chase irrelevant objections.

In dialectical debate (jadal), when an objector denies a premise (man'), the proponent must answer appropriately. The passage presents a concrete example: a debate about whether the banishment punishment is an abrogation. The objector challenges the major premise, and the proponent responds by specifying his reliance on Imam Abu Hanifa's madhhab. This lesson teaches you that response and warns against four unhelpful diversions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism and the major premise
Find the categorical syllogism in the example. The major premise is 'every addition to the text is an abrogation'. The minor is 'banishment is an addition to the text'. The conclusion is 'banishment is an abrogation'. Understand that the objector attacks the major premise.
2
Recognize the objector's man' and its basis
The objector (sa'il) denies the major premise by arguing that the rule 'every addition is abrogation' only applies when the addition contradicts the text. He gives a specific condition: negation of what the text affirmed or affirmation of what it negated. This is a well-formed man' because it provides a reason.
The objector does not just reject the premise; he gives a rationale based on definition of naskh.
3
Apply the proper response: tahrir al-madhhab
The proponent (mu'allil) does not enter into a general debate about the definition of naskh. Instead, he specifies that his premise is based on the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa, who holds that any addition to a text is naskh, regardless of contradiction. This response defends the premise within its authoritative framework.
This is called 'tahrir al-madhhab al-'ilmi' – clarifying the doctrinal basis.
4
Distinguish proper from improper responses
The author lists four irrelevant responses: correcting the denial's occurrence, denying the conclusive proof, denying the suitability of the sanad, and objecting to the wording's grammar. If the proponent engages in any of these instead of answering the man', he is refuted (ufhima). Recognize these as fallacious diversions.
The proper response is either to prove the premise or to specify its authoritative basis.

ومثاله أن يقول المعلل : تغريب الزاني البكر سنة زيادة على قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ فَاجْلِدُوا كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا مِائَةَ جَلْدَةٍ ﴾ [النور/ ٢]، وكل زيادة على النص فهي نسخ ، فينتج من الشكل الأول : تغريب الزاني البكر سنة نسخ لقوله فَاجْلِدُوا كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا الآية. ومراده أن الآية متواترة، وأحاديث التغريب أخبار آحاد والمتواتر عنده لا ينسخ بالأحاد، فيمنع عنده التغريب لذلك . فيقول السائل : أمنع كبرى دليلك هذا وهي قولك: (وكل زيادة على النص نسخ)؛ لأن محل ذلك فيما إذا نفت الزيادة شيئًا أثبته النص، أو أثبتت شيئًا نفاه فإن كانت لم تنف ما أثبته النص ولم تثبت ما نفاه فإنها ليست بنسخ؛ لأنها إنما رفعت البراءة الأصلية فقط، ورفعها ليس بنسخ . فيجيب المعلل عن منع كبرى دليله هذا بتحرير مراده من المذهب العلمي الذي بنى عليه الممنوع فيقول : إني بنيت قولي (وكل زيادة على النص فهي نسخ) على مذهب الإمام أبي حنيفة ـ رحمه الله، ولم أبنه على رأي من خالفه . فقد أجاب عن منع كبرى دليله بتحرير المراد من المذهب العلمي الذي بناها عليه . فهذه هي أجوبة المعلل عن المنع المجرد عن السند والمقرون به . واعلم أنه لا ينفعه أن يصحح ورود المنع، ولا أن يمنع القطعي، ولا أن يمنع صلاحية السند للاستناد إليه، ولا ينفعه الاشتغال بالاعتراض على عبارة المنع بدعوى أنها مخالفة لقوانين العربية، فإن اشتغل بشيء من ذلك ولم يُجب بأحد الأجوبة التي بينا فقد أُفحم، ووجب انتقال الكلام إلى بحث آخر .
An example is that the proponent says: 'The banishment of the unmarried adulterer for a year is an addition to the saying of Allah the Exalted: "So flog each one of them with one hundred lashes" (Qur'an 24:2). And every addition to the text is an abrogation.' So from the first figure it results: 'The banishment of the unmarried adulterer for a year is an abrogation of His saying "So flog each one of them" etc.' His intention is that the verse is mutawatir, and the hadiths of banishment are ahad reports, and in his view mutawatir is not abrogated by ahad, so banishment is rejected for that reason. Then the objector says: 'I deny the major premise of your proof, which is your saying: "And every addition to the text is an abrogation"; because the applicability of that is when the addition negates something that the text affirmed, or affirms something that the text negated. If it does not negate what the text affirmed nor affirm what it negated, then it is not an abrogation, because it only removes the original permission, and removing it is not abrogation.' Then the proponent answers this denial of his major premise by specifying his intended meaning from the doctrinal school (madhhab) upon which he based the denied statement, saying: 'I built my saying "And every addition to the text is an abrogation" upon the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa—may Allah have mercy on him—and I did not build it upon the opinion of those who disagree with him.' So he answered the denial of his major premise by specifying the intended meaning from the doctrinal school upon which he built it. These are the answers of the proponent to the denial that is stripped of the basis (sanad) and that which is coupled with it. And know that it does not benefit him to correct the occurrence of the denial, nor to deny the definitive proof, nor to deny the suitability of the basis (sanad) for reliance upon it, nor does it benefit him to occupy himself with objecting to the wording of the denial by claiming that it violates the rules of Arabic. For if he occupies himself with something of that and does not answer with one of the answers that we have explained, he is refuted, and it becomes necessary to move the discussion to another topic.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical illustration from the science of jadal (disputation). It presents an example of a mu'allil (proponent) who argues that the banishment of the unmarried adulterer (taghrib) is an abrogation (naskh) of the Quranic flogging verse because it adds a punishment. The mu'allil uses a syllogism in the first figure: the major premise is 'every addition to the text is an abrogation', the minor is 'banishment is an addition', so the conclusion is 'banishment is an abrogation'. The author notes that the mu'allil's reasoning relies on the verse being mutawatir and the banishment hadiths being ahad; in his view, ahad cannot abrogate mutawatir, so banishment is not accepted. The objector (sa'il) then challenges the major premise by specifying that the rule 'every addition is abrogation' only applies when the addition contradicts the text—either negating something affirmed or affirming something negated. If the addition merely removes an original permission (ibaha asliyya), it is not abrogation. The mu'allil responds not by arguing the definition of naskh in general, but by clarifying that his premise is based on the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa, who holds that any addition to a text is naskh. This response is called 'tahrir al-madhhab al-'ilmi' (specifying the doctrinal school). The author then lists three irrelevant responses that do not help the mu'allil: (1) correcting the validity of the denial itself, (2) denying the conclusive proof (qat'i), (3) denying the suitability of the basis (sanad) for reliance, and (4) objecting to the wording of the denial as ungrammatical. If the mu'allil engages in these instead of properly answering the objection, he is refuted (ufhima) and the discussion must move to another topic. This lesson teaches the proper etiquette of jadal: when faced with an objector who denies a premise, the proponent should respond either by providing proof for the premise or by specifying the authoritative basis (madhhab) on which the premise rests, not by diverting to peripheral issues.

Key Terms
المنع
الكبرى
النسخ
تحرير المذهب العلمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example, the objector denies the major premise 'every addition is abrogation'. How does the mu'allil respond?

B) He clarifies that his premise is based on Imam Abu Hanifa's madhhab.
C) He corrects the objector's grammar.
D) He denies the validity of the objector's evidence.

Key Takeaway
The proper way to answer a denial of a premise in jadal is to clarify the authoritative basis (madhhab) of the premise, not to divert to side issues.

Linguistic Objections in Jadal: When Do They Silence the Objector?
Author explains the consequence of objecting to a prohibition phrase with linguistic arguments without proper response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a linguistic objection to a prohibition phrase in jadal is effective and when it results in the objector being silenced.

Core Idea
A linguistic objection without a proper answer backfires: the objector is silenced.

In disputation (jadal), an objector may challenge the proponent's claim by attacking its linguistic formulation. This rule clarifies that such an objection is not automatically effective; it must be supported by a proper answer according to the established methods. If the objector fails to provide that answer, he is considered defeated, and the discussion proceeds to a new topic. This keeps debates focused and prevents frivolous linguistic distractions from derailing substantive argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibition phrase (man‘)
Locate the proponent's claim that something is prohibited or not allowed. This is the target of the objection.
In jadal, the proponent often asserts a ruling using a prohibition phrase.
2
Recognize the linguistic objection
The objector claims the phrasing violates the rules of Arabic (qawānīn al-‘arabiyya). Determine whether the objection is purely linguistic or also substantive.
The objection must be relevant to the validity of the phrase.
3
Recall the expected responses
The author previously explained the proper answers (ajwiba) to such objections. Check if the objector provides one of those answers.
Examples include showing the phrase is formally correct, or that the objection itself is mistaken.
4
Apply the ruling
If the objector engages in the linguistic objection without giving one of the prescribed answers, he is considered silenced (ufhim) and the debate must move to another topic.
This is a procedural rule to maintain discipline in disputation.

However, if the objector pursues this line of objection without providing a proper answer according to the established methods (the answers previously explained by the author), then he is considered silenced (ufhim), meaning his objection is ineffective and the debate proceeds to a different issue. Ghasp: The Cardinal Sin of Dialectic
Author defines ghasp as the questioner's attack on unproven theoretical or obscure intuitive assent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when ghasp occurs in a disputation and explain why it is prohibited by the majority of jadal scholars.

Core Idea
Ghasp is attacking before proof — the questioner's premature argument.

In the discipline of jadal (disputation), not every counter-argument is legitimate. Ghasp is a specific procedural violation where the questioner (sā'il) argues against a claim before the proponent (mu'allil) has had a chance to provide his proof. This passage from the Adab al-Bahth tradition defines ghasp and distinguishes two types of assents that can be targeted: theoretical (naẓarī) and obscure intuitive (badīhī khafī). Understanding ghasp is essential to conducting dialectic fairly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles in the disputation
Note the two main actors: the questioner (sā'il) who objects, and the proponent (mu'allil) who defends a claim. Ghasp is defined from the questioner's perspective.
The passage also mentions 'owner' of the assent; generally the proponent.
2
Determine what the questioner may legitimately do
The questioner may prohibit (man‘) any unproven claim or premise and demand proof. This is not ghasp; it is a right.
Man‘ here is the right to request evidence.
3
Spot when the questioner crosses the line into ghasp
Ghasp occurs when the questioner himself provides a proof of falsity for a claim before the proponent has provided a proof for it. He is not allowed to preemptively argue.
The passage says: 'if the questioner provides a proof of falsity... before the proponent provides a proof, then he is a ghāṣib.'
4
Differentiate the two types of assent targeted
The passage distinguishes theoretical assents (need proof) from obscure intuitive assents (self-evident but unclear, need an alert). Ghasp applies to both, but the proponent's obligation differs slightly: for obscure intuitive, the proponent should first provide a tanbīh (alert).
The shift alert on taṣdīq naẓarī: in mantiq, it means belief requiring proof; here it is used in jadal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الغصب
اعلم أن كل ما صح للسائل أن يمنعه فإن استدلاله عليه من الغصب .
وإيضاحه أنا قد بينا فيما سبق (۱) أن كلاً من المدعى الذي لم عليه المعلل دليلاً، ومقدمة الدليل التي لم يقم عليها دليلاً يجوز للسائل أن يمنعهما، ويطلب الدليل على صحتهما، فإن أقام السائل دليلاً على بطلان واحد منهما قبل أن يقيم المعلل عليه دليلاً فهو غاصب، والغصب وظيفة غير مقبولة عند جماهير أهل هذا الفن. والحاصل أن الغصب هو استدلال السائل على بطلان تصديق نظري لم يقم صاحبه عليه دليلاً، أو استدلاله على بطلان تصديق بديهي خفي لم يقم صاحبه عليه تنبيها .
(۱) راجع ص ۲۰۲.
Chapter on Ghasp (Usurpation). Know that everything that the questioner (sa'il) is allowed to prohibit (man‘), his arguing against it is part of ghasp. Its clarification: We have explained earlier (1) that both the claim (mudda‘ā) for which the proponent (mu‘allil) has not provided a proof, and the premise of the proof for which a proof has not been provided, it is permissible for the questioner to prohibit them and request proof of their correctness. If the questioner provides a proof of the falsity of one of them before the proponent provides a proof of it, then he is a ghāṣib (usurper). Ghasp is a method not accepted by the majority of the practitioners of this art. The summary is that ghasp is the questioner's arguing for the falsity of a theoretical assent (taṣdīq naẓarī) for which its owner has not provided a proof, or his arguing for the falsity of an obscure intuitive assent (taṣdīq badīhī khafī) for which its owner has not provided an alert (tanbīh). (1) Refer to p. 202.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage introduces a key concept in disputation (jadal): ghasp (literally 'usurpation' or 'going beyond bounds'). It defines ghasp as a prohibited tactic where the questioner (sa'il) attacks a claim or premise before the proponent (mu‘allil) has had a chance to defend it with a proof. The author first notes that the questioner is allowed to demand proof for any unproven claim or premise. However, if the questioner himself constructs an argument to prove the falsity of such a claim before the proponent provides his own proof, then that is ghasp, and it is not accepted by most scholars. The passage distinguishes two types of assents that can be targeted in ghasp: theoretical (naẓarī) and obscure intuitive (badīhī khafī). For theoretical assents, the questioner must not argue against them before a proof is given; for obscure intuitive ones, the proponent should have first clarified them with an alert (tanbīh). Failure to observe these rules constitutes ghasp.

The Fallacy of Ghasp: Attacking Before Proof
Defines the fallacy of ghasp where the questioner argues against a claim before proof is provided.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of ghasp in a dialectical exchange and distinguish it from permissible denial.

Core Idea
Ghasp: arguing against a claim before the opponent provides proof.

In adab al-bahth, the rules of debate prohibit the questioner from refuting a claim before the proponent has had a chance to support it. This lesson explains the fallacy called 'ghasp' (usurpation), where the questioner prematurely provides proof of falsity. Understanding ghasp is essential for fair and rigorous dialectical practice in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles in the debate
The passage distinguishes between the proponent (mu'allil) who provides proof and the questioner (sā'il) who challenges. Notice which actions are permitted for each.
2
Understand what the questioner may deny
The questioner can demand proof for the claim itself and for the premises of the proponent's proof. This is not ghasp.
Denial (man') is allowed.
3
Pinpoint the prohibited action
Ghasp occurs when the questioner brings a proof of falsity (rather than just demanding proof) before the proponent provides his own proof. The timing is key.
4
Classify the types of assents involved
The passage mentions two types of assents that can be subject to ghasp: theoretical (taṣdīq naẓarī) and obscure intuitive (taṣdīq badīhī khafī). The latter requires an alert (tanbīh) rather than full proof.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Imagine a debate where the proponent claims 'qiyas is valid'. If the questioner immediately argues 'qiyas is invalid because it is speculative' before the proponent offers any evidence, this is ghasp.
The questioner must wait for the proponent's proof first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن كل ما صح للسائل أن يمنعه فإن استدلاله عليه من الغصب. وإيضاحه أنا قد بينا فيما سبق أن كلاً من المدعى الذي لم يقم عليه المعلل دليلاً، ومقدمة الدليل التي لم يقم عليها دليلاً يجوز للسائل أن يمنعهما، ويطلب الدليل على صحتهما، فإن أقام السائل دليلاً على بطلان واحد منهما قبل أن يقيم المعلل عليه دليلاً فهو غاصب، والغصب وظيفة غير مقبولة عند جماهير أهل هذا الفن. والحاصل أن الغصب هو استدلال السائل على بطلان تصديق نظري لم يقم صاحبه عليه دليلاً، أو استدلاله على بطلان تصديق بديهي خفي لم يقم صاحبه عليه تنبيها.
Know that anything which it is valid for the questioner to deny, his argument against it is ghasp. Its clarification: we have already explained previously that both the claim for which the proponent has not provided a proof, and a premise of the proof for which he has not provided a proof, it is permissible for the questioner to deny them and demand proof of their correctness. If the questioner brings a proof of the falsity of one of them before the proponent brings a proof for it, then he is a ghāṣib (usurper). Ghasp is an unacceptable function according to the majority of the people of this art. The upshot is that ghasp is the questioner's argument for the falsity of a theoretical assent for which its owner has not provided a proof, or his argument for the falsity of an obscure intuitive assent for which its owner has not provided an alert.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage defines the fallacy of ghasp (usurpation) in dialectical debate. The author begins by stating a general principle: anything that the questioner can validly deny, his argument against it falls under ghasp. He clarifies by recalling earlier discussion: the questioner is allowed to demand proof for two things: the claim itself if the proponent hasn't proven it, and the premises of the proponent's proof if they haven't been proven themselves. However, if the questioner goes beyond demanding proof and actually argues for the falsity of either before the proponent provides his own proof, then that action is called ghasp. The author emphasizes that ghasp is an unacceptable tactic according to most scholars in this field. Finally, he gives a concise definition: ghasp is the questioner's proof of the falsity of a theoretical assent that lacks a proof, or of an obscure intuitive assent that lacks an alert (tanbīh). The passage is essentially a rule of debate etiquette—it prohibits attacking an opponent's position before the opponent has had a chance to substantiate it.

Mukabara: Identifying Obstinate Denial in Debate
Defines mukabara with examples and ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify instances of mukabara in dialectical exchanges and distinguish them from legitimate objections.

Core Idea
Mukabara is denying what is obviously true or proven just to win.

In debating, not all denials are equal. Some are based on genuine doubt; others are pure obstinacy. This lesson teaches you how to spot mukabara—when an opponent denies a self-evident truth or a proven claim merely to dominate the discussion. We'll analyze the definition and examples from the classical text.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Mukabara is disputation not aimed at truth but at superiority and victory. This is the key criterion.
The author explicitly states this in the opening line.
2
Review the examples
The proponent states obvious truths: whole greater than part, one is half of two, four is even. The questioner denies them. That constitutes mukabara.
These are self-evident propositions; denying them is irrational.
3
Apply the test
Ask: Is the claim self-evident or supported by sound proof? If yes, denying it without counterargument is mukabara.
Mukabara includes denying a theoretical assent that has been proven with a sound, irrefutable proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المكابرة وهي في الاصطلاح المنازعة بين الخصمين لا لإظهار الصواب بل لإظهار الفضل والغلبة. ومن أمثلتها أن يقول المعلل صاحب التصديق: الكل أكبر من الجزء، والواحد نصف الاثنين، والأربعة زوج. فيقول السائل : أمنعُ هذه الدعاوى أو واحدة منها، فإن قال ذلك فهو مكابر، والمكابرة وظيفة مردودة ، لا تُسمع ولا تقبل كما لا يخفى . ومن المكابرة منع التصديق النظري الذي أقام المعلل عليه دليلاً صحيحًا لا يمكن تطرق الخلل إليه بوجه من الوجوه . وقد بينا سابقا أن التصديق النظري المجرد عن الدليل والتصديق البديهي الخفي المجرد عن التنبيه لا يجوز الاعتراض على واحد منهما إلا بالمنع فقط، وأن التصديق النظري المصحوب بالدليل من المعلل، والتصديق البديهي الخفي المصحوب بالتنبيه يجوز الاعتراض على كل واحد منهما بثلاث طرق كلها يصح الاعتراض بها وهي: ١ - المناقضة، وتسمى بالنقض التفصيلي، والمنع الحقيقي مقدمة معينة من مقدمات الدليل، وهذه الطريق والممانعة، وهي منع هي التي قدمنا الكلام عليها مستوفى وأوجه الجواب عن المنع فيها .
Chapter on Obstinacy. It is, in terminology, disputation between two adversaries not to manifest the truth but to manifest superiority and victory. Among its examples: The proponent, the owner of the assent, says: 'The whole is greater than the part, one is half of two, and four is even.' Then the questioner says: 'I deny these claims or one of them.' If he says that, then he is an obstinate person (mukābir). Obstinacy is a rejected function, not listened to nor accepted, as is not hidden. Among obstinacy is the denial of a theoretical assent for which the proponent has provided a sound proof that cannot be exposed to defect in any way. And we have explained previously that a theoretical assent stripped of proof, and an obscure intuitive assent stripped of alert, it is not permissible to object to either of them except by denial only. And that a theoretical assent accompanied by proof from the proponent, and an obscure intuitive assent accompanied by alert, it is permissible to object to each of them in three ways, all of which are valid objections. They are: 1. Al-Munāqaḍa (contradiction), called al-Naqḍ al-Tafṣīlī (detailed refutation), and al-Man' al-Ḥaqīqī (true denial) of a specific premise from the premises of the proof. This way and al-Mumāna'a (prevention) which is denial... (text incomplete).

Commentary
This passage defines mukabara (obstinacy) as a type of fallacy in dialectical debate. The author begins by giving the technical definition: it is a dispute aimed not at revealing the truth but at showing off and winning. Then he provides clear examples: a proponent states self-evident truths like 'the whole is greater than the part', and the questioner denies such claims. That denial constitutes mukabara. The author declares this tactic 'rejected'—it is ignored and dismissed. The definition is extended to also include denying a theoretical assent that has been proven with a sound, irrefutable proof. So mukabara is essentially denial of what is clearly true or proven. The passage then recalls an earlier distinction: theoretical assents without proof and obscure intuitive assents without alert can only be objected to by simple denial (man'). But theoretical assents with proof and obscure intuitive assents with alert can be objected to in three ways. The text begins to enumerate them but cuts off. The first way mentioned is al-Munāqaḍa (contradiction) which is also called detailed refutation or true denial of a specific premise. The text mentions al-Mumāna'a (prevention) but does not complete the list. The passage serves to identify unacceptable debate behavior (mukabara) and also remind the reader of the permissible methods of objection.

Mukabara is the fallacy of denying the undeniable just to win.

Three Valid Objections in Dialectic: Contradiction, Denial, and Prevention
Lists permissible objection methods against theoretical assents with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the three permissible methods of objection (munāqaḍa, man', mumāna'a) as described in the text.

Core Idea
Theoretical assents with proof can be challenged in three specific ways; denial alone is not enough.

Building on the previous lesson, we now explore the legitimate ways to object to a claim in debate. The author distinguishes between what can be objected to and how. This lesson unpacks the three methods: detailed refutation (munāqaḍa), true denial (man' haqīqī), and prevention (mumāna'a). We'll focus on what the text says.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of assents
The author distinguishes theoretical assents stripped of proof vs. with proof, and obscure intuitive assents with/without alert. Only certain objects can be challenged.
Assents without proof/alert can only be denied; those with proof/alert allow three methods.
2
Know the objection rules
For assents without proof/alert, only simple denial (man') is allowed. For assents with proof/alert, three methods are permissible.
This is a key rule from the text.
3
Learn the three methods
The text lists: 1) al-Munāqaḍa (detailed refutation), also called true denial of a specific premise; 2) al-Man' al-Ḥaqīqī; 3) al-Mumāna'a (prevention). The text begins to elaborate but is cut off.
Focus on the first method as defined: pointing out a contradiction in the proof or denying a specific premise.

Definition of al-Mukabara and the Three Methods of Objection
Author defines obstinate disputation and outlines three ways to object to a demonstrative statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-mukābarah and classify the three permissible methods of objection to a demonstrative proof.

Core Idea
Obstinate disputation rejects truth; valid objections follow specific methods.

In this passage, the author defines what makes a debate obstinate (mukābarah) and distinguishes it from legitimate objection. He then classifies the types of theoretical assent and outlines three valid ways to object to a proof. Understanding this distinction is crucial for engaging in productive theological and juristic debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of al-mukābarah
Find the phrase where the author defines obstinate disputation: it is a dispute aimed not at truth but at superiority.
2
Recognize an example of mukābarah
The author gives the example of denying obvious truths like 'the whole is greater than the part'. Understand why this is rejected.
3
Distinguish types of theoretical assent
The author distinguishes between theoretical assent without proof (can only be denied) and theoretical assent with proof (can be objected to in three ways).
This classification determines which objection methods are permissible.
4
List the three permissible methods
Identify the three methods: 1) al-munāqaḍah (detailed refutation), 2) al-naqḍ (refutation), 3) al-muʿāraḍah (counter-argument).
The first method has already been discussed; the other two will be explained later.

فصل في المكابرة
وهي في الاصطلاح المنازعة بين الخصمين لا لإظهار الصواب بل لإظهار الفضل والغلبة . ومن أمثلتها أن يقول المعلل صاحب التصديق: الكل أكبر من الجزء، والواحد نصف الاثنين، والأربعة زوج. فيقول السائل : أمنعُ هذه الدعاوى أو واحدة منها، فإن قال ذلك فهو مكابر، والمكابرة وظيفة مردودة ، لا تُسمع ولا تقبل كما لا يخفى . ومن المكابرة منع التصديق النظري الذي أقام المعلل عليه دليلاً صحيحًا لا يمكن تطرق الخلل إليه بوجه من الوجوه . وقد بينا سابقا (١) أن التصديق النظري المجرد عن الدليل والتصديق البديهي الخفي المجرد عن التنبيه لا يجوز الاعتراض على واحد منهما إلا بالمنع فقط، وأن التصديق النظري المصحوب بالدليل من المعلل، والتصديق البديهي الخفي المصحوب بالتنبيه يجوز الاعتراض على كل واحد منهما بثلاث طرق كلها يصح الاعتراض بها وهي: ١ - المناقضة، وتسمى بالنقض التفصيلي، والمنع الحقيقي مقدمة معينة من مقدمات الدليل، وهذه الطريق والممانعة، وهي منع هي التي قدمنا الكلام عليها مستوفى وأوجه الجواب عن المنع فيها . Section on obstinate disputation. It is, in terminology, the disputation between two opponents not to manifest truth but to manifest superiority and victory. Among its examples: the demonstrator, the possessor of assent, says: 'The whole is greater than the part, one is half of two, four is even.' Then the questioner says: 'I deny these claims or one of them.' If he says that, then he is an obstinate disputer, and obstinate disputation is a rejected function; it is neither listened to nor accepted, as is not hidden. And of obstinate disputation is the denial of a theoretical assent for which the demonstrator has established a sound proof that cannot be undermined in any way. And we have previously explained (1) that purely theoretical assent devoid of proof, and hidden self-evident assent devoid of indication, it is not permissible to object to either of them except by denial only; and that theoretical assent accompanied by proof from the demonstrator, and hidden self-evident assent accompanied by indication, it is permissible to object to each of them by three ways all of which are valid for objection. They are: 1) Contradiction, also called detailed refutation, and genuine denial of a specific premise from the premises of the proof. This way and prevention, which is denial, is the one we have already spoken about exhaustively, including the ways of answering the denial in it. (1) See pp. 201, 202. 2) The second way: Refutation. 3) The third way: Counter-argument. And we will clarify, God willing, the discussion of these two ways as we clarified the first which is contradiction.

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of al-mukābarah (obstinate disputation) and classifies the permissible methods of objection in dialectical debate. The author first states that mukābarah is a dispute aimed not at truth but at superiority. Examples include denying obvious truths like 'the whole is greater than the part'. Such denial is rejected. The author then distinguishes two types of theoretical assent: one without proof (which can only be denied) and one with proof (which can be objected to in three ways). These three ways are: (1) al-munāqaḍah (contradiction/detailed refutation) and al-mumānaʿah (prevention) – already discussed; (2) al-naqḍ (refutation); and (3) al-muʿāraḍah (counter-argument) – to be discussed. The passage sets the stage for the detailed exposition of these objection techniques in dialectic.

Definition of Naqd (Refutation) in Dialectical Debate
Author defines naqd in dialectical debate and lists its two types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define naqd in adab al-bahth, identify its two types, and explain why a shahid is required.

Core Idea
A claim of invalidity without evidence is not a valid naqd.

In dialectical debate, simply denying the respondent's proof is not enough. The disputant must demonstrate why the proof fails, either by showing the conclusion does not follow or that the proof leads to absurdity. This is naqd, a structured refutation that must be supported by its own evidence (shahid).

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of naqd
The author defines naqd as the disputant's claim that the respondent's proof is invalid, but this claim must be backed by evidence. Note that naqd is not just denial; it is a reasoned objection.
The term 'naqd' here is used in the dialectical tradition of adab al-bahth.
2
Identify the two grounds for naqd
The author lists two ways to show invalidity: (1) the implication is absent despite the evidence (takhalluf al-madlul 'an al-dalil), meaning the evidence does not lead to the claimed conclusion; (2) the evidence entails an impossibility (istilzam al-muhal), such as circular reasoning or infinite regress.
These correspond to the two types of shahid.
3
Recognize the requirement of shahid
The author emphasizes that naqd is not accepted unless accompanied by a shahid, which is the evidence for the invalidity. Without a shahid, the naqd is dismissed.
The shahid itself is of the same two types.
4
Apply the concept to the philosophers' example
The author begins an example: a respondent who believes in the eternity of the world claims 'The world is eternal' and adduces evidence. A naqd would require the disputant to either show that the evidence does not imply eternity or that it leads to an impossibility. Although the example is cut off, you can practise completing it.
Consider what evidence the philosophers might use and how a naqd could be raised.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في النقض وأقسامه ويسمى النقض الإجمالي
اعلم أولاً أن النقض في اصطلاح أهل هذا الفن هو ادعاء السائل بطلان دليل المعلل مع إقامته الدليل على دعواه بطلانه، وذلك إما بتخلف المدلول عن الدليل، بمعنى أن الدليل يكون موجودًا والمدلول ليس بموجود، فيكون الدليل جاريًا على مدعي آخر غيرِ المدعى الذي أقامه عليه المعلَّل، أو بسبب استلزامه المحال كالدور السبقي والتسلسل المحال ونحو ذلك، ولهم فيه تعاريف غير هذا، وسيتضح لك ذلك في الكلام عليه في تطبيقه على القادح المسمى بالنقض عند أهل الأصول.
واعلم أن النقض لا يُقبل إلا مقترنا بشاهد، والمراد بالشاهد المذكور هو الدليلُ على صحة النقض، فإن لم يذكر السائل مع النقض الشاهد المذكور لم يقبل منه، وقد عرفت من تعريفه أن الشاهد المذكور نوعان:
الأول: تخلف المدلول عن الدليل والثاني استلزامه المحال.
فمثال تخلف المدلول عن الدليل أن يقول المعلَّل المعتقد مذهب الفلاسفة في قدم العالم: العالم قديم، ثم يقيم على ذلك الدليل في
Chapter on Naqd (Refutation) and its divisions, and it is called the comprehensive refutation. Know first that naqd in the terminology of the practitioners of this art is the disputant's claim of the invalidity of the respondent's proof while he presents evidence for his claim of its invalidity. That is either by the absence of the implication from the evidence, meaning that the evidence exists but the implication does not exist, so the evidence applies to another claim different from the claim for which the respondent adduced it; or due to its entailing an impossibility, such as circular priority, infinite regress, and the like. They have other definitions for it, and this will become clear to you in the discussion of it when applied to the defect called naqd according to the scholars of Usul. Know that naqd is not accepted unless accompanied by a witness, and the intended meaning of the mentioned witness is the evidence for the validity of the naqd. For if the disputant does not mention with the naqd the mentioned witness, it is not accepted from him. You have already known from its definition that the mentioned witness is of two types: first: the absence of the implication from the evidence; second: its entailing an impossibility. An example of the absence of the implication from the evidence is: the respondent who believes in the philosophers' doctrine of the eternity of the world says: The world is eternal, then he adduces evidence for it by...
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term 'al-naqd' as used in the dialectical art of adab al-bahth. The author first states that naqd is the disputant's claim that the respondent's proof is invalid, and this claim must be supported by its own evidence. The author then explains two ways to show invalidity: either by pointing out that the evidence does not lead to the claimed implication (the implication is absent despite the evidence being present), or by showing that the evidence leads to an impossibility like circular reasoning or infinite regress. The author notes that there are other definitions but promises clarity later. Next, the author emphasizes that naqd is only accepted if accompanied by a 'shahid' (witness), which is the evidence proving the invalidity. The shahid is of two types corresponding to the two ways mentioned. The author begins an example from the philosophers' claim that the world is eternal, but the text cuts off before completing the example. In Islamic jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh), naqd is a common tool to invalidate a qiyas by showing that the effective cause ('illa) is not present in a parallel case, or that the analogy leads to an absurd result.

Key Terms
النقض
الشاهد
تخلف المدلول عن الدليل
استلزام المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The naqd must be based on a known text from Quran or Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
Naqd is a structured rebuttal requiring evidence, and it operates either by showing the conclusion does not follow or the proof leads to absurdity.

The Definition and Condition of Naqd in Jadal
Author defines naqd and its condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define naqd in jadal and explain why it must be accompanied by a shāhid.

Core Idea
Naqd is not mere denial; it requires evidence (shāhid) to be accepted.

In dialectical debate, a refutation must be supported by proof. This lesson establishes the technical definition of naqd and its essential condition: the refuter must provide a shāhid (evidence) to demonstrate invalidity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the technical definition of naqd
Find where the author defines naqd. It is the claim of invalidity of the prover's argument, but it must be supported by evidence.
The author uses the phrase 'ادعاء السائل بطلان دليل المعلل مع إقامته الدليل على دعواه بطلانه'.
2
Locate the condition for acceptance
The passage states that naqd is not accepted unless accompanied by a shāhid. Note the word 'لا يقبل' indicating a strict requirement.
Shāhid is the evidence that proves the invalidity.
3
Distinguish naqd from simple denial
A simple denial without evidence is not a valid naqd in jadal. The shāhid is what transforms a mere objection into a legitimate refutation.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the dialectical method.

فصل في النقض وأقسامه ويسمى النقض الإجمالي
اعلم أولاً أن النقض في اصطلاح أهل هذا الفن هو ادعاء السائل بطلان دليل المعلل مع إقامته الدليل على دعواه بطلانه، وذلك إما بتخلف المدلول عن الدليل، بمعنى أن الدليل يكون موجودًا والمدلول ليس بموجود، فيكون الدليل جاريًا على مدعي آخر غيرِ المدعى الذي أقامه عليه المعلَّل، أو بسبب استلزامه المحال كالدور السبقي والتسلسل المحال ونحو ذلك، ولهم فيه تعاريف غير هذا، وسيتضح لك ذلك في الكلام عليه في تطبيقه على القادح المسمى بالنقض عند أهل الأصول (١) .
واعلم أن النقض لا يُقبل إلا مقترنا بشاهد، والمراد بالشاهد المذكور هو الدليلُ على صحة النقض، فإن لم يذكر السائل مع النقض الشاهد المذكور لم يقبل منه ، وقد عرفت من تعريفه أن الشاهد المذكور نوعان :
الأول : تخلف المدلول عن الدليل والثاني استلزامه المحال .
•
فمثال تخلف المدلول عن الدليل أن يقول المعلَّل المعتقد مذهب الفلاسفة في قدم العالم : العالم قديم، ثم يقيم على ذلك الدليل في زعمه فيقول : لأنه أثر القديم، وكل ماهو أثر القديم فهو قديم، ينتج من الشكل الأول : هو - أي العالم - قديم .
فيقول السائل : هذا الدليل باطل ؛ لأنه يوجد مع عدم وجود مدلوله، وقد يوجد مع مدعى آخر مقطوع بأنه غير حق، كأن يقول : الحوادث اليومية المتجددة في الدنيا حينًا بعد حين أثر القديم، وكل ماهو أثر القديم فهو قديم ينتج من الشكل الأول : الحوادث اليومية المتجددة في الدنيا حينًا بعد حين قديمة، مع أن حدوثها مشاهد مدرك بالحواس، فهو يقيني ومقدمة الدليل المذكور الصغرى ـ وهي كونها أثر القديم - صحيحة، فتعين أن قوله وكل ماهو أثر القديم فهو قديم) باطل ؛ لأنه منقوض بالحوادث اليومية المتجددة بمرأى منا ومسمع (١)
ولا يلزم في النقض تعيين المقدمة التي منها الفساد، وإنما عيناها في هذا المثال لأجل الإيضاح .
والحاصل أنه يقول له : دليلك الذي هو قولك وكل ما هو أثر القديم فهو قديم منقوض ؛ لأن الحوادث اليومية أثر القديم، وليست بقديمة .
وإيضاح الإبطال بهذا النوع من النقض أن المدلول لازم للدليل
وتخلفُ اللازم عن الملزوم لا يمكن فلا يكون تخلف المدلول عن الدليل إلا لفساد فيه، وسيأتي (٢) إن شاء الله ـ تعالى ـ كثير من أمثلة هذا
Chapter on al-Naqd and its divisions, and it is called al-Naqd al-Ijmālī. Know first that naqd in the terminology of the people of this art is the claim of the questioner of the invalidity of the argument of the prover, while he provides evidence for his claim of its invalidity. That is either by the non-following of the conclusion from the evidence, meaning that the evidence exists while the conclusion does not exist, so the evidence applies to another claim other than the claim for which the prover brought it, or due to its implying an impossibility such as prior circularity, infinite regress, and the like. They have other definitions for it, and that will become clear to you in the discussion on it when applied to the objection called naqd by the scholars of uṣūl. Know that naqd is not accepted unless accompanied by a shāhid, and what is meant by the mentioned shāhid is the evidence for the validity of the naqd. So if the questioner does not mention with the naqd the mentioned shāhid, it is not accepted from him. You have already known from its definition that the mentioned shāhid is of two types: The first: the non-following of the conclusion from the evidence, and the second: its implying impossibility. An example of the non-following of the conclusion from the evidence is that the prover who believes in the school of the philosophers regarding the eternity of the world says: The world is eternal, then he provides evidence for that in his claim saying: Because it is the effect of the Eternal, and everything that is the effect of the Eternal is eternal. It follows from the first figure: It – i.e., the world – is eternal. The questioner says: This evidence is invalid because it exists while its conclusion does not exist, and it may exist with another claim known to be untrue, as he says: The daily events that occur in the world from time to time are the effect of the Eternal, and everything that is the effect of the Eternal is eternal. It follows from the first figure: The daily events that occur in the world from time to time are eternal, while their occurrence is observed and perceived by the senses; it is certain. And the minor premise of the mentioned evidence – that they are the effect of the Eternal – is correct, so it is established that his statement 'And everything that is the effect of the Eternal is eternal' is invalid because it is refuted by the daily events occurring in our sight and hearing. It is not necessary in naqd to specify the premise from which the corruption arises, but we specified it in this example for clarity. The conclusion is that he says to him: Your evidence, which is your statement 'Everything that is the effect of the Eternal is eternal,' is refuted because daily events are the effect of the Eternal and are not eternal. The explanation of invalidation with this type of naqd is that the conclusion is a necessary concomitant of the evidence, and the non-existence of the concomitant with the existence of the concomitant cannot occur, so the non-following of the conclusion from the evidence only occurs due to corruption in it. And many examples of this will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of naqd (refutation) in the context of dialectical debate (jadal). It opens by stating that naqd is the claim of invalidity of an opponent's argument, but it must be supported by a shāhid (evidence) that proves the invalidity. The author then identifies two types of shāhid: (1) the conclusion does not follow from the premises (takhalluf al-madlūl 'an al-dalīl), and (2) the premises imply an impossibility (istilzām al-muhāl). To illustrate the first type, a classic kalām example is given: A philosopher argues for the eternity of the world using the syllogism: 'The world is the effect of the Eternal. Everything that is the effect of the Eternal is eternal. Therefore, the world is eternal.' The refuter counters by pointing out that daily events (like rain, earthquakes) are also effects of the Eternal, yet they are observed to be temporally originated (ḥādith). Since the minor premise (they are effects of the Eternal) is true, the major premise ('Every effect of the Eternal is eternal') must be false, because it would lead to the false conclusion that daily events are eternal. The refuter does not need to specify which premise is flawed, but does so here for clarity. The passage concludes by explaining the underlying logic: if the conclusion were truly a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the evidence, it cannot fail to exist when the evidence exists; thus, its failure indicates corruption in the evidence itself.

Key Terms
النقض
الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for a naqd (refutation) to be accepted in jadal?
A) The prover must admit his mistake.
B) The refuter must provide a shāhid (evidence) for his claim of invalidity.
C) The refuter must specify which premise is flawed.
D) The argument must be from the first figure.

Key Takeaway
A valid naqd in jadal must be supported by a shāhid that demonstrates the invalidity, not merely a bare denial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Explain why the following statement is not a valid naqd: 'Your argument for the eternity of the world is invalid because I disagree with it.' What crucial element is missing?
?
In a fīqh debate, one jurist says: 'Qiyās on the prohibition of wine applies to nabīdh because both are intoxicants.' The refuter replies: 'That is invalid.' Is this a valid naqd? Why or why not?

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
7 min
Two Types of Shāhid: Takhalluf al-Madlūl and Istilzām al-Muhāl
Author explains the two types of shāhid with an example from kalām.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two types of shāhid and apply them to refute a syllogistic argument in kalām.

Core Idea
A refutation succeeds by showing either that the conclusion doesn't follow or that the argument leads to absurdity.

The author identifies two concrete ways to prove an argument invalid: (1) the conclusion does not follow from the premises (takhalluf al-madlūl), and (2) the premises imply an impossibility (istilzām al-muhāl). This lesson unpacks the first type using a classic kalām example about the eternity of the world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of shāhid
The passage explicitly lists two types: first, the conclusion not following from the evidence; second, the implication of impossibility.
These are the only accepted grounds for a naqd.
2
Analyze the example of takhalluf al-madlūl
The prover (philosopher) argues: 'The world is the effect of the Eternal; every effect of the Eternal is eternal; therefore the world is eternal.' The refuter counters with a counterexample: daily events are also effects of the Eternal but are observed to be temporal, not eternal. Thus, the major premise fails.
The refuter does not need to specify which premise is flawed, but does so for clarity.
3
Understand the underlying logic
If the conclusion were a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the premises, it could not fail to hold. Its failure indicates corruption in the evidence itself.
This is the logical basis for takhalluf al-madlūl.
4
Apply to a new case
Consider another syllogism from kalām: 'The Qur'an is composed of letters; everything composed is created; therefore the Qur'an is created.' Construct a naqd using takhalluf al-madlūl.
Try to find a counterexample where the premises hold but the conclusion is false (e.g., the divine speech is composed but not created in the view of some theologians).
