Commentary
This passage defines the concept of naqd (refutation) in the context of dialectical debate (jadal). It opens by stating that naqd is the claim of invalidity of an opponent's argument, but it must be supported by a shāhid (evidence) that proves the invalidity. The author then identifies two types of shāhid: (1) the conclusion does not follow from the premises (takhalluf al-madlūl 'an al-dalīl), and (2) the premises imply an impossibility (istilzām al-muhāl). To illustrate the first type, a classic kalām example is given: A philosopher argues for the eternity of the world using the syllogism: 'The world is the effect of the Eternal. Everything that is the effect of the Eternal is eternal. Therefore, the world is eternal.' The refuter counters by pointing out that daily events (like rain, earthquakes) are also effects of the Eternal, yet they are observed to be temporally originated (ḥādith). Since the minor premise (they are effects of the Eternal) is true, the major premise ('Every effect of the Eternal is eternal') must be false, because it would lead to the false conclusion that daily events are eternal. The refuter does not need to specify which premise is flawed, but does so here for clarity. The passage concludes by explaining the underlying logic: if the conclusion were truly a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the evidence, it cannot fail to exist when the evidence exists; thus, its failure indicates corruption in the evidence itself.

Checkpoint
In the example of the philosopher's argument, what did the refuter use as a shāhid to invalidate the major premise?
A) The world is observed to be temporal.
B) Daily events are effects of the Eternal but are not eternal.
C) The syllogism is not in the first figure.
D) The philosopher did not provide evidence.

Key Takeaway
The first type of shāhid for naqd, takhalluf al-madlūl, demonstrates invalidity by showing that the premises are true but the conclusion is false, usually via a counterexample.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which type of shāhid (takhalluf al-madlūl or istilzām al-muhāl) is used in the example of daily events refuting the philosopher's argument. Then, construct a similar naqd against the following argument: 'The Qur'an is composed of letters; everything composed is created; therefore the Qur'an is created.' (Assume the refuter holds that the Qur'an is uncreated.)
?
In a fīqh debate, a prover argues: 'Selling dates before they ripen is invalid because it is a sale of non-existent items. Every sale of non-existent items is invalid. Therefore, selling dates before they ripen is invalid.' Provide a naqd using takhalluf al-madlūl, citing a valid sale of non-existent items (e.g., salam contract).

The First Type of Naqd: Direct Counterexample
Illustration of the first type of naqd using the eternal world example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the first type of naqd (direct counterexample) to invalidate an opponent's syllogism in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
If your opponent's proof can be applied to a clear counterexample that produces a false conclusion, the proof is unsound.

In this passage, the author illustrates the first method of refutation (naqd): taking the opponent's own syllogism and applying it to a case that yields an obviously false result. This shows that at least one premise must be false. The example involves a kalām argument for the world's eternity being refuted by the observable occurrence of transient events.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's syllogism
Find the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion in the opponent's argument. In the example, the major premise is 'Everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal', minor is 'The world is an effect of the Eternal', conclusion 'The world is eternal'.
2
Find a parallel case
Choose a case that shares the minor premise's middle term (e.g., daily events as effects of the Eternal) but yields a clearly false conclusion (e.g., 'daily events are eternal', which contradicts observation).
3
Show the contradiction
Demonstrate that the opponent's major premise leads to a false conclusion when applied to the parallel case. Since the conclusion is necessary from the premises, the false conclusion indicates a false premise.
The refutation does not require specifying which premise is flawed; it suffices that the conclusion fails.

زعمه فيقول : لأنه أثر القديم، وكل ما هو أثر القديم فهو قديم، ينتج من الشكل الأول : هو - أي العالم - قديم . فيقول السائل : هذا الدليل باطل ؛ لأنه يوجد مع عدم وجود مدلوله، وقد يوجد مع مدعى آخر مقطوع بأنه غير حق، كأن يقول : الحوادث اليومية المتجددة في الدنيا حينًا بعد حين أثر القديم، وكل ما هو أثر القديم فهو قديم ينتج من الشكل الأول : الحوادث اليومية المتجددة في الدنيا حينًا بعد حين قديمة، مع أن حدوثها مشاهد مدرك بالحواس، فهو يقيني ومقدمة الدليل المذكور الصغرى ـ وهي كونها أثر القديم - صحيحة، فتعين أن قوله وكل ما هو أثر القديم فهو قديم) باطل ؛ لأنه منقوض بالحوادث اليومية المتجددة بمرأى منا ومسمع ولا يلزم في النقض تعيين المقدمة التي منها الفساد، وإنما عيناها في هذا المثال لأجل الإيضاح . والحاصل أنه يقول له : دليلك الذي هو قولك وكل ما هو أثر القديم فهو قديم منقوض ؛ لأن الحوادث اليومية أثر القديم، وليست بقديمة . وإيضاح الإبطال بهذا النوع من النقض أن المدلول لازم للدليل وتخلفُ اللازم عن الملزوم لا يمكن فلا يكون تخلف المدلول عن الدليل إلا لفساد فيه، وسيأتي إن شاء الله ـ تعالى ـ كثير من أمثلة هذا النوع من النقض في تطبيقه على القادح المعروف في الأصول بالنقض . ومثال النوع الثاني من نوعي النقض وهو استلزام دليل المعلل المحال : فقد مثل له بعضهم بأن يقول المعلل : الحد له تعريف، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه تعريف، وكل تعريف له تعريف، ينتج من الشكل الأول : الحد له تعريف . فيقول السائل هذا الدليل منقوض؛ لاستلزامه المحال وهو التسلسل إلى غير نهاية ؛ لأن قولك وكل تعريف له تعريف يقتضي أنه كلما أتى بتعريف لزمه تعريفه بتعريف آخر إلى غير نهاية، وهذا محال . ووجه النقض باستلزامه المحال أن الأمور المتحققة في الواقع لا تستلزم المحال فاستلزام الدليل للمحال لا يكون إلا لعدم صحته في الواقع . ووجه استحالة التسلسل فيما ذكر أن كل تعريف من حيث هو معرَّف - باسم المفعول - محتاج إلى تعريف، وهذا محتاج أيضًا إليه إلى ما لا نهاية، فلا تعقل معرفة تعريف منها بحال . ومثل بعضهم لاستحالته بالدور السبقي بأن يقول المعلل : الإنسان ابن بشر وكل ابن بشر بشر، ينتج : الإنسان بشر . فيقول السائل : هذا منقوض باستلزامه المحال ؛ لأن لفظة (بشر) في الصغرى يتوقف إدراكها على لفظة ( بشر ) في الكبرى، كعكسه
His claim: he says: because it is the effect of the Eternal, and everything that is the effect of the Eternal is eternal, produces from the first figure: it—i.e., the world—is eternal. So the questioner says: This proof is invalid because it exists when its implication does not exist, and it may exist alongside another claim that is categorically known to be false, such as if he says: The daily events that renew in the world from time to time are the effect of the Eternal, and everything that is the effect of the Eternal is eternal, produces from the first figure: The daily events that renew in the world from time to time are eternal, despite the fact that their occurrence is observed and perceived by the senses, so it is certain, and the minor premise of the mentioned proof—namely that they are the effect of the Eternal—is correct, so it is determined that his statement 'and everything that is the effect of the Eternal is eternal' is false because it is refuted by the daily renewing events that are within our sight and hearing. And in refutation (naqd) it is not necessary to specify the premise from which the corruption comes, but we specified them in this example for clarification. The upshot is that he says to him: Your proof, which is your statement 'everything that is the effect of the Eternal is eternal,' is refuted because the daily events are the effect of the Eternal and are not eternal. And the clarification of invalidation by this type of refutation is that the implication (madlul) is necessary for the proof, and the separation of the necessary from the necessitating is impossible, so the separation of the implication from the proof can only be due to corruption in it. And there will come, God willing, many examples of this type of refutation in its application to the well-known objection in usul called 'naqd'. And an example of the second type of the two types of refutation, which is that the proof of the proponent necessitates absurdity: some gave an example: the proponent says: 'The definition (hadd) has a definition,' then establishes proof for that by saying: because it is a definition, and every definition has a definition, produces from the first figure: The definition has a definition. So the questioner says: This proof is refuted because it necessitates absurdity, namely infinite regress; because your statement 'every definition has a definition' entails that whenever a definition is brought, its definition necessitates another definition ad infinitum, and this is absurd. And the aspect of refutation by necessitating absurdity is that things that actually exist do not necessitate absurdity, so the proof's necessitation of absurdity can only be due to its lack of correctness in reality. And the aspect of the impossibility of regress in what was mentioned is that every definition insofar as it is a definiendum (ism al-maf'ul) needs a definition, and this also needs it ad infinitum, so no knowledge of any definition can be conceived. And some gave an example of its impossibility by the priority (sabqi) circularity: the proponent says: 'The human is the son of a human (bashar), and every son of a human is human, produces: The human is human.' So the questioner says: This is refuted because it necessitates absurdity; because the word 'human' (bashar) in the minor premise depends for its comprehension on the word 'human' in the major premise, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of two types of counterexample (naqd) used in dialectical disputation (jadal). The author begins by illustrating the first type: direct refutation by showing that the opponent's proof can be applied to a case that yields an obviously false conclusion. The example is from kalām: the opponent argues that the world is eternal because it is the effect of the Eternal (God), and everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal. The questioner (sā'il) applies the same syllogism to daily transient events—like a new fire kindled today—which are observed to occur after not existing. If the major premise 'everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal' were true, then these events would also be eternal, which contradicts sensory observation. Thus the major premise is false. The author notes that one need not specify which premise contains the error; that was done only for clarity. The underlying logical principle: the conclusion (madlūl) is necessary from the premises; if the conclusion fails (false), then at least one premise must be false. This type of naqd corresponds to the well-known 'naqd' in uṣūl al-fiqh.

The second type is refutation by showing that the opponent's proof necessitates an impossibility (muḥāl). The example: the proponent claims that 'the definition (ḥadd) has a definition' and proves it by saying 'it is a definition, and every definition has a definition'—a first-figure syllogism. The questioner points out that this leads to infinite regress (tasalsul): if every definition needs a definition, then the definition of a definition needs another, and so on ad infinitum. Since actual things do not entail absurdity, the proof must be unsound. The author explains the impossibility: every definition, as a definiendum, requires a definition, so no definition can ever be known. Another example of absurdity is priority circularity (dawr sabqī): the proponent says 'the human is son of a human, and every son of a human is human, therefore the human is human.' Here the term 'human' in the minor premise depends on understanding 'human' in the major premise, and vice versa, creating a vicious circle. Both examples illustrate that a proof which entails an absurd consequence is invalid.

The passage is a core text for understanding the dialectical technique of naqd, distinguishing two forms: one based on counterexample (naqd bi'l-mithāl) and one based on consequence (naqd bi'l-ilzām). The author emphasizes that naqd does not require pinpointing the exact flawed premise; it is sufficient that the proof yields a falsehood or an impossibility.

Key Terms
النقض
المدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first type of naqd, what is the key logical principle that makes the refutation valid?
A) The conclusion is necessary from the premises.
B) The opponent's argument is in the first figure.
C) The counterexample must be exactly the same in all respects.
D) The questioner must identify the false premise.

Key Takeaway
The first type of naqd disproves a premise by showing it leads to a false conclusion in a parallel case.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following argument from a mutakallim: 'The world is temporally originated; whatever is temporally originated requires a creator; therefore the world has a creator.' Apply the first type of naqd by finding a counterexample that shares the minor premise but yields a false conclusion. Write your analysis.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
10 min
The Second Type of Naqd: Necessitating Absurdity
Illustration of the second type of naqd using definition and human examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an opponent's proof entails an absurdity (muhal) such as infinite regress or circularity, and use that as a valid refutation.

Core Idea
If a proof logically forces an absurdity, it cannot correspond to reality and is therefore invalid.

The second type of naqd is more subtle: instead of providing a direct counterexample, the questioner demonstrates that the opponent's argument, if accepted, leads to an impossible consequence—like an infinite chain of definitions or a vicious circle. This refutation is powerful because it shows the argument cannot be true without entailing a logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim and logical chain
Determine the claim and how the opponent attempts to prove it. In the example, the proponent claims 'the definition has a definition' by saying 'it is a definition, and every definition has a definition'.
2
Examine the implications for infinite regress
If the opponent's argument requires an endless chain (e.g., every definition needs another definition ad infinitum), then it leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible.
Infinite regress is an absurdity because it cannot be realized in actual existence.
3
Check for circular dependency
Look for cases where understanding one term depends on another that in turn depends on the first (dawr sabqī). In the example, the term 'human' in the minor premise depends on 'human' in the major premise.
Circularity makes definitions impossible to know.
4
Conclude that the proof is unsound
Since the proof entails an absurdity, it cannot be correct. The refutation does not require a counterexample; the absurdity itself invalidates the proof.

Divisions of Naqd: Real, Resembling, Well-known, and Broken
Author classifies naqd into real and resembling, then real into well-known and broken.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the three types of naqd (refutation) in disputation according to the author's scheme.

Core Idea
A refutation is only valid if the proof's form is unchanged; deleting essential conditions breaks it.

In disputation, a questioner may attempt to refute the respondent's proof by counterexample. But not all refutations are equal. The author divides naqd into real and resembling, and real naqd further into well-known and broken. Understanding these categories is essential for evaluating the strength of an objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division
The author first divides naqd into two: real (haqiqi) and resembling (shabihi). Real naqd is based on the exact proof; resembling naqd only looks like a refutation.
2
Subdivide real naqd
Real naqd is further divided into two: well-known (mashhur) and broken (maksur). This yields three total categories: mashhur, maksur, and shabihi.
3
Define well-known naqd
In well-known naqd, the questioner brings the respondent's proof in the exact same form, without deleting any part. This is the standard, accepted form.
The author calls it 'what we defined earlier and gave an example of'.
4
Define broken naqd
In broken naqd, the questioner deletes some descriptions from the original proof before applying the counterexample. The deletion may be essential or irrelevant.
The term 'broken' (maksur) indicates the proof is compromised.
5
Analyze the example
The author gives a qisas example: the respondent argues that a person must be executed because he killed a peer intentionally and wrongfully. The questioner deletes 'wrongful' and claims the blood guardian's intentional killing of the killer has no retaliation. Since 'wrongful' is essential, the naqd is broken and rejected.
This illustrates that deleting a beneficial description invalidates the naqd.

واعلم أن النقض في اصطلاحهم ينقسم إلى قسمين: الأول النقض الحقيقي، والثاني النقض الشبيهي والأول الذي هو النقض الحقيقي ينقسم إلى قسمين: أحدهما النقض المشهور، والثاني النقض المكسور، فتحصل أن أقسام النقض ثلاثة : الأول نقض مشهور الثاني نقض مكسور الثالث نقض شبيهي . أما النقض الحقيقي المشهور فهو ما عرفناه آنفا ومثلنا له، وضابطه أن السائل إذا أراد الاعتراض على دليل المعلل بالنقض جاء بدليل المعلل على نفس الهيئة التي أورده عليها صاحبه، ولم يحذف منه شيئًا ؛ فإنْ حذف بعض الأوصاف وأجرى النقض على دليل المعلل في حال كونه حاذفا بعض الأوصاف فهو النقض المكسور، وتارة يكون الوصف المحذوف له فائدة بحيث أنه لو لم يحذفه لما صح له النقض، وتارة يكون الوصف المحذوف لا فائدة فيه فوجوده كعدمه بالنسبة إلى صحة النقض وعدمها . ومثال النقض المكسور بحذف بعض الأوصاف التي لها فائدة عدم توجه النقض أن يقول المعلل : هذا يجب قتله قصاصا، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه قاتل كفؤا عمدًا عدوانًا، وكل قاتل كفؤا عمداً عدوانًا يجب قتله قصاصا ينتج من الشكل الأول : هذا يجب قتله قصاصا . فيقول السائل : دليلك هذا منقوض ؛ لأنه يوجد دون مدولوله؛ فإن ولي الدم إذا مكنه السلطان من قاتل وليه فإنه يقتله عمدًا، وهذا القتل العمد لا قصاص فيه فقد وجد دليلك وهو القتل العمد لكفؤ مع تخلف القصاص . وإنما كان هذا النوع من النقض المسكور لأنه حذف في نقضه بعض أوصاف دليل المعلل التي لها فائدة في عدم توجه النقض، ولو لم يحذفها لما توجه نقضه، وذلك هو الوصف بكون القتل عدوانا ؛ لأن قتل ولي الدم للجاني قصاصًا ليس عدوانًا . وهذا النوع من النقض المكسور مردود غير مقبول، فلا يجوز ارتكابه في المناظرة ، لكن السائل إذا ارتكبه أجاب عنه المعلل . أما إن كان الوصف المحذوف لا فائدة فيه فحذفه لا بأس به، ولا يؤثر في توجه النقض، ومثاله أن يقول المعلل المعتقد مذهب
Know that naqd in their terminology is divided into two divisions: the first is real naqd, and the second is resembling naqd. And the first, which is real naqd, is divided into two divisions: one of them is well-known naqd, and the second is broken naqd. So it results that the divisions of naqd are three: first, well-known naqd; second, broken naqd; third, resembling naqd. As for the real well-known naqd, it is what we defined earlier and gave an example of. Its rule is that if the questioner wants to object to the proof of the respondent (al-mu'allil) by naqd, he brings the respondent's proof in the very form in which its author presented it, and does not delete anything from it. If he deletes some descriptions and applies the naqd to the respondent's proof while being one who deletes some descriptions, then it is broken naqd. Sometimes the deleted description has a benefit such that if he had not deleted it, the naqd would not have been valid for him. And sometimes the deleted description has no benefit, so its existence is like its non-existence with respect to the validity or invalidity of the naqd. An example of broken naqd by deleting some descriptions that have the benefit of preventing the validity of the naqd is when the respondent says: 'This one must be killed as retaliation (qisas),' then he establishes the proof for that by saying: 'Because he is a killer of a peer, intentionally, wrongfully. And every killer of a peer, intentionally, wrongfully must be killed as retaliation.' This yields from the first figure: 'This one must be killed as retaliation.' Then the questioner says: 'This proof of yours is refuted (manqud) because it exists without its implication (madlul). For the guardian of the blood, if the sultan gives him power over the killer of his relative, then he kills him intentionally, and this intentional killing has no retaliation in it. So your proof—intentional killing of a peer—has occurred while the retaliation is absent.' And this type was called broken naqd because he deleted in his naqd some descriptions of the respondent's proof that have benefit in preventing the validity of the naqd, and had he not deleted them, his naqd would not have been valid. That description is the description of the killing being wrongful (udwanan), because the killing of the criminal by the blood guardian as retaliation is not wrongful. And this type of broken naqd is rejected, unacceptable, so it is not permissible to commit it in the disputation. However, if the questioner commits it, the respondent must answer it. As for if the deleted description has no benefit, then its deletion is not problematic and does not affect the validity of the naqd. Its example is when the respondent who adopts a school says...
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of refutation (naqd) in disputation. The author divides naqd into two main types: real (haqiqi) and resembling (shabihi). Real naqd is further divided into well-known (mashhur) and broken (maksur). The well-known naqd is the standard type where the opponent brings the exact same proof without any alteration. The broken naqd occurs when the opponent deletes some descriptions from the original proof. The author then distinguishes two subcases: when the deleted description is essential (has benefit) and when it is not. An example is given from the law of retaliation (qisas). The respondent argues that a certain person must be killed as retaliation because he is a killer, peer, intentional, and wrongful. The opponent deletes the 'wrongful' condition and points out that the blood guardian kills the killer intentionally, but that killing is not subject to retaliation. However, this is invalid because the original proof required 'wrongful', which is missing. The author concludes that this type of broken naqd is unacceptable, but if used, the respondent must answer. He also notes that if the deleted description is irrelevant, the naqd is fine. The passage ends mid-sentence with an example of unnecessary deletion.

Broken Refutation by Deleting Beneficial Descriptions
Example and ruling of invalid broken refutation by deleting beneficial descriptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a refutation (naqd) is invalid due to deletion of a beneficial description and formulate the correct response.

Core Idea
A refutation that omits a key description is broken—it fails to truly challenge the evidence.

In dialectical debates, a questioner may attempt to refute the defender's argument by presenting a counterexample. However, if the questioner deletes an essential description that would have prevented the refutation, the refutation becomes 'broken' (naqd maksur) and invalid. This lesson walks through an example from the law of retaliation to illustrate this concept and shows how a defender should respond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defender's complete argument
Find the full syllogism: the defender claims 'This murderer must be killed in retaliation' because 'he is a murderer of a protected person intentionally and aggressively.' Note all three conditions: protected person, intentional, and aggressive.
The defender uses a first-figure categorical syllogism.
2
Detect the questioner's attempted refutation
The questioner brings a case: a guardian kills the murderer with the ruler's permission. This is intentional but not aggressive. The questioner claims the defender's evidence exists (intentional killing of a protected person) but the ruling (retaliation) does not apply.
The questioner deletes the description 'aggressive'.
3
Assess whether the deleted description is beneficial
If the description 'aggressive' were included in the questioner's refutation, the case would not apply because the guardian's killing is not aggressive. Thus, the deletion is beneficial—it allows the refutation to appear valid. Had it been included, the refutation would not succeed.
A beneficial description is one whose inclusion would block the refutation.
4
Determine the ruling
The author rules that this type of naqd maksur is rejected (mardud) and not permissible in disputation. The questioner's refutation is invalid because it deletes a necessary condition.
The ruling is based on the principle of complete accuracy in challenging evidence.
5
Formulate the defender's response
If the questioner commits this error, the defender should point out that the refutation is broken because it omitted the description 'aggressive', and thus does not truly contradict the original evidence.
The defender's response preserves the integrity of the original argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال النقض المكسور بحذف بعض الأوصاف التي لها فائدة عدم توجه النقض أن يقول المعلل : هذا يجب قتله قصاصا، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه قاتل كفؤا عمدًا عدوانًا، وكل قاتل كفؤا عمداً عدوانًا يجب قتله قصاصا ينتج من الشكل الأول : هذا يجب قتله قصاصا . فيقول السائل : دليلك هذا منقوض ؛ لأنه يوجد دون مدولوله؛ فإن ولي الدم إذا مكنه السلطان من قاتل وليه فإنه يقتله عمدًا، وهذا القتل العمد لا قصاص فيه فقد وجد دليلك وهو القتل العمد لكفؤ مع تخلف القصاص . وإنما كان هذا النوع من النقض المسكور لأنه حذف في نقضه بعض أوصاف دليل المعلل التي لها فائدة في عدم توجه النقض، ولو لم يحذفها لما توجه نقضه، وذلك هو الوصف بكون القتل عدوانا . وهذا النوع من النقض المكسور مردود غير مقبول، فلا يجوز ارتكابه في المناظرة، لكن السائل إذا ارتكبه أجاب عنه المعلل .
And an example of the broken refutation by deleting some descriptions that have benefit (in preventing the refutation) is that the defender says: 'This person must be killed in retaliation,' then he establishes evidence for that by saying: 'Because he is a murderer of a protected person intentionally and aggressively, and every murderer of a protected person intentionally and aggressively must be killed in retaliation,' producing from the first figure: 'This person must be killed in retaliation.' Then the questioner says: 'Your evidence is refuted because it exists without its implication; for if the guardian of the blood is given authority by the ruler over the murderer of his ward, then he kills him intentionally, and this intentional killing has no retaliation. So your evidence exists, which is intentional killing of a protected person, along with the absence of retaliation.' And this type of broken refutation is so because he deleted in his refutation some descriptions of the defender's evidence that have benefit in preventing the refutation; had he not deleted them, his refutation would not have applied. That description is the characteristic of being aggressive. And this type of broken refutation is rejected and not accepted, so it is not permissible to commit it in disputation, but if the questioner commits it, the defender responds to it.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage explains the invalid type of 'broken refutation' (naqd maksur) where the questioner deletes a description that is essential for preventing the refutation. The defender argues that a murderer must be killed in retaliation because he killed intentionally and aggressively. The questioner attempts a refutation by citing a case where a guardian kills the murderer with the ruler's permission, which is intentional but not aggressive. However, the questioner deletes the description 'aggression' from his refutation. The author points out that if the questioner had included 'aggression', the refutation would not hold, because the guardian's killing is not an act of aggression. Therefore, this type of naqd maksur is invalid (mardud) and not permissible in disputation. Nevertheless, if the questioner commits it, the defender must provide a response.

Real vs Spurious Refutation in Dialectic
Defines two types of refutation with an example from mu'amalat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real refutation (al-naqd al-haqiqi) and a spurious refutation (al-naqd al-shabihi) by identifying their respective loci in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Real refutation targets evidence; spurious refutation targets the claim.

This lesson introduces two key types of refutation in Islamic dialectic (adab al-bahth). Understanding the difference is crucial for evaluating objections in theological and legal debates. We will analyze an example from Islamic contract law to see how a spurious refutation uses external authorities like consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the definitions of al-naqd al-haqiqi (real refutation) and al-naqd al-shabihi (spurious refutation). Note that real refutation deals with the defender's evidence (dalil), while spurious refutation deals with the claim (da'wa) itself.
The passage explicitly states the locus (mawrid) of each.
2
Understand the example context
The defender argues that a worker's contract is invalid due to gharar (uncertainty). He constructs a syllogism to prove his claim. The questioner responds by saying this claim contradicts the consensus of scholars.
This is a typical debate in Islamic law on transactions.
3
Determine the type of refutation
Since the questioner attacks the claim (da'wa) by citing consensus, not the defender's evidence (dalil), this is a spurious refutation (naqd shabihi). Real refutation would have shown that the evidence exists but its implication does not hold.
The passage labels this as spurious because it does not address the evidence.
4
Analyze the syllogism
The defender uses a categorical syllogism in the first figure: (1) Every worker working for uncertain money has an invalid contract, (2) This worker works for uncertain money, therefore (3) This worker has an invalid contract. This is a valid Barbara syllogism.
The spurious refutation does not invalidate this syllogism; it only challenges the conclusion's acceptance by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يخفى أن النقض الحقيقي الذي ذكرناه بأمثلته مَوْرده دليل المعلل . وأما النقض الشبيهي فضابطه إبطال الدعوى بشهادة فساد مخصوص ككونها مخالفة لإجماع العلماء، أو منافية لمذهب المعلل، وهذا مورده الدعوى كما هو واضح . ومثال كونها مخالفة لإجماع العلماء أن يقول المعلل : هذا العامل يعمل في مقابلة أن يُعطَى مالاً لا يدري أيوجد ذلك المال أو لا يوجد أصلاً، وعلى تقدير وجوده فلا يدري أيكون قليلاً أو كثيرًا، وهذا عين المعاوضة بما فيه غرر وكل عامل يعمل كذلك فمعاوضته فاسدة، ينتج من الشكل الأول : هذا العامل معاوضته فاسدة . فيقول السائل: هذا الدليل منقوض بإجماع العلماء على تخلف
And it is not hidden that the real refutation which we mentioned with its examples has its locus the defender's evidence. As for the spurious refutation, its criterion is invalidating the claim by a specific evidence of corruption, such as it being contrary to the consensus of scholars, or contradictory to the defender's doctrine, and this has its locus the claim, as is clear. An example of it being contrary to consensus is that the defender says: 'This worker works in exchange for being given money that he does not know whether it exists or not at all, and assuming its existence, he does not know whether it is little or much, and this is exactly a contract involving uncertainty, and every worker who works like that has an invalid contract,' producing from the first figure: 'This worker has an invalid contract.' Then the questioner says: 'This evidence is refuted by the consensus of scholars on the absence [of the ruling].'
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage clarifies the difference between two types of refutation in dialectic. The 'real refutation' (naqd haqiqi) attacks the defender's evidence (dalil) by showing that the evidence exists without its implication. This was the focus of the previous examples. The 'spurious refutation' (naqd shabihi) attacks the claim (da'wa) itself by showing that the claim contradicts a consensus (ijma') or the defender's own doctrinal position. The example given concerns a defender who claims that a worker's contract is invalid due to gharar (uncertainty). The questioner refutes not by attacking the evidence but by noting that this claim contradicts the consensus of scholars (presumably that such contracts are valid). The locus of the spurious refutation is the claim, not the evidence.

Key Terms
النقض الحقيقي
النقض الشبيهي
الغرر

Key Takeaway
Real refutation challenges the defender's evidence; spurious refutation challenges the claim by appealing to external authorities like consensus or the defender's own doctrine.

Three Types of Refutation in Jadal
Author defines three types of refutation with examples from philosophy and fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between broken, true, and similar refutation by identifying their targets: proof, claim, or premise removal.

Core Idea
A refutation's type determines what it challenges—the proof's structure, the claim's validity, or the argument's superfluous parts.

In jadal, not all refutations are equal. The author classifies naqd into three precise categories based on what is being attacked: the proof itself, the claim via a sign of corruption, or a redundant premise. Mastering these categories equips you to parse debates and judge the strength of objections in Islamic scholarly discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three targets
Recognize that a refutation can target the proof (dalil), the claim (da'wa), or a premise (muqaddima). This determines the type: true refutation attacks the proof, similar refutation attacks the claim, broken refutation attacks a redundant premise.
2
Analyze the philosopher's proof
The philosopher proves the world's eternity using a first-figure syllogism. The refuter omits the premise 'its existence is dependent on Him' because it is redundant. This is a broken refutation since removing a useless premise does not harm the argument.
The missing premise adds no benefit; the proof stands without it.
3
Contrast true and similar refutation
True refutation attacks the proof's validity or completeness. Similar refutation attacks the claim by pointing to a specific sign of corruption, such as contradiction of consensus (ijma') or the prover's own doctrine.
Similar refutation does not challenge the proof's logic, only the claim's acceptability.
4
Apply to the mudaraba example
The prover claims that a worker's transaction is invalid due to gharar. The refuter counters by citing consensus on mudaraba where such transactions are valid. This is a similar refutation because it uses contradiction of ijma' to invalidate the claim, not the proof.
The proof's syllogism remains untouched; only the claim is shown to conflict with established authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفلاسفة : العالم ،قديم، ثم يقيم دليله الباطل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه أثر القديم، ومستند في وجوده إليه، وكل ماهو كذلك فهو قديم، ينتج من الشكل الأول : العالم قديم . فيقول السائل : دليلك هذا منقوض بالحوادث اليومية، كما تقدم إيضاحه، ويذكر دليله ويحذف لفظ ومستند في وجوده إليه)؛ لأن كونه مستندًا في وجوده إليه لا فائدة في ذكره، ولا ضرر في حذفه؛ لأن كونه أثر القديم يُغني عنه كما لا يخفى . وهذا النوع من النقض المكسور مقبول ؛ لأن حذف مالا فائدة فيه وذكره سيان . ولا يخفى أن النقض الحقيقي الذي ذكرناه بأمثلته مَوْرده دليل المعلل . وأما النقض الشبيهي فضابطه إبطال الدعوى بشهادة فساد ،مخصوص ككونها مخالفة لإجماع العلماء، أو منافية لمذهب المعلل، وهذا مورده الدعوى كما هو واضح . ومثال كونها مخالفة لإجماع العلماء أن يقول المعلل : هذا العامل يعمل في مقابلة أن يُعطَى مالاً لا يدري أيوجد ذلك المال أو لا يوجد أصلاً، وعلى تقدير وجوده فلا يدري أيكون قليلاً أو كثيرًا، وهذا عين المعاوضة بما فيه غرر وكل عامل يعمل كذلك فمعاوضته فاسدة، ينتج من الشكل الأول : هذا العامل معاوضته فاسدة. فيقول السائل: هذا الدليل منقوض بإجماع العلماء على تخلف جميع مدلوله عنه في صورة شركة المضاربة المعروفة بالقراض، فقد أجمع العلماء فيها على أن الرجل يجوز له أن يدفع للعامل مالاً يعمل فيه بالتجارة، على أن يكون للعامل نصفُ الربح أو ربعه أو ثلثه مثلاً ، حسبما اتفقا عليه، مع بقاء رأس المال ملكًا لصاحبه، ولا شيء للعامل في مقابلة عمله إلا ما يحصل من الربح مع أن الربح يجوز أن يحصل أو لا يحصل، وعلى تقدير حصوله يجوز أن يكون قليلاً أو كثيرًا، فقد أجمع العلماء على وجود ما استدل به المعلل على المنع، مع إجماعهم على تخلف الحكم الذي هو المنع عنه في هذه الصورة. فهذا نقض شبيهي ؛ لأن إبطال الدعوى فيه كان بشهادة فساد مخصوص وهو مخالفتها لإجماع العلماء في صورة النقض، وإن أمكن صدقها في غيرها .
The philosophers [say]: The world is eternal. Then he establishes his false proof on that, saying: Because it is the effect of the Eternal, and its existence is dependent on Him, and everything that is such is eternal. It follows from the first figure: The world is eternal. Then the questioner says: This proof of yours is refuted by daily events, as previously clarified. He mentions his proof and omits the phrase 'and its existence is dependent on Him'; because its being dependent on Him has no benefit in mentioning it, and no harm in omitting it, because its being the effect of the Eternal suffices, as is not hidden. This type of broken refutation is acceptable, because omitting something that has no benefit and mentioning it are the same. And it is not hidden that the true refutation which we mentioned with its examples applies to the prover's proof. As for the similar refutation, its control is the invalidation of the claim by a specific evidence of corruption, such as it being contrary to the consensus of the scholars, or contradictory to the prover's doctrine. And this applies to the claim, as is clear. An example of it being contrary to the consensus of the scholars is when the prover says: This worker works in exchange for being given money, not knowing whether that money exists or does not exist at all, and given its existence he does not know whether it is little or much. This is exactly a transaction involving uncertainty (gharar). And every worker who works in this way, his transaction is invalid. It follows from the first figure: This worker's transaction is invalid. Then the questioner says: This proof is refuted by the consensus of the scholars on the non-occurrence of all its implications in the case of the mudaraba company known as qirad. For the scholars have unanimously agreed in it that a man may give money to a worker to work with in trade, on the condition that the worker gets half, a quarter, or a third of the profit, as agreed upon, while the capital remains the property of its owner. And the worker gets nothing in exchange for his work except what is obtained from the profit, even though the profit may or may not occur, and given its occurrence it may be little or much. So the scholars have unanimously agreed on the existence of what the prover used as evidence for prohibition, while they unanimously agree on the non-occurrence of the ruling (which is prohibition) in this case. This is a similar refutation, because the invalidation of the claim was by a specific evidence of corruption, namely its contradiction of the consensus of the scholars in the case of the refutation, although it might be true in other cases.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition of the types of refutation (naqd) used in jadal. The author begins with an example from the philosophers: they claim the world is eternal using a syllogism from the first figure—'The world is the effect of the Eternal, and everything that is an effect of the Eternal is eternal, therefore the world is eternal.' The questioner refutes this by pointing to daily events (temporal events), saying the proof is refuted (naqd). The author then explains a subtype: the questioner presents the proof but omits the premise 'its existence is dependent on Him' because it is redundant—it adds nothing and its removal harms nothing. This is called 'broken refutation' (naqd maksur), which is acceptable because removing a useless premise is harmless. Next, the author distinguishes true refutation (naqd haqiqi) and similar refutation (naqd shabeehi). True refutation attacks the proof itself (the evidence of the prover). Similar refutation attacks the claim (the proposition) by pointing to a specific sign of corruption, such as the claim contradicting scholarly consensus (ijma') or contradicting the prover's own doctrine (madhhab). The author gives a detailed example for consensus: the prover argues that a worker who works for uncertain pay (gharar) makes an invalid contract, using a categorical syllogism. The refuter counters by citing the case of mudaraba (partnership in trade) where scholars unanimously allow a similar arrangement despite uncertainty in profit. Since the prover's proof would imply that mudaraba is invalid, yet scholars unanimously consider it valid, the proof is refuted. This is a similar refutation because it uses a specific mark of corruption: contradiction of ijma'. The passage carefully delineates the conditions under which each type of refutation is valid in debate.

النقض الحقيقي

Similar Refutation and the Role of Ijma'
Author defines similar refutation with an example from consensus on mudaraba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a similar refutation (naqd shabeehi) based on contradiction of consensus and explain why it attacks the claim, not the proof.

Core Idea
Similar refutation invalidates a claim by showing it contradicts a specific authoritative source, such as ijma', without attacking the proof itself.

The author's detailed example on mudaraba illustrates how a prover's claim about gharar can be refuted not by breaking the proof, but by showing the claim contradicts scholarly consensus. This type of refutation is powerful because it shifts the burden to the prover to reconcile his claim with established authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prover's claim and syllogism
The prover claims: 'This worker's transaction is invalid because it involves uncertain pay (gharar).' He uses a categorical syllogism from the first figure: every worker with uncertain pay has an invalid transaction; this worker has uncertain pay; therefore his transaction is invalid.
The syllogism is logically valid; the refutation will not challenge its form.
2
Recognize the refuter's move
The refuter does not attack the syllogism. Instead, he points to the case of mudaraba (qirad) where scholars unanimously allow a similar arrangement despite uncertainty in profit. The refuter argues that the prover's claim contradicts this consensus.
The refutation uses ijma' as a sign of corruption (shahadat fasad).
3
Understand why this is similar refutation
Similar refutation attacks the claim (da'wa) itself by showing it conflicts with a specific authority or principle. Here, the conflict is with ijma'. The proof (dalil) remains untouched; only the claim is shown to be unacceptable.
If the claim were true, it would imply mudaraba is invalid, but consensus says it is valid. Hence the claim is corrupt.
4
Contrast with true refutation
True refutation would attack the proof itself, e.g., by showing a logical flaw or missing premise. Similar refutation bypasses the proof and targets the claim via external evidence like ijma' or the prover's own doctrine.
This distinction is crucial for evaluating the strength of objections in jadal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفلاسفة : العالم ،قديم، ثم يقيم دليله الباطل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه أثر القديم، ومستند في وجوده إليه، وكل ماهو كذلك فهو قديم، ينتج من الشكل الأول : العالم قديم . فيقول السائل : دليلك هذا منقوض بالحوادث اليومية، كما تقدم إيضاحه، ويذكر دليله ويحذف لفظ ومستند في وجوده إليه)؛ لأن كونه مستندًا في وجوده إليه لا فائدة في ذكره، ولا ضرر في حذفه؛ لأن كونه أثر القديم يُغني عنه كما لا يخفى . وهذا النوع من النقض المكسور مقبول ؛ لأن حذف مالا فائدة فيه وذكره سيان . ولا يخفى أن النقض الحقيقي الذي ذكرناه بأمثلته مَوْرده دليل المعلل . وأما النقض الشبيهي فضابطه إبطال الدعوى بشهادة فساد ،مخصوص ككونها مخالفة لإجماع العلماء، أو منافية لمذهب المعلل، وهذا مورده الدعوى كما هو واضح . ومثال كونها مخالفة لإجماع العلماء أن يقول المعلل : هذا العامل يعمل في مقابلة أن يُعطَى مالاً لا يدري أيوجد ذلك المال أو لا يوجد أصلاً، وعلى تقدير وجوده فلا يدري أيكون قليلاً أو كثيرًا، وهذا عين المعاوضة بما فيه غرر وكل عامل يعمل كذلك فمعاوضته فاسدة، ينتج من الشكل الأول : هذا العامل معاوضته فاسدة. فيقول السائل: هذا الدليل منقوض بإجماع العلماء على تخلف جميع مدلوله عنه في صورة شركة المضاربة المعروفة بالقراض، فقد أجمع العلماء فيها على أن الرجل يجوز له أن يدفع للعامل مالاً يعمل فيه بالتجارة، على أن يكون للعامل نصفُ الربح أو ربعه أو ثلثه مثلاً ، حسبما اتفقا عليه، مع بقاء رأس المال ملكًا لصاحبه، ولا شيء للعامل في مقابلة عمله إلا ما يحصل من الربح مع أن الربح يجوز أن يحصل أو لا يحصل، وعلى تقدير حصوله يجوز أن يكون قليلاً أو كثيرًا، فقد أجمع العلماء على وجود ما استدل به المعلل على المنع، مع إجماعهم على تخلف الحكم الذي هو المنع عنه في هذه الصورة. فهذا نقض شبيهي ؛ لأن إبطال الدعوى فيه كان بشهادة فساد مخصوص وهو مخالفتها لإجماع العلماء في صورة النقض، وإن أمكن صدقها في غيرها .

The Logical Structure of Refutation (Naqd) as a Compound Argument
Author explains that a refutation acts like a compound syllogism and gives an example about marrying a fornicator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to expand a given refutation (naqd) into its explicit logical form and identify how it functions as a compound argument in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
A refutation is not just denial; it is a compressed syllogism that exposes contradictions.

In dialectical argumentation, a refutation often appears as a simple counterexample, but the author reveals that it contains a hidden logical structure. This lesson unpacks the two standard forms of refutation and applies them to a real fiqh dispute, showing how the refutation acts as a compound syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two explicit refutation forms
The author gives two forms: (1) 'This proof has its implication unfulfilled, and every such proof is invalid, therefore this proof is invalid.' (2) 'This proof entails an impossibility, and every such proof is invalid, therefore this proof is invalid.' Notice the repeated pattern: a minor premise about this proof, a major premise about all such proofs, and a conclusion.
2
Expand each form into a full categorical syllogism
Write the arguments in standard categorical form: Major premise: 'Every proof that has its implication unfulfilled is invalid.' Minor premise: 'This proof has its implication unfulfilled.' Conclusion: 'Therefore, this proof is invalid.' Verify that this is a valid first-figure Barbara syllogism.
The second form is analogous, substituting 'entails an impossibility' for the minor term.
3
Connect the refutation to the dialectical example
The Shafi'i example shows a refutation by contradiction with the prover's own doctrine. Identify the two positions: the prover (opponent) claims that marriage of a fornicatress is prohibited, citing the verse [24:3]. Shafi'i and Malik argue it is not prohibited, claiming the verse is abrogated by [24:32]. The refutation points out that the abrogator is more general, meaning it includes the fornicatress – contradicting the prover's own acceptance of general abrogation.
The passage cuts off, but the logical structure is clear: the prover's move implies a contradiction with his own principles.
4
Recognize the refutation as a compound argument
Though the explicit reasoning appears as a single syllogism, the author notes that refutation is 'fi quwwat dalil murakkab' – it is equivalent to a compound argument with omitted premises. The missing premises are often dialectically accepted propositions (e.g., that the prover's doctrine is consistent). Understanding this helps in reconstructing the full force of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الاعتراض بالنقض في قوة دليل مركب بحذف بعض مقدماته، وصورته أن يقول مثلاً : هذا الدليل تخلف عنه مدلوله، وكل دلیل كان كذلك فهو فاسد فهذا الدليل فاسد أو يقول : هذا الدليل مستلزم للمحال وكل دليل كان كذلك فهو فاسد، فهذا الدليل فاسد . ومثال كونها منافية لمذهب المعلل قول الشافعي ومالك وغيرهما : إن الزانية لا يحرم نكاحها واستدلوا على ذلك بأن قوله تعالى وَالزَّانِيَةُ لَا يَنكِحُهَا إِلَّا زَانٍ أَوْ مُشْرِكُ ) [النور/ ٣] الآية منسوخ بقوله : وَأَنكِحُوا الْأَيْمَى مِنكُمْ﴾ [النور/ ۳۲] ومعلوم أن هذا الناسخ المزعوم الذي هو آية ﴿ وَأَنكِحُوا الْأَيْمَى مِنكُمْ أعم من المنسوخ في محل النزاع، وهو آية ﴿ وَالزَّانِيَةُ لَا يَنكِحُهَا إِلَّا زَانٍ أَوْ مُشْرِكٌ ؛ لأن لفظ الأيامي صادق
Know that the objection by refutation is equivalent to a compound argument with some of its premises omitted. Its form is that he says, for example: This proof has its implication unfulfilled, and every proof that is such is invalid, so this proof is invalid. Or he says: This proof entails an impossibility, and every proof that is such is invalid, so this proof is invalid. An example of it being contradictory to the prover's doctrine is the saying of al-Shafi'i, Malik and others: The marriage of the fornicatress is not prohibited. They argued for that by saying that the verse 'And the fornicatress, none marries her except a fornicator or an idolater' [Quran 24:3] is abrogated by His saying 'And marry the unmarried among you' [Quran 24:32]. And it is known that this alleged abrogator, which is the verse 'And marry the unmarried among you', is more general than the abrogated in the point of dispute, which is the verse 'And the fornicatress, none marries her except a fornicator or an idolater', because the term 'unmarried' applies...
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical analysis of the logical structure of refutation. The author begins by stating that an objection by refutation (naqd) is equivalent to a compound argument (daly murakkab) with some premises omitted. He provides two common forms: either 'This proof has its implication unfulfilled, and every such proof is invalid, therefore this proof is invalid' or 'This proof entails an impossibility, and every such proof is invalid, therefore this proof is invalid.' These are first-figure syllogisms with an implicit major premise. The author then gives an example of a similar refutation (naqd shabeehi) where the prover's claim contradicts his own doctrine (madhhab). The example is from al-Shafi'i and Malik: they argue that it is not forbidden to marry a fornicatress, using the claim that the verse 'And the fornicatress, none marries her except a fornicator or an idolater' [24:3] is abrogated by the verse 'And marry the unmarried among you' [24:32]. The abrogator is more general (أعم) because it includes all unmarried women, including the fornicatress. The passage cuts off before completing the argument, but the idea is that the prover (who follows the opposite view) would be contradicting his own doctrine by accepting this abrogation. The refutation points out the inconsistency. The author emphasizes that the refutation functions as a compound argument, even when it appears simple.

Key Terms
دليل مركب (dalīl murakkab): A compound argument consisting of a syllogism with an implied premise; the refutation is treated as such an argument. [Note: Standard in Mantiq; no shift.]
النقض الشبيهي (al-naqd al-shabīhī): Refutation by showing contradiction with the prover's doctrine (or consensus); an example is given here from fiqh. [Note: Same as earlier lesson.]
المنافي لمذهب المعلل (al-munāfī li-madhhab al-muʿallil): Contradiction to the prover's own school/doctrine; a specific sign of corruption used in similar refutation. [Note: Standard term in jadal.]
الاستلزام للمحال (al-istilzām li-l-muḥāl): Entailing an impossibility; one of the forms of the compound argument showing the proof is invalid. [Note: Standard logical term; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) A disjunctive syllogism.

Key Takeaway
A refutation (naqd) is logically equivalent to a compound syllogism; expanding it reveals its inferential structure and exposes contradictions in the opponent's position.

The Mudaraba Example of Naqd Shabihi
Author presents a fiqhi example of naqd shabihi using the mudaraba contract.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a naqd shabihi (similitude refutation) and apply it to a given fiqhi example.

Core Idea
Naqd shabihi refutes an opponent's 'illa without disproving the general claim.

In this lesson, we examine a concrete case from Islamic finance: the mudaraba (qirad) contract. The opponent (mu'allil) argued that a certain property prohibits a transaction. The refuter (sa'il) demonstrates that this property is present in mudaraba, yet scholars unanimously permit it. This proves the opponent's evidence fails in this specific case, without destroying the opponent's general principle. Understanding this dialectical move is crucial for engaging in scholarly debate (jadal).

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's 'illa
Determine what property the mu'allil claims necessitates prohibition. The passage assumes this is known, but we infer that some property is present in mudaraba.
2
Find the refutation case
Locate the specific contract (mudaraba/qirad) used to challenge the opponent's evidence.
3
Verify the property's presence and ruling's absence
Check that the property exists in the refutation case (scholarly consensus on its existence) and that the ruling (prohibition) is absent (scholarly consensus on permissibility).
This shows the opponent's evidence fails.
4
Classify as naqd shabihi
Understand that this is not a complete refutation of the opponent's claim but a specific invalidation of the evidence in a similar case.
Naqd shabihi does not require disproving the general principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جميع مدلوله عنه في صورة شركة المضاربة المعروفة بالقراض، فقد أجمع العلماء فيها على أن الرجل يجوز له أن يدفع للعامل مالاً يعمل فيه بالتجارة، على أن يكون للعامل نصفُ الربح أو ربعه أو ثلثه مثلاً ، حسبما اتفقا عليه، مع بقاء رأس المال ملكًا لصاحبه، ولا شيء للعامل في مقابلة عمله إلا ما يحصل من الربح مع أن الربح يجوز أن يحصل أو لا يحصل، وعلى تقدير حصوله يجوز أن يكون قليلاً أو كثيرًا، فقد أجمع العلماء على وجود ما استدل به المعلل على المنع، مع إجماعهم على تخلف الحكم الذي هو المنع عنه في هذه الصورة. فهذا نقض شبيهي ؛ لأن إبطال الدعوى فيه كان بشهادة فساد مخصوص وهو مخالفتها لإجماع العلماء في صورة النقض، وإن أمكن صدقها في غيرها .
All of its meaning regarding the form of the partnership known as qirad: The scholars have unanimously agreed that a man is permitted to give an agent money to work with in trade, on the condition that the agent gets half, a quarter, or a third of the profit, for example, according to what they agree upon, while the capital remains the property of its owner, and the agent has nothing in return for his work except the profit, even though profit may or may not occur, and if it occurs it may be little or much. So the scholars have unanimously agreed on the existence of what the opponent (mu'allil) used as evidence for prohibition, while they also unanimously agree on the absence of the ruling (prohibition) in this case. So this is a similitude refutation (naqd shabihi), because the invalidation of the claim here was through a specific corruption, namely its violation of the consensus of scholars in the refutation case, even if it could be true in other cases.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents a concrete example of a 'similitude refutation' (naqd shabihi) from Islamic finance. The opponent (mu'allil) had argued that a certain property (perhaps risk or uncertainty) prohibits a transaction, using evidence that such a property exists in the mudaraba (qirad) contract. The refuter (sā'il) points out that despite the presence of that property, scholars unanimously permit mudaraba, showing that the opponent's evidence does not yield the claimed ruling (prohibition) in this specific form. This is a 'similitude refutation' because it demonstrates that the opponent's reasoning fails in a case that resembles the original in the relevant property, yet the ruling is absent. The refutation does not disprove the opponent's claim entirely but shows it is invalid in this particular instance, which suffices to undermine the opponent's argument.

Key Terms
نقض شبيهي (naqd shabihi): A refutation that shows the opponent's evidence fails in a specific case that shares the claimed effective property ('illa), yet the ruling is absent, without disproving the general principle. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable in jadal literature.]
مضاربة (mudaraba): A partnership contract where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, with profits shared according to agreement, also known as qirad.
معلل (mu'allil): The opponent in a dialectical exchange who provides an 'illa (cause) for a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the mudaraba example, why is the refutation called 'naqd shabihi'?
A) Because it directly contradicts the opponent's madhab.
B) Because it shows that the opponent's evidence fails in a case that shares the same property, despite scholarly consensus on the ruling's absence.
C) Because it uses a compound syllogism.
D) Because the opponent's evidence is based on a false premise.

Key Takeaway
Naqd shabihi refutes an opponent's 'illa by showing a case where the 'illa exists but the ruling is absent, without disproving the overall claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a hypothetical fiqhi debate: The opponent claims that gharar (excessive uncertainty) renders a contract prohibited, citing examples. Using the mudaraba example, explain how you would construct a naqd shabihi against this claim.
?
Identify a real case from Islamic finance (e.g., salam or murabaha) where a naqd shabihi might be used. Explain why it fits.

The Structure of Naqd as a Compound Argument
Author explains that naqd is a compound syllogism with a missing premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical structure of a naqd refutation and reconstruct its missing premise in a jadal debate.

Core Idea
Naqd is not mere contradiction — it is a compound syllogism that exposes a defect in the opponent's evidence.

In dialectical debates (jadal), a refutation called naqd is often used to attack an opponent's proof. At first glance, it may appear as a simple denial, but this lesson reveals its syllogistic backbone. Understanding naqd as a compound argument with an implicit premise will sharpen your ability to evaluate objections in theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion of naqd
What is the refuter trying to prove? In the passage, the conclusion is always that the opponent's evidence is invalid (fasid).
2
Locate the explicit premise
Find the specific defect the refuter claims: either the evidence lacks its intended meaning (takhalluf 'an al-madlul) or it entails an impossibility (istilzam al-muhal).
3
Recognise the implicit general rule
The refuter assumes a general principle: any evidence with that defect is invalid. This is the missing major premise that turns the objection into a syllogism.
4
Reconstruct the full compound syllogism
Combine the steps: Major premise (any evidence with defect X is invalid), Minor premise (this evidence has defect X), Conclusion (therefore this evidence is invalid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الاعتراض بالنقض في قوة دليل مركب بحذف بعض مقدماته، وصورته أن يقول مثلاً : هذا الدليل تخلف عنه مدلوله، وكل دليل كان كذلك فهو فاسد فهذا الدليل فاسد أو يقول : هذا الدليل مستلزم للمحال وكل دليل كان كذلك فهو فاسد، فهذا الدليل فاسد .
Know that the objection by refutation (naqd) is in the force of a compound proof (dil murkkab) with some of its premises omitted. Its form is that he says, for example: 'This evidence lacks its meaning (madlul), and every evidence that is like that is invalid, so this evidence is invalid.' Or he says: 'This evidence entails an impossibility, and every evidence that is like that is invalid, so this evidence is invalid.'
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage defines the logical structure of a refutation (naqd) in jadal. The author explains that naqd is not a simple statement but a compound argument (dil murkkab) that is missing a premise. The structure is that of a syllogism: the refuter presents a conditional statement about the evidence (either that it lacks its intended meaning or that it leads to an absurdity), then adds a general rule that any evidence with that defect is invalid, and concludes that the opponent's evidence is invalid. The missing premise is the general rule, which is often understood implicitly. This shows that naqd is a rational refutation, not a mere denial. Two common forms are given: one using the absence of the meaning (takhalluf al-madlul) and one using the entailment of impossibility (istilzam al-muhal). Both lead to the conclusion of invalidity through a syllogistic structure.

Key Terms
دليل مركب
تخلف عن المدلول
مستلزم للمحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) A syllogism with a missing premise, concluding that the opponent's evidence is invalid.
C) An inductive generalization from many cases.
D) A direct quotation of a revealed text.

Key Takeaway
Naqd is not a simple contradiction but a reasoned refutation structured as a compound syllogism, typically with an implicit major premise.

Naqd by Contradicting the Opponent's Doctrine (Naqd Munaafi li-Madhab al-Mu'allil)
Author gives a fiqhi example of naqd contradicting opponent's doctrine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a naqd (refutation) that shows an opponent's argument violates his own accepted usul principles.

Core Idea
A naqd is strongest when it uses the opponent's own principles against him.

Naqd is a powerful dialectical tool in jadal. The strongest naqd does not merely provide a counterexample; it reveals that the opponent's own argument cannot stand given his own accepted principles. This lesson dissects a classic example from usul al-fiqh where Shafi'i's naskh argument is refuted by pointing out that it violates his own rule that a specific cannot be abrogated by a general. You will learn how to identify the contradiction and use it to refute the opponent effectively.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's argument
Shafi'i argues that the verse prohibiting marrying a fornicator (Q. 24:3) is abrogated by the general command to marry the unmarried (Q. 24:32). He constructs a syllogism: the general verse is the abrogator, so the ruling is permissibility.
The argument relies on the claim that the general verse is later and abrogates the specific.
2
Identify the opponent's own accepted principle
Shafi'i and Malik hold that a specific text (khas) cannot be abrogated by a general text (amm). According to their usul, the specific controls and specifies the general, regardless of order.
This principle is foundational in their madhhab.
3
Find the contradiction
In the opponent's argument, the alleged abrogator (Q. 24:32) is general, while the abrogated (Q. 24:3) is specific. This directly violates the opponent's own principle that a specific cannot be abrogated by a general.
The questioner spots this inconsistency.
4
Formulate the naqd
The questioner says: 'This evidence is refuted because the abrogator here is more general than the abrogated, and that contradicts your doctrine, because in your doctrine it is not permissible for a specific to be abrogated by a general.' Thus the opponent's argument fails on his own terms.
The naqd does not require a separate counterexample; it uses the opponent's own principles.
5
Verify the refutation
The opponent cannot accept the conclusion (permissibility) without abandoning his usul principle. Therefore, the argument is invalid because it leads to an inconsistency with his madhab.
This naqd is decisive because it targets the internal coherence of the opponent's system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال كونها منافية لمذهب المعلل قول الشافعي ومالك وغيرهما : إن الزانية لا يحرم نكاحها واستدلوا على ذلك بأن قوله تعالى (الزانية لا ينكحها إلا زان أو مشرك) [النور/3] الآية منسوخ بقوله (وأنكحوا الأيامى منكم) [النور/32] ومعلوم أن هذا الناسخ المزعوم الذي هو آية (وأنكحوا الأيامى منكم) أعم من المنسوخ في محل النزاع، وهو آية (الزانية لا ينكحها إلا زان أو مشرك)؛ لأن لفظ الأيامى صادق بالعفائف والزواني والذكور والإناث ومعلوم أن مذهب الشافعي ومالك أن الخاص لا يُنسخ بالعام؛ لأن الخاص عندهما يقضي على العام فيخصص عمومه، سواء تأخر نزوله عنه أو عنه أو تقدم، كما هو مقرر في أصولهما. فيقول المعلل كالشافعي مثلاً : دليل تحريم نكاح الزانية الذي خاص منسوخ بدليل إباحته الذي هو عام وكل دليلين نسخ أحدهما الآخر فالحكم للناسخ منهما، ينتج من الشكل الأول : الحكم هنا لدليل الإباحة العام؛ لأنه هو الناسخ. فيقول السائل : هذا الدليل منقوض؛ لأن الناسخ فيه أعم المنسوخ، وذلك ينافي مذهبك؛ لأنه لا يجوز فيه نسخ خاص بعام. فقد نقض عليه دليله بتخلف الحكم عنه بمقتضى مذهب المعلل؛ لأن ثبوت حكمه معه مناف لمذهب المعلل .
An example of it being contradictory to the opponent's doctrine: The statement of al-Shafi'i, Malik, and others that the marriage of a fornicator is not prohibited. They argued for that by saying that the verse 'The fornicator marries only a fornicator or an idolater' (Q. 24:3) is abrogated by His statement 'Marry the unmarried among you' (Q. 24:32). And it is known that this alleged abrogating verse, which is 'Marry the unmarried among you', is more general than the abrogated verse in the point of dispute, i.e., 'The fornicator marries only a fornicator or an idolater', because the word 'al-ayama' (unmarried) applies to chaste women, fornicators, males, and females. And it is known that the doctrine of al-Shafi'i and Malik is that the specific is not abrogated by the general, because according to them the specific rules over the general and specifies its generality, whether the specific came later or earlier, as established in their usul. So the opponent (mu'allil), al-Shafi'i for example, says: 'The evidence for the prohibition of marrying a fornicator, which is specific, is abrogated by the evidence for its permissibility, which is general. And whenever two evidences abrogate one another, the ruling is for the abrogator.' This yields from the first figure: The ruling here is for the general evidence of permissibility, because it is the abrogator. Then the questioner says: 'This evidence is refuted (manqud), because the abrogator here is more general than the abrogated, and that contradicts your doctrine, because in your doctrine it is not permissible for a specific to be abrogated by a general.' So he refuted his evidence by the absence of the ruling according to the opponent's doctrine, because the establishment of the ruling along with it contradicts the opponent's doctrine.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed example of a refutation (naqd) that contradicts the opponent's own doctrinal principles. The opponent (al-Shafi'i) argues that the prohibition of marrying a fornicator is abrogated by the general command to marry the unmarried. He constructs a syllogism: the prohibition verse is specific, the permission verse is general and abrogates it; whenever two evidences abrogate, the ruling follows the abrogator; therefore, the general permission is the ruling. The questioner refutes this by pointing out that the opponent's argument violates his own usul principle that a specific cannot be abrogated by a general. Since the opponent holds that the specific controls the general, the abrogation claim is invalid. Thus, the opponent's evidence does not yield its conclusion (the ruling of permissibility) given his own principles. This is a powerful naqd because it uses the opponent's own acceptance against him. The refutation is structured as a compound argument: it shows that the evidence entails an impossibility (contradiction with the opponent's madhab), so it is invalid.

Key Terms
ناسخ
منسوخ
خاص
عام
نقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Naqd is effective when it exposes an inconsistency between the opponent's argument and his own accepted doctrinal principles.

Naqdh (Objection) in Dialectics: Abrogation Example
Author illustrates a naqdh (objection) against a mu'allil's argument about abrogation of marriage prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a naqdh (objection) in a dialectical exchange and evaluate when it successfully refutes an argument by showing inconsistency with the opponent's own doctrine.

Core Idea
A naqdh refutes an argument by showing it contradicts the opponent's own principles.

In dialectical disputation (munazara), a common move is the naqdh, where the questioner points out that the opponent's argument leads to a conclusion that conflicts with the opponent's own doctrinal commitments. This lesson walks through a concrete example from usul al-fiqh: the abrogation of a specific ruling by a general one. You will see how the mu'allil's argument is undone not by attacking its form but by showing it violates his own madhhab's principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify roles and positions
The mu'allil (proponent) is like al-Shafi'i arguing that a specific prohibition (marrying a prostitute) is abrogated by a general permission. The sa'il (questioner) objects.
The mu'allil's madhhab holds that a specific (khas) cannot be abrogated by a general ('amm).
2
Extract the mu'allil's syllogism
The mu'allil uses a first-figure categorical syllogism: major premise — when two texts conflict, the abrogator prevails; minor premise — here the general abrogates the specific; conclusion — the general (permission) is the ruling.
The syllogism is formally valid, but the sa'il attacks not its form but its consistency with the mu'allil's own usul.
3
Analyze the sa'il's naqdh
The sa'il objects that the mu'allil's conclusion (abrogation of specific by general) directly contradicts the mu'allil's own madhhab principle that the specific cannot be abrogated by the general.
The objection is a naqdh because it shows the argument's conclusion undermines the proponent's accepted doctrine.
4
Evaluate the naqdh's success
The naqdh succeeds because it demonstrates an inconsistency: if the mu'allil accepts his own madhhab, he must reject his own conclusion. Thus the argument is refuted.
This is a standard dialectical tactic: forcing the opponent to choose between his argument and his principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالعفائف والزواني والذكور والإناث ومعلوم أن مذهب الشافعي ومالك أن الخاص لا يُنسخ بالعام؛ لأن الخاص عندهما يقضي على العام فيخصص عمومه، سواء تأخر نزوله عنه أو عنه أو تقدم، كما هو مقرر في أصولهما . هو فيقول المعلل كالشافعي مثلاً : دليل تحريم نكاح الزانية الذي خاص منسوخ بدليل إباحته الذي هو عام وكل دليلين نسخ أحدهما الآخر فالحكم للناسخ منهما، ينتج من الشكل الأول : الحكم هنا لدليل الإباحة العام؛ لأنه هو الناسخ . فيقول السائل : هذا الدليل منقوض؛ لأن الناسخ فيه أعم المنسوخ، وذلك ينافي مذهبك ؛ لأنه لا يجوز فيه نسخ خاص بعام . من فقد نقض عليه دليله بتخلف الحكم عنه بمقتضى مذهب المعلل ؛ لأن ثبوت حكمه معه مناف لمذهب المعلل .
With chaste women and prostitutes, males and females, and it is known that the madhhab of al-Shafi'i and Malik is that the specific is not abrogated by the general; because according to them, the specific overrides the general and specifies its generality, whether its revelation occurred after it or before, as established in their usul. So the mu'allil, like al-Shafi'i for example, says: The evidence for the prohibition of marrying a prostitute, which is specific, is abrogated by the evidence for its permissibility, which is general; and every pair of evidences where one abrogates the other, the ruling is according to the abrogating one; it yields from the first figure: the ruling here is according to the general evidence of permissibility, because it is the abrogator. So the questioner says: This argument is invalid (manqud), because the abrogator here is more general than the abrogated, and that contradicts your madhhab, because according to you, a specific cannot be abrogated by a general. So he refuted his argument by the non-existence of the ruling in accordance with the mu'allil's madhhab; because the existence of his ruling with it contradicts the mu'allil's madhhab.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage illustrates a naqdh (objection) in a dialectical exchange. The author presents a scenario where the mu'allil (the one providing the proof) is like al-Shafi'i. The mu'allil argues that the evidence for the prohibition of marrying a prostitute (a specific ruling) is abrogated by the evidence for its permissibility (a general ruling). He constructs a categorical syllogism in the first figure: the major premise is that whenever two evidences mutually abrogate, the ruling is according to the abrogator; the minor premise is that this is such a pair; the conclusion is that the ruling is according to the general evidence (the abrogator). The sa'il (questioner) then objects (naqdh) that the abrogator (general) is more general than the abrogated (specific), which contradicts the mu'allil's own madhhab (Shafi'i and Malik) that a specific ruling cannot be abrogated by a general one. Thus, the objection shows that the mu'allil's argument conflicts with his own principles, effectively refuting the argument. This example demonstrates a common dialectical move: pointing out that an opponent's argument leads to a conclusion that undermines his own doctrinal commitments.

Key Terms
النقض (al-naqdh)
المعلل (al-mu'allil)
السائل (al-sa'il)
الخاص (al-khas)
العام (al-'amm)

Key Takeaway
A naqdh refutes an argument by showing it conflicts with the opponent's own accepted principles, not by attacking its logical form.
