Four Answers to Naqdh in Dialectic
Author classifies four types of answers to a naqdh and gives an example of the third type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of answers to a naqdh (objection) in jadal and explain the logical move in the third type using the basmalah example.

Core Idea
Four strategic moves to counter a naqdh: deny the proof's completeness, deny the conclusion's absence, deny the proof entails impossibility, or deny the impossibility itself.

In dialectical disputation (jadal), when an objection (naqdh) is raised against an argument, the respondent must have systematic strategies to reply. This passage from a classical text on the art of disputation presents four distinct types of answers, each targeting a different aspect of the objection. Understanding these moves is essential for defending a position in usul al-fiqh and kalam discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four answer types
Read the Arabic carefully and list the four types: (1) man' wujud tamam al-dalil (denying complete proof), (2) man' takhalluf al-madlul (denying absence of implication), (3) man' istilzam al-muhal (denying necessitation of the impossible), (4) man' kawnihi muhalan (denying it is impossible).
The first two will be explained later with examples.
2
Understand the logical structure of the third type
The third type is illustrated with an example: The mu'allil argues that a certain matter should begin with the basmalah. He uses a categorical syllogism in the first figure: 'This matter is a matter of importance; every matter of importance should begin with the basmalah; therefore, this matter should begin with the basmalah.' The objector claims this argument leads to an impossibility. The answer is to deny that the proof necessitates that impossibility.
The syllogism is valid; the objection must target the consequences, not the form.
3
Contrast the four types
The first two types attack the proof or its conclusion directly, while the third and fourth address objections based on impossible consequences. Know when each is appropriate in a debate.
In practice, the choice depends on the nature of the naqdh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأجوبة عن النقض اعلم أنه لا يخفى على من فهم حقيقة النقض أن الجواب عنه في هذا الفن إما : ۱ - بمنع وجود تمام دليل الدعوى فيقول : دليلها غير موجود على التمام، ولو كان موجودًا كذلك لما تخلف مدلوله عنه ٢ - الثاني أن يمنع تخلف المدلول فيقول : المدلول موجود غير متخلف . - وإما بمنع استلزامه ](١) المحال . - أو [بمنع ](٢) كونه محالاً . أما الجوابان الأولان فسيأتي إيضاحهما بأمثلتهما إن شاء الله - تعالى - إيضاحا شافيا في الكلام على تطبيق النقض في القادح الأصولي المعروف بالنقض وأما الجواب عن النقض باستلزام الدليل المحال، الذي هو منع استلزامه المحال فمن أمثلته أن يقول المعلل مثلاً : هذا الأمر الذي شرعت فيه ينبغي ابتداؤه بالبسملة، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه أمر ذو بال وكل أمر ذي بال ينبغي ابتداؤه بالبسملة، ينتج من الشكل الأول : هذا الأمر ينبغي ابتداؤه بالبسملة .
Chapter on the answers to the objection. Know that it is not hidden from one who understands the reality of the objection that the answer to it in this art is either: 1 - By denying the existence of the complete proof of the claim, saying: its proof does not exist completely, and if it existed thus, its implication would not be absent from it. 2 - The second is to deny the absence of the implication, saying: the implication exists, not absent. And either by denying its necessitation of the impossible, or by denying that it is impossible. As for the first two answers, their clarification with their examples will come later, God willing, with a sufficient explanation, in the discussion on applying the objection in the usuli defect known as naqdh. As for the answer to the objection by the proof's necessitation of the impossible, which is the denial of its necessitation of the impossible, one of its examples is that the mu'allil says, for example: This matter which I have undertaken should begin with the basmalah, then he provides the proof for that, saying: because it is a matter of importance, and every matter of importance should begin with the basmalah, it yields from the first figure: this matter should begin with the basmalah.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on the types of answers (ajwiba) to a naqdh (objection) in the art of disputation. The author states that for someone who understands the nature of naqdh, the answers are of four types. The first is to deny the existence of the complete proof (man' wujud tamam dalil al-da'wa). That is, the objector claims that the proof is not fully present; if it were, the conclusion would follow, but since it is not, the argument fails. The second is to deny the absence of the implication (man' takhalluf al-madlul). That is, the objector claims that the intended conclusion (al-madlul) actually exists and is not absent; the argument is still valid. The third is to deny that the proof necessitates an impossibility (man' istilzam al-muhal). The fourth is to deny that the thing in question is impossible (man' kawnihi muhalan). The author notes that the first two answers will be elaborated later with examples in another context. He then provides an example for the third type. The example: The mu'allil argues that a certain matter (like beginning a task) should be initiated with the basmalah (bismillah). He constructs a syllogism: the matter is a matter of importance (dhu bal); every matter of importance should begin with the basmalah; therefore, this matter should begin with the basmalah. This syllogism is in the first figure. The objection would be that this argument leads to an impossibility (perhaps because it implies an infinite regress or something absurd). The answer (third type) is for the mu'allil to deny that his argument necessitates such an impossibility. Thus, the classification provides systematic strategies for countering objections.

Key Terms
منع وجود تمام الدليل
منع تخلف المدلول
منع استلزام المحال
منع كونه محالاً

There are four distinct strategies to answer a naqdh: denying the completeness of the proof, the absence of the conclusion, the necessitation of impossibility, or the impossibility itself.

Classifying Responses to a Dialectical Objection: The Four Man' Strategies
Classification of four answers to a naqd objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four types of responses to a naqd objection in dialectical argumentation.

Core Idea
Four defensive moves: deny the proof, deny the conclusion, deny the impossibility, deny the absurdity.

In the art of jadal, when one's argument is attacked through a naqd (objection), the respondent has a limited set of legitimate replies. This chapter from the Adab al-Bahth tradition classifies exactly four answers. Understanding this classification equips you to respond systematically to objections in theological and juristic debates.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of a naqd objection
A naqd is a critical objection that claims either the proof is incomplete, the conclusion is missing, the argument implies an impossibility, or the conclusion itself is impossible.
The four answer types correspond to these four possible flaws.
2
Identify the first answer: man' wujud tamam al-dalil
The respondent denies that the proof is complete. He asserts that the evidence is fully present; if it were, the conclusion would follow.
3
Identify the second answer: man' takhalluf al-madlul
The respondent denies that the conclusion is absent. He insists the intended meaning holds despite the objection.
4
Identify the third answer: man' istilzam al-muhal
The respondent denies that the proof implies an impossibility. He rejects the objector's claim that the argument leads to absurdity.
5
Identify the fourth answer: man' kawnihi muhalan
The respondent denies that the thing claimed to be impossible is actually impossible. He argues that what the objector calls impossible is not inherently so.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأجوبة عن النقض
اعلم أنه لا يخفى على من فهم حقيقة النقض أن الجواب عنه في هذا الفن إما :
۱ - بمنع وجود تمام دليل الدعوى فيقول : دليلها غير موجود على التمام، ولو كان موجودًا كذلك لما تخلف مدلوله عنه
۲ - الثاني أن يمنع تخلف المدلول فيقول : المدلول موجود غير متخلف .
- وإما بمنع استلزامه المحال .
- أو بمنع كونه محالاً .
أما الجوابان الأولان فسيأتي إيضاحهما بأمثلتهما إن شاء الله تعالى إيضاحا شافيا في الكلام على تطبيق النقض في القادح الأصولي المعروف بالنقض
Chapter on responses to the objection. Know that it is not hidden from one who understands the reality of the objection that the answer to it in this art is either: 1 - by preventing the existence of the complete proof of the claim, saying: its proof does not exist completely, and if it existed completely, its conclusion would not be absent from it. 2 - The second is to prevent the absence of the conclusion, saying: the conclusion exists and is not absent. - Or by preventing its implying the impossible. - Or by preventing its being impossible. As for the first two answers, their clarification with examples will come later, God willing, with a sufficient clarification in the discussion on applying the objection in the fundamental defect known as 'naqd'.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage classifies the four types of answers (ajwiba) that a respondent (mu'allil) can give when faced with a logical objection (naqd) in the art of jadal. The author states that understanding the reality of naqd makes these answers clear. The first answer is to deny that the proof (dalil) of the opponent's claim is complete (man' wujud tamam dalil). The respondent says the evidence is not fully present, and if it were, the conclusion would not be absent. The second answer is to deny that the conclusion (madlul) is absent, asserting that the intended meaning is actually present despite the objection. The third is to deny that the proof implies an impossibility (man' istilzam al-muhal), i.e., the objection claims the argument leads to an absurdity, but the respondent rejects that implication. The fourth is to deny that the thing claimed to be impossible is indeed impossible (man' kawnihi muhalan). The author notes that examples for the first two types will be provided later in the discussion on the fundamental defect (qadih usuli) known as naqd.

محال

Key Takeaway
The four man' strategies form a complete toolkit for responding to a naqd objection: deny the proof, deny the conclusion, deny the implication, or deny the absurdity.

Denying Implication of Impossibility: Basmala and Infinite Regress
Example of answering naqd by denying implication of impossibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a dialectical exchange where the respondent denies that his proof implies an impossibility (tasalsul) by excluding the key instance (basmala) from the general rule.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is avoided by excluding the basmala from its own rule.

In this lesson, we examine a classic dialectical maneuver: when an objection claims that your proof leads to an impossibility (infinite regress), you can counter by showing that the impossibility does not actually follow. The example involves the basmala – whether it must itself be preceded by another basmala. We will see how the respondent denies the implication by excluding the basmala from the general rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mu'allil's proof
The mu'allil (arguer) gives a categorical syllogism in the first figure: 'This matter is a matter of significance, and every matter of significance should be started with the basmala; therefore this matter should be started with the basmala.' Recognize the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion.
The syllogism is in the form Barbara (AAA-1).
2
Understand the sa'il's objection
The sa'il (objector) points out that the same reasoning applies to the basmala itself: it is significant, so it would need to be preceded by another basmala, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). This regress is considered impossible (muhal).
The objection is a naqd based on implying an impossibility (istilzam al-muhal).
3
Analyze the mu'allil's answer
The mu'allil denies that the proof implies the infinite regress. He argues that the basmala itself is excluded (mustathna) from the general rule 'every matter of significance should begin with the basmala.' The rule is not absolutely universal; it does not apply to itself.
This is the third type of answer to a naqd: man' istilzam al-muhal (denying the implication of impossibility).
4
Evaluate the success of the answer
By excluding the basmala, the infinite regress stops at the first basmala. The mu'allil shows that the objection does not actually follow from his proof. The objector would need to show that the basmala must be included; otherwise, the answer stands.
This highlights the importance of clarifying the scope of a universal claim in dialectical contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن النقض باستلزام الدليل المحال، الذي هو منع استلزامه المحال فمن أمثلته أن يقول المعلل مثلاً : هذا الأمر الذي شرعت فيه ينبغي ابتداؤه بالبسملة، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه أمر ذو بال وكل أمر ذي بال ينبغي ابتداؤه بالبسملة، ينتج من الشكل الأول : هذا الأمر ينبغي ابتداؤه بالبسملة .
فيقول السائل : هذا الدليل منقوض باستلزامه المحال وهو التسلل ؛ لأن نفس البسملة أمر ذو بال ينبغي أن يصدر بالبسملة، وهذه البسملة أيضًا أمر ذو بال ينبغي تصديره بالبسملة، وهكذا إلى غير نهاية، وهو تسلسل ممنوع، واستلزام الدليل له فيجب نقض له فيجب المعلل عن هذا النقض بمنع استلزام الدليل للمحال، ويستند في ذلك المنع إلى أن البسملة نفسها مستثناة من حكم الدليل، فيقول : أمنع استلزام هذا الدليل المحال ؛ لأن محل ذلك فيما لو كانت البسملة نفسها مع كونها من الأمور ذوات البال داخلة في عموم (كل أمر ذي بال، لكنها غير داخلة فيه، بل مستثناة منه .
As for the answer to the objection regarding the proof's implying the impossible, which is preventing its implying the impossible, one of its examples is that the mu'allil says, for instance: This matter I have begun should be started with the basmala. Then he presents the proof for that, saying: Because it is a matter of significance, and every matter of significance should be started with the basmala. This yields in the first figure: This matter should be started with the basmala. The questioner says: This proof is nullified because it implies the impossible, which is infinite regress; because the basmala itself is a matter of significance and should be begun with the basmala, and that basmala is also a matter of significance and should be begun with the basmala, and so on ad infinitum. This is a forbidden regress, and the implication of the proof necessitates its nullification. So the mu'allil responds to this objection by preventing the proof's implication of the impossible, basing that denial on the fact that the basmala itself is excluded from the ruling of the proof. He says: I deny that this proof implies the impossible; because the situation where it would apply is if the basmala itself, despite being a matter of significance, were included in the generality of 'every matter of significance', but it is not included in it; rather it is excluded from it.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage provides an example of the third type of answer to a naqd: denying that the argument implies an impossibility (man' istilzam al-muhal). The mu'allil (arguer) claims that a certain matter (such as a book or speech) should begin with the basmala, and he gives a categorical syllogism in the first figure: 'This matter is a matter of significance, and every matter of significance should begin with the basmala; therefore this matter should begin with the basmala.' The objector (sa'il) then points out that this proof leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul): if everything significant must start with basmala, then the basmala itself is significant and must also start with basmala, leading to an endless chain. The mu'allil responds by denying that the proof implies such a regress. He argues that the basmala itself is excluded (mustathna) from the general rule. The general rule 'every matter of significance should begin with the basmala' is not universal; it does not apply to the basmala itself. Therefore, there is no infinite regress, and the objection fails. This illustrates how the respondent can block the objection by showing that the claimed impossibility does not actually follow from his argument.

تسلسل
مستثناة
الشكل الأول
منع استلزام المحال

Key Takeaway
The third type of answer to a naqd is to deny that the proof implies the claimed impossibility, often by showing that the problematic instance is excluded from the rule.

Denying Impossibility: Substance, Accident, and Simultaneous Cycle
Example of answering by denying impossibility: substance/accident and simultaneous cycle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the dialectical move of denying impossibility using the distinction between simultaneous (dawr ma'i) and temporal (dawr sabqi) cycles.

Core Idea
Not every cycle is impossible – simultaneous cycles can be valid in metaphysical contexts.

In dialectical argument, an opponent may try to refute your proof by claiming it leads to an impossible cycle. This lesson walks through a classic response: showing that the cycle is only simultaneous, not temporal. The example of substance and accident from Islamic philosophy demonstrates how this distinction defuses the objection, while preserving the possibility of truth in the original claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mu'allil's claim and argument
Find the conditional reasoning: if each of two things depends on the other for perception, then perceiving either is impossible. The mu'allil concludes this leads to an impossible cycle.
The mu'allil is the one who initially proposes the proof.
2
Recognize the objector's counterexample
The objector brings up substance (jawhar) and accident ('arad), which also depend on each other for perception, yet both are perceived together. This contradicts the mu'allil's claim that such a situation is impossible.
Substance and accident are a standard example in Islamic philosophy for mutual dependency.
3
Understand the cycle distinction
The objector argues that the cycle implied by the mu'allil's proof is simultaneous (dawr ma'i), not temporal (dawr sabqi). A temporal cycle is impossible, but a simultaneous one is not necessarily impossible.
Simultaneous cycle: A and B depend on each other at the same time. Temporal cycle: A depends on B which depends on A in a temporal sequence.
4
Note the mu'allil's possible response
After the objection, the mu'allil may still prove his original claim with a different argument. This shows that refuting one proof does not necessarily refute the overall position.
The term 'ifham' (refutation) is used here; it means silencing the opponent by presenting a stronger proof.
5
Assess the dialectical outcome
The objector succeeds in showing the proof is not universally valid, but the mu'allil can still prevail by providing another proof. The exchange illustrates the constructive nature of jadal: it refines arguments without destroying truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال منع كونه محالاً أن يقول المعلل: هذان الأمران يتوقف إدراك كل واحد منهما على إدراك الآخر ، وكل شيئين توقف إدراك كل واحد منهما على إدراك الآخر استحال إدراك أي واحد منهما ؛ لاستلزام ذلك للدور المحال .
فيقول السائل : دليلك هذا منقوض بالجوهر والعرض؛ فإنهما أمران يتوقف إدراك كل واحد منهما على إدراك الآخر مع إمكان إدراكهما معا كما هو مشاهد والدور الذي استلزمه دليلك ليس محالاً؛ لأنه دور مَعِيٌّ، والمحال إنما هو الدور السبقي.
وللمعلل بعد ورود النقض على دليله أن يثبت مُدعاه بدليل آخر،
فيكون ذلك إفحامًا من وجه وإظهارا للصواب من وجه .
An example of preventing its being impossible is that the mu'allil says: These two things, the perception of each one is dependent on the perception of the other. And every two things such that the perception of each one depends on the perception of the other, it is impossible to perceive any one of them, because that implies the impossible cycle. The questioner says: This proof of yours is nullified by substance and accident; for they are two things such that the perception of each one depends on the perception of the other, yet it is possible to perceive them together, as is observed. And the cycle implied by your proof is not impossible, because it is a simultaneous cycle (dawr ma'i); the impossible is only the temporal cycle (dawr sabqi). And after the objection against his proof, the mu'allil can establish his claim with another proof, and that would be a refutation from one perspective and a showing of the truth from another perspective.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage gives an example of the fourth type of answer: denying that something is impossible (man' kawnihi muhalan). The mu'allil argues that if two things (like two concepts) are such that perceiving each depends on perceiving the other, then neither can be perceived, because it would lead to an impossible cycle (dawr muhal). The objector (sail) counters with the example of substance (jawhar) and accident ('arad). In Islamic philosophy, substance and accident are mutually dependent: accident is known through substance, and substance is known through its accidents. Yet it is possible to perceive both together, as is evident. The cycle involved is a simultaneous (ma'i) cycle, not a temporal (sabqi) cycle. A temporal cycle (A depends on B which depends on A in time) is impossible, but a simultaneous cycle (where A and B coexist and depend on each other at the same time) is not impossible. Therefore, the mu'allil's proof does not lead to an impossibility. The author adds that after the objection, the mu'allil can respond by providing another proof for his original claim, thereby achieving both refutation (ifham) and demonstration of truth.

دور معي
دور سبقي
جوهر

إفحام

Key Takeaway
In dialectical argument, a cycle can be excused as simultaneous (dawr ma'i) rather than temporal, preserving the validity of the argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Two Examples of Naqd: Tasalsul and Dawr
Author illustrates two types of naqd using examples of infinite regress and circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between the two types of naqd (tasalsul and dawr) in a jadal exchange and analyze the defender's response.

Core Idea
A proof is refuted not only by direct counterexamples, but by showing it implies an impossibility like infinite regress or vicious circularity.

In dialectical debate, a refutation (naqd) can target a proof by demonstrating that it leads to an impossible consequence. This lesson examines two classic forms of such refutation: infinite regress (tasalsul) and circular reasoning (dawr). The passage works through concrete examples from the Islamic tradition, showing how the defender (mu'allil) can respond by preventing the implication or by distinguishing between vicious and non-vicious forms of circularity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's objection
Locate where the sa'il (questioner) asserts that the proof is refuted (manqud). In the first example, he claims the proof implies an infinite regress because the basmala itself would require another basmala. In the second, he objects that the defender's argument about mutual dependence implies a circle that is actually possible (simultaneous), not impossible.
The sa'il always frames his objection as 'your proof is refuted because it implies X impossible'.
2
Classify the type of naqd
Determine whether the objection relies on infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr). Tasalsul involves an endless chain; dawr involves a loop. In the first example it is tasalsul; in the second it is dawr.
Note that the passage distinguishes between sequential (sabqi) and simultaneous (ma'i) circles, which is a nuance in later dialectic.
3
Analyze the defender's response (mu'allil)
The mu'allil can respond by preventing (man') the implication. In the first example, he claims the basmala is excepted from the general rule, so no regress follows. In the second, the opponent prevents the impossibility by showing the circle is simultaneous and therefore not vicious.
After a naqd, the defender may also offer a new proof to strengthen his position.
4
Evaluate the outcome
The passage states that after a naqd, the defender can still 'silence' (ifham) by providing a different proof. This shows that a single refutation does not necessarily defeat the overall claim if the defender can re-establish it with fresh evidence.
In jadal, the defender is not limited to the original proof; he can pivot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقول السائل : هذا الدليل منقوض باستلزامه المحال وهو التسلل ؛ لأن نفس البسملة أمر ذو بال ينبغي أن يصدر بالبسملة، وهذه البسملة أيضًا أمر ذو بال ينبغي تصديره بالبسملة، وهكذا إلى غير نهاية، وهو تسلسل ممنوع، واستلزام الدليل له فيجب نقض له فيجب المعلل عن هذا النقض بمنع استلزام الدليل للمحال، ويستند في ذلك المنع إلى أن البسملة نفسها مستثناة من حكم الدليل، فيقول : أمنع استلزام هذا الدليل المحال ؛ لأن محل ذلك فيما لو كانت البسملة نفسها مع كونها من الأمور ذوات البال داخلة في عموم (كل أمر ذي بال، لكنها غير داخلة فيه، بل مستثناة منه . ومثال منع كونه محالاً أن يقول المعلل: هذان الأمران يتوقف إدراك كل واحد منهما على إدراك الآخر ، وكل شيئين توقف إدراك كل واحد منهما على إدراك الآخر استحال إدراك أي واحد منهما ؛ لاستلزام ذلك للدور المحال . فيقول السائل : دليلك هذا منقوض بالجوهر والعرض؛ فإنهما أمران يتوقف إدراك كل واحد منهما على إدراك الآخر مع إمكان إدراكهما معا كما هو مشاهد والدور الذي استلزمه دليلك ليس محالاً؛ لأنه دور مَعِيٌّ، والمحال إنما هو الدور السبقي. وللمعلل بعد ورود النقض على دليله أن يثبت مُدعاه بدليل آخر، فيكون ذلك إفحامًا من وجه وإظهارا للصواب من وجه .
The opponent says: This proof is refuted by its implication of the impossible, which is infinite regress; because the basmala itself is a matter of importance that should be begun with the basmala, and this basmala also is a matter of importance that should be begun with the basmala, and so on without end, and that is a prohibited infinite regress, and the proof's implication of it. So it must be refuted, so the defender responds to this refutation by preventing the proof's implication of the impossible, and he relies in that prevention on the fact that the basmala itself is excepted from the ruling of the proof, so he says: I prevent the implication of this proof of the impossible; because the place for that would be if the basmala itself, being among matters of importance, were included in the generality of 'every matter of importance', but it is not included in it, rather it is excepted from it. And an example of preventing it being impossible is that the defender says: These two matters, the perception of each one depends on the perception of the other, and for any two things the perception of each depends on the perception of the other, perception of any one of them is impossible, because that implies the impossible circle. So the opponent says: This proof of yours is refuted by substance and accident; for they are two matters the perception of each depends on the perception of the other, while it is possible to perceive them together as is observed, and the circle implied by your proof is not impossible, because it is a simultaneous circle, and the impossible is only the sequential circle. And after the refutation has been brought against his proof, the defender can prove his claim with another proof, and that is a silencing from one perspective and a showing of the truth from another perspective.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents two examples of a refutation (naqd) in dialectical debate. The first example concerns the basmala: an opponent argues that a proof (presumably that every important matter should begin with basmala) leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul) because the basmala itself is an important matter that would require another basmala, ad infinitum. The defender (mu'allil) responds by preventing (man') the implication: he claims that the basmala is excluded from the generality of the rule 'every matter of importance', so the regress does not occur. This is a common tactic: showing that the counterexample is not covered by the original claim. The second example involves circularity (dawr). The defender sets up a case where two things mutually depend on each other for perception, leading to an impossible circle (dawr muhal). The opponent then objects using the example of substance and accident: we perceive them together even though each perception depends on the other, so the circle is possible if it is simultaneous (dawr ma'i), not sequential (dawr sabqi). The passage concludes that after a naqd, the defender can still win by providing a new proof, achieving 'ifham' (silencing) from one angle and demonstrating truth from another. The key logical move is distinguishing between vicious and non-vicious circles, a nuance in dialectical argument.

Key Terms
نقض
تسلسل
دور
معلل
سائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example, why does the opponent claim the proof is refuted?
A) Because the basmala is not a matter of importance.
B) Because the proof implies an infinite regress.
C) Because the basmala contradicts itself.
D) Because the proof is based on circular reasoning.

Key Takeaway
A proof can be refuted by showing it implies an impossibility such as infinite regress (tasalsul) or a vicious circle, but the defender may respond by preventing the implication or distinguishing between harmless and harmful forms of circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the claim: 'Every revealed law (shar') has a clear scriptural basis. The Qur'an itself is a revealed law, so it requires a clear scriptural basis, leading to an infinite regress.' Identify the type of naqd (tasalsul) and formulate a response using the technique of 'man' (prevention) by excepting the Qur'an from the generality of the claim, similar to the basmala example.
?
In a debate about the nature of knowledge, a defender claims: 'Knowledge of the soul depends on knowledge of the body, and knowledge of the body depends on knowledge of the soul, so neither can be known.' An opponent objects that this is a simultaneous circle, as seen in the mutual dependence of substance and accident (which are known together). Evaluate whether this objection succeeds by applying the distinction between sequential and simultaneous circles.

Definition and Three Types of Mu'arada (Counter-Argument)
Author defines mu'arada and its three types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate the three types of mu'arada and explain how each invalidates the opponent's claim.

Core Idea
Mu'arada defeats a claim by proving its opposite, an equivalent, or a more specific negation.

In dialectical debate (jadal), an opponent can object in two main ways: man' (denial without proof) and mu'arada (counter-argument with proof). This lesson unpacks the precise definition of mu'arada and its three sub-types: proving the exact opposite, something equivalent, or a more specific negation. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for both constructing and responding to counter-arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main objection types
The passage states that all objections reduce to man' (prevention/denial) and mu'arada (counter-argument). Note that some scholars subsume mu'arada under man'.
The author cites a line from Maraqi al-Su'ud mentioning this reduction.
2
Extract the genus of mu'arada
Mu'arada is defined as 'the opponent's establishing a proof' (iqamat al-khasm al-dalil). This is the genus: it is a proof, not mere assertion.
The proof must be valid (dafil muntij).
3
Identify the differentia: three products of the proof
The proof must produce one of three things: the opposite (naqid) of the claim, something equal to that opposite (musawi al-naqid), or something more specific than the opposite (akhas min al-naqid). These are the differentiae that complete the definition.
The author lists them explicitly: 'أو المنتج ما يساوي نقيضها أو ما هو أخص من نقيضها'.
4
Understand why each type invalidates the claim
The invalidity follows from logical impossibilities: two opposites cannot coexist, a thing and its equivalent opposite cannot coexist, and a thing and a more specific negation cannot coexist (since the more specific entails the negation). The author references a previous explanation.
This shows the foundation of mu'arada in the law of non-contradiction.
5
Apply to a concrete example
The passage begins an example: if a defender claims 'the world is eternal' (false philosophers' view), a mu'arada would be to prove 'the world is temporally originated' (its opposite). Think of what would constitute an equivalent or more specific negation.
The example is cut off, but we can complete it logically.

A chapter on counter-argument, the third of the three methods already discussed. Know first that all methods of objection reduce to two things: prevention and counter-argument. And some reduce them all to one thing, which is prevention, as the author of Maraqi al-Su'ud said: 'Objection returns to counter-argument and prevention together, or the latter.' And know that counter-argument in terminology is the opponent's establishing a proof that produces the opposite of the claim that his opponent has inferred and established with his proof, or that produces what is equal to its opposite, or what is more specific than its opposite; because his establishing a proof that produces one of the three things necessitates the invalidation of his opponent's claim; for if its opposite or the equal of its opposite or the more specific than its opposite is established by the counter-proof, then its invalidity is verified; because of the impossibility of the conjunction of two opposites, and the impossibility of the conjunction of a thing and the equal of its opposite, and the impossibility of the conjunction of a thing and the more specific than its opposite, as previously clarified. Commentary
This passage defines al-mu'arada (counter-argument), the third method of objection in dialectic. The author first states that all objections reduce to two types: al-man' (prevention/denial) and al-mu'arada, though some reduce all to one type, prevention. He cites a line from the poem Maraqi al-Su'ud. Then he gives the technical definition: al-mu'arada is when the opponent (khasm) brings a proof that produces the opposite (naqid) of the opponent's original claim, or something equal to that opposite, or something more specific (akhas) than that opposite. The effect is to invalidate the original claim because it is impossible for a claim and its opposite (or its equal or a more specific negation) to be true together. The passage then begins an example: the defender who holds the false view that the world is eternal would set up a proof for that, but the example is cut off. The key logical principle here is that if you can prove the opposite (or an equivalent or a more specific opposite) of the opponent's claim, then his claim must be false. This is a standard method in jadal.

Mu'aradah: The Three Forms of Counterobjection
Author defines mu'aradah and illustrates with three categorical syllogism examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of mu'aradah (contradictory, equal, more specific) in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Counterobjection isn't just denying – it's proving the opposite, or something equal or more specific.

In dialectical debate, objecting to an opponent's proof can take two forms: outright denial (mana') or counterobjection (mu'aradah). This lesson unpacks the latter, where you adduce your own proof that yields not just the contradictory of the opponent's claim, but also something logically equal to it or even more specific. Mastering these distinctions sharpens your ability to dismantle false arguments in Islamic theological and jurisprudential debates.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the trichotomy of mu'aradah
Mu'aradah is defined as producing one of three: the contradictory (naqiid), what equals the contradictory (musawi), or what is more specific than the contradictory (akhass). Understand why each works: if you prove the contradictory, the opponent's claim collapses; if you prove something equal, it's as good as the contradictory; if you prove something more specific, it entails the contradictory.
The text emphasizes that these three exhaust the logical relationships that falsify a claim.
2
Analyze first example – direct contradictory
The philosopher claims 'The world is eternal' using a syllogism. The objector counters with a syllogism concluding 'The world is not eternal', which is the exact contradictory. Trace the logical structure: major premise 'Every changing thing is not eternal', minor 'The world changes', conclusion 'The world is not eternal'.
This is the most straightforward form of mu'aradah.
3
Analyze second example – equal to contradictory
In the same scenario, the objector proves 'The world is temporally originated (hadith)'. Since 'temporally originated' is logically equivalent to 'not eternal', this conclusion, though not identical, equals the contradictory. Recognize the subtle shift: the objector uses 'hadith' which is co-extensive with 'not eternal'.
The musawi relationship means both statements have the same truth value.
4
Analyze third example – more specific than contradictory
The Mu'tazilite claims 'Seeing Allah is impossible'. The objector cites revelation that it will occur. The proposition 'It will occur' is more specific than 'It is not impossible' (the contradictory). Proving a specific instance of seeing falsifies the universal claim of impossibility. Understand the logical asymmetry: the specific entails the contradictory but not vice versa.
This form is especially useful when direct contradictory proof is unavailable.
5
Summarize the logical relationships
For each type, spell out the logical relation: naqiid = contradiction (cannot both be true/false); musawi = mutual implication; akhass = one-way implication (specific implies general contradictory). Internalize why each nullifies the opponent's claim.
These relationships are grounded in basic logical principles like non-contradiction.

القيامة
فيقول السائل المعارض : رؤية الله - تعالى - بالأبصار أخبر الله في كتابه ورسوله في الأحاديث الصحيحة بأنها واقعة
And it is a chapter on counterobjection (mu'aradah), the third of the three methods previously discussed. Know firstly that all methods of objection reduce to two things: denial (al-mana') and counterobjection (al-mu'aradah). Some scholars reduce them all to one thing, which is denial, as the author of Maraqi al-Sa'ud said: 'Objection reduces to counterobjection and denial together, or the latter alone.' And know that counterobjection in terminology is the opponent's adducing a proof that produces the contradictory of the claim for which his opponent argued and which he established by his proof, or a proof that produces what is equal to its contradictory or what is more specific than its contradictory. Because his adducing a proof that produces one of these three things necessitates voiding his opponent's claim. For if its contradictory, or something equal to its contradictory, or something more specific than its contradictory is established by the counterevidence, then its falsehood is realized, due to the impossibility of the coexistence of two contradictories, and the impossibility of the coexistence of a thing and something equal to its contradictory, and the impossibility of the coexistence of a thing and something more specific than its contradictory, as previously explained. An example of counterobjection by producing the contradictory is that the reasoner who believes the false doctrine of the philosophers regarding the eternity of the world says: 'The world is eternal.' Then he adduces, in his false opinion, a proof for his claim, saying: 'Because it is an effect of the eternal. Everything that is an effect of the eternal is eternal.' So this is a proof that the reasoner sets up to prove his claim; it produces, in his opinion: 'The world is eternal.' Then the objector says: 'The world is not eternal.' Then he adduces a proof for that, saying: 'Because it undergoes change through non-existence and similar kinds of change. Everything that is like that is not eternal.' This produces: 'It — i.e., the world — is not eternal.' This result is precisely the contradictory of the claim for which the original arguer set up his proof, which is his statement: 'The world is eternal,' because the contradictory of 'eternal' is 'not eternal.' An example of it producing something equal to the contradictory of the claim for which the reasoner argued is that one says in this last example: 'The world undergoes change through non-existence and similar kinds of change. Everything that is like that is temporally originated (hadith).' This produces: 'The world is temporally originated.' The temporally originated is equal to the contradictory of 'eternal' because the contradictory of 'eternal' is 'not eternal,' and 'temporally originated' is equal to 'not eternal,' as is clear. An example of it producing what is more specific than the contradictory of the claim for which the reasoner argued is that the Mu'tazilite reasoner who believes the impossibility of seeing Allah with eyes on the Day of Judgment says: 'Seeing Allah with eyes necessitates many kinds of similarity to creation. Everything that is like that is impossible with respect to Him — exalted is He.' So this false proof of his produces a false result, which is his statement: 'Seeing Allah — exalted is He — with eyes is impossible,' meaning it is not rationally possible. Then the objector says in counterobjection: 'Seeing Allah — exalted is He — with eyes: Allah has informed in His Book and His Messenger in the authentic hadiths that it will occur.'
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage defines 'mu'aradah' (counterobjection) and illustrates it with three examples, each demonstrating a different logical relationship to the contradictory of the opponent's claim. The passage begins by stating that all objections fall into two categories: denial (al-mana') and counterobjection (al-mu'aradah), though some reduce them all to denial. It then defines mu'aradah as the opponent adducing a proof that yields either the direct contradictory (naqiid) of the opponent's claim, or something equal (musawi) to that contradictory, or something more specific (akhass) than that contradictory. The rationale is that if any of these three is established, the opponent's claim is falsified due to the impossibility of coexisting contradictories, or of a thing and its equal's contradictory, or of a thing and what is more specific than its contradictory. The first example involves the philosophers' claim that the world is eternal. The reasoner argues: 'The world is an effect of the eternal; every effect of the eternal is eternal; therefore the world is eternal.' The objector counters: 'The world undergoes change; everything that undergoes change is not eternal; therefore the world is not eternal.' This result is the exact contradictory of the original claim. The second example uses the same scenario but yields a different conclusion: 'The world is temporally originated (hadith).' The objector explains that 'temporally originated' is equal to 'not eternal,' thus it is equal to the contradictory. The third example is from theology: A Mu'tazilite argues that seeing Allah on the Day of Judgment is impossible because it would entail similarity to creation; the objector counters by citing divine revelation that it will occur. This example is incomplete in the text, but it illustrates producing something more specific than the contradictory (i.e., a specific report of occurrence is more specific than the general claim of impossibility).

Mu'arada: Producing Contradictory, Equivalent, or More Specific Counter-Claims
Author illustrates mu'arada by showing three types of outcomes from opponent's counter-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish three types of mu'arada outcomes (contradictory, equivalent, more specific) and identify them in dialectical examples.

Core Idea
Mu'arada turns the opponent's own reasoning to produce a counter-claim that opposes, equals, or narrows the original claim.

In dialectical debate, mu'arada is a powerful move: instead of simply denying the claim, you use the opponent's evidence to derive a conclusion that contradicts, equals, or narrows the original. This passage illustrates each type with concrete kalam arguments about the world's eternity and the possibility of seeing God. Understanding these patterns sharpens your ability to anticipate and counter opponents' arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claim and evidence
Find the original thesis (mudda'a) and the proof (dalil) the arguer provides. In the first example, the claim is 'the world is eternal' and the evidence is 'it is an effect of the eternal'.
2
Locate the opponent's counter-argument structure
The opponent uses a different set of premises to derive a conclusion that opposes the original claim. Identify the premises and the conclusion of the opponent's syllogism.
The opponent's syllogism should be categorical.
3
Classify the outcome type
Determine whether the opponent's conclusion is the exact contradictory (naqid) of the original claim, logically equivalent (musawi), or more specific (akhass). Compare the terms used.
Use the definitions: naqid is direct negation; musawi is co-extensive; akhass has narrower extension.
4
Evaluate the validity of the opponent's syllogism
Check if the opponent's argument is logically valid. If invalid, the mu'arada may fail. In the first example, the syllogism is valid (Celarent).
Valid mu'arada assumes the opponent's premises are accepted by the proponent or are evident.
5
Reflect on the strategic value of each outcome type
Why would an opponent prefer to produce an equivalent or more specific claim rather than a direct contradiction? Consider the dialectical context and the burden of proof.
An equivalent claim avoids wordplay; a more specific claim can force a concession on details.

الباطل على دعواه فيقول : لأنه أثر القديم، وكل ما هو أثر القديم فهو قديم فهذا دليل على الدعوى نصبه المعلل لإثبات دعواه، ينتج في زعمه : العالم قديم . فيقول السائل : العالم غير قديم، ثم يقيم الدليل على ذلك فيقول : لأنه متغير بالانعدام ونحوه من أنواع التغير، وكل ما هو كذلك فليس بقديم، ينتج : هو - أي العالم - ليس بقديم، وهذه النتيجة عين نقيض دعوى المستدل التي نصب عليها دليله وهي قوله: (العالم قديم؛ لأن نقيض القديم (ليس بقديم) . ومثال إنتاجه مساوي نقيض الدعوى التي استدل عليها المعلل أن يقال في هذا المثال الأخير : العالم متغير بالانعدام ونحوه من أنواع التغير، وكل ما هو كذلك فهو حادث، ينتج : العالم حادث. والحادث مساو لنقيض القديم ؛ لأن نقيض القديم ليس بقديم)، والحادث مساو لـ ( ليس بقديم) كما لا يخفى . ومثال إنتاجه ما هو أخص من نقيض الدعوى التي استدل عليها المعلل أن يقول المعلل المعتزلي المعتقد استحالة رؤية الله بالأبصار يوم القيامة : رؤية الله بالأبصار يلزمها كثير من أنواع مشابهة الخلق، وكل ما هو كذلك فهو مستحيل في حقه - تعالى فينتج له دليله هذا الباطل باطلاً، وهو قوله : رؤية الله - تعالى - بالأبصار مستحيلة، يعني لا تمكن عقلاً . فيقول السائل المعارض : رؤية الله - تعالى - بالأبصار أخبر الله في كتابه ورسوله في الأحاديث الصحيحة بأنها واقعة بالفعل، وكل ما هو كذلك فهو حق صحيح. وهذه النتيجة التي وقوع الرؤية بالفعل أخصُّ من إمكانها، الذي هو نقيض دعوى الخصم التي هي استحالتها كما قدمنا إيضاحه .
The falsehood [of his claim] upon his claim, so he says: because it is an effect of the eternal, and everything that is an effect of the eternal is eternal. This is a proof for the claim that the arguer set up to prove his claim; it produces in his claim: the world is eternal. Then the questioner says: the world is not eternal, then he establishes the proof for that and says: because it is changeable through annihilation and similar types of change, and everything that is such is not eternal; it produces: it — that is, the world — is not eternal. And this result is exactly the contradictory of the claimant's claim for which he set up his proof, namely his statement: 'The world is eternal'; because the contradictory of 'eternal' is 'not eternal'. And an example of its producing the equivalent of the contradictory of the claim for which the arguer argued is that in this last example one says: The world is changeable through annihilation and similar types of change, and everything that is such is originated; it produces: the world is originated. And 'originated' is equivalent to the contradictory of 'eternal'; because the contradictory of 'eternal' is 'not eternal', and 'originated' is equivalent to 'not eternal', as is not hidden. And an example of its producing what is more specific than the contradictory of the claim for which the arguer argued is that the Mu'tazili arguer who believes the impossibility of seeing God with eyes on the Day of Resurrection says: Seeing God with eyes entails many types of similarity to creation, and everything that is such is impossible with respect to Him — Exalted is He. So his proof produces this falsehood falsely, namely his statement: Seeing God — Exalted is He — with eyes is impossible, meaning it is not rationally possible. Then the opposing questioner says: Seeing God — Exalted is He — with eyes, God informed in His Book and His Messenger in the authentic hadiths that it occurs actually, and everything that is such is true and correct. And this result, that the occurrence of seeing is actual, is more specific than its possibility, which is the contradictory of the opponent's claim which is its impossibility, as we have previously clarified.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents three examples of mu'arada (opposition) in dialectical debate. The first example shows the opponent producing the exact contradictory of the arguer's claim. The arguer claims the world is eternal because it is an effect of the eternal. The opponent counters by arguing that the world is changeable (through annihilation etc.) and anything changeable is not eternal, thus the world is not eternal—the direct contradictory. The second example shows the opponent producing a claim that is equivalent to the contradictory. Using the same premise (the world is changeable), the opponent concludes it is originated (hadith), which is equivalent to 'not eternal' since 'eternal' (qadim) and 'originated' (hadith) are mutually exclusive and exhaustive in this context. The third example shows a more specific result. A Mu'tazili arguer claims that seeing God in the afterlife is impossible because it would imply similarity to creation. The opponent counters by citing scriptural evidence that seeing God actually occurs, concluding that it is true. This result—actual occurrence—is more specific than the mere possibility (which would be the contradictory of impossibility). The author emphasizes that the goal of mu'arada is to show that the arguer's own reasoning can be turned to produce a conclusion that opposes his claim in various ways.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the world, the opponent uses the premise 'the world is changeable' to conclude 'the world is originated'. This result is what relative to the contradictory of the original claim 'the world is eternal'?
A) Exactly the contradictory
B) More specific than the contradictory
C) Equivalent to the contradictory
D) Unrelated to the contradictory

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada can produce outcomes that are exactly contradictory, logically equivalent, or more specific, providing flexible strategies for counter-argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the outcome type (naqid, musawi, or akhass) in the following exchange: A claims 'The Qur'an is created because it is contingent'; opponent says 'The Qur'an is not created because it is speech of Allah and everything that is speech of Allah is eternal.'
?
In the debate on seeing God, the Mu'tazili claims impossibility. The opponent claims actual occurrence. Which outcome type is that? Explain.
